Gold Level criteria 
Climate Change Adaptation Benefit. The project is committed to protecting 
peatlands and forests from disturbances while supporting communities in 
adopting sustainable livelihood practices. Key efforts include implementing 
Zero Burning Agriculture Practices, implementing a Sustainable Community 
Enterprise Program, and promoting eco-friendly livelihoods. An integrated 
fire prevention and forest protection system will also be established, 
engaging communities and key stakeholders. In addition, the project will 
conduct awareness-raising campaigns and capacity-building programs to 
strengthen local knowledge and awareness in forest and peatlands 
conservation. Through these initiatives, the project aims to balance 
ecological conservation with long-term economic sustainability for local 
communities. 
Exceptional Community Benefits. The project zone of SPCP qualifies as a 
rural area where more than 50% of households live below the national 
poverty line, thereby meeting the eligibility criteria for CCB Gold Level. 
Communities will receive support to transition from environmentally 
harmful practices toward climate-smart agriculture, aquaculture, and 
sustainable non-timber forest product (NTFP) harvesting, thereby reducing 
pressure on ecosystems. The project will also create employment 
opportunities in ecosystem restoration, providing inclusive and sustainable 
alternatives to environmentally degrading activities. Together, these 
interventions enhance the community’s long-term adaptive capacity, 
strengthen socio-economic resilience, and improve well-being across all 
social groups  
Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits. SPCP meets the Gold Level biodiversity 
criteria as the project area is located within a critical peat ecosystem and is 
known to support populations of 12 critically endangered species, 14

implement multispecies population and species diversity monitoring

1 SUMMARY OF PROJECT BENEFITS 
1.1 Unique Project Benefits 
 
Outcome or impact estimated by the end of project lifetime Section reference 
1) Climate Outcome  
The project aims to conserve and restore approximately 
28,506 hectares of tropical peatlands and forests by 
preventing planned degradation, planned deforestation, 
and peatland degradation within the project area. By 
implementing conservation and restoration efforts, the 
project is expected to prevent the release of over 
37,829,938.71 metric tons of CO₂ equivalent (tCO2e) in 
greenhouse gas emissions over the crediting periods. 
3 
2) Community Outcome 
The project strives to enhance community livelihoods by 
promoting sustainable forest and peatland management 
with a focus of fostering income-generating, community-
based enterprises tied to environmental conservation. 
The project is committed to enhancing the livelihoods and 
overall well-being of communities residing within and 
surrounding the project area. The project is expected to 
benefit up to 5,350 people, with direct livelihood 
improvement of 1,700 people within 14 neighboring 
villages.  
4 
3) Biodiversity Outcome  
The project will protect and enhance biodiversity within the 
project area by preserving key habitats and reducing 
threats to the Critically Endangered Bornean Orangutan 
and other threatened wildlife. The project further aims to 
prevent habitat loss, support species survival, and 
strengthen ecosystem resilience for long-term 
conservation. 
5

2 PROJECT DETAILS 
2.1 Project Goals, Design and Long-Term Viability  
2.1.1 Summary Description of the Project (VCS, 3.2, 3.6, 3.10, 3.11, 3.13, 3.14) 
The Siluk Peatlands Conservation Project (SPCP) is dedicated to the protection and restoration of 
28,506 hectares within the concession area managed by PT ASK in Kapuas Hulu District-West 
Kalimantan Province. This project is expected to generate 37,829,938.71 tCO2e (1,304,480.65 
tCO2/year) in carbon emission reductions by preventing planned forest disturbance (deforestation and 
degradation), avoiding peatland conversion, and by conducting restoration activities.  
In 2021, the Ministry of Environment and Forestry granted PT ASK a forest utilization license through 
Decree No. SK.965/MENLHK/SETJEN/HPL.0 /10/2021. This license authorized PT ASK to conduct 
timber logging and convert forests into timber plantations in the 33,605 Ha area for an initial period of 
30 years, with the possibility of extension by a further 90 years. Following a change in ownership in 
2022, the company made a strategic decision to abort the forest utilization plan and instead focus on 
forest and peatland conservation.  
In addition to mitigating greenhouse gas emissions, the project contributes significantly to biodiversity 
conservation by protecting wildlife habitats. Key species targeted for conservation by the project include 
the Bornean Orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus pygmaeus), Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis larvatus), Tricolor 
Langur (Presbytis chrysomelas ssp. cruciger), Hairy-nosed Otter (Lutra sumatrana), Otter Civet 
(Cynogale bennettii), Bornean Clouded Leopard (Neofelis diardi ssp. borneensis), and Sun Bear 
(Helarctos malayanus). The Project will make significant efforts to ensure the preservation of diverse 
and endangered species within the project area, emphasizing its ecological importance. 
SPCP is also committed to raising community awareness about sustainable natural resource 
management and fostering collaborative partnerships with 14 neighboring villages. The project 
provides direct employment opportunities through activities such as forest patrolling, monitoring, and 
restoration. Additionally, SPCP ensures communities have access to training and capacity-building 
programs aimed at improving entrepreneurship skills in key areas, including sustainable agriculture, 
fisheries, and zero-burning farming practices. These initiatives foster economic growth and sustainable 
livelihoods while promoting active community involvement in conservation efforts. 
At the time of project registration, the area where SPCP is implementing a project is not included in a 
jurisdictional REDD+ program.

2.1.4 Project Eligibility (VCS, 3.1, 3.6, 3.8, 3.18, 4.1; CCB Program Rules, 4.2.4, 4.6.4) 
The project meets all applicable rules and requirements set out under the VCS and CCB Programs, 
including:  
• The project activity falls under VCS Scope 14 which concerns Agriculture, Forestry, and Other Land 
Use (AFOLU) and which is defined as “The sectoral scope that covers GHG emissions and emission 
reductions and/or removals from project or program activities in the agriculture, forestry, and other 
land use/land use change sectors and for which the VCS Program has established rules and 
requirements with respect to specific project categories” as described in detail in this Project 
Description.  
• The project is assigned to the correct AFOLU project category (as given in Section 2.1.3 above, and 
as described in detail in Section 3.1.5) including their spatial separation within the project area.   
• All related category requirements are met, as described in detail below in Section 3.1.2.

2.1.9 Project Ownership (VCS, 3.2, 3.7, 3.10; CCB, G5.8) 
PT Annisa Surya Kencana (PT ASK) as project proponent is the rights-holder of forest management 
concessionaire under the Forest Utilization Business Licensing (Perizinan Berusaha Pemanfaatan 
Hutan/PBPH) scheme with the Decree of the Minister of Environment and Forestry SK.965/MENLHK / 
SETJEN/HPL.0/10/2021 on October 25, 2021.  
In accordance with Government Regulation No. 23 of 2021 on Forestry Administration, PT ASK is fully 
authorized to implement a multi-forestry business system (Multi Usaha Kehutanan/MUK) within its 
licensed area. This regulation, particularly Article 181, explicitly recognizes the utilization of 
environmental services for carbon sequestration and/or storage as a legitimate component of forest 
utilization.  As such, PT ASK’s initiative to engage in carbon-related activities within the concession 
period is not only permissible under the law but also aligned with sustainable forest management and 
climate change mitigation 
This license, combined with applicable laws, authorizes ASK to generate and sell carbon credits and 
implement planned project activities. This complies with the VCS Standard 4.7 definition of "Project 
ownership arising or granted under statute, regulation, or decree by a competent authority. 
2.1.10 Project Start Date (VCS, 3.8) 
Project start date  
01-January -2023 
Justification  
Following the company's acquisition on 04-March-2022, the new 
management made a strategic decision to abandon the timber-
based business model originally planned by the previous owner. 
Instead, the company shifted its focus toward the protection and 
conservation of peatlands and forests which lead to the generation 
of greenhouse gas emissions avoidance.  
Under the baseline scenario, ASK was required to submit and 
receive approval for its logging plan by 25-October-2022; within one 
year of the date of license issuance. This in turn would have 
resulted in the start of logging activities by late 2022. Accordingly, 
the project start date was conservatively set as 01-January-2023.

which activities that led to the generation of reductions or removals 
were implemented through ‘changing agricultural or forestry 
practices, rewetting, restoring hydrological functions, or 
implementing management or protection plans’ 
2.1.11 Benefits Assessment and Project Crediting Period (VCS, 3.9; CCB, G1.9) 
Crediting period  
The project crediting period runs from the project start date on 01-
January-2023 until 24
-
October-2051 (28.75 years). This crediting 
period corresponds to the remaining term of the initial licence held 
by ASK. This period also corresponds to the period over which 
changes in climate change adaptive capacity and resilience, 
biodiversity and community well-being resulting from project 
activities are monitored. Prior to the end of this licence term, ASK 
will seek a licence term extension to extend the project activities, as 
allowed in Ministry of Forestry regulation, for a further 90-year 
period. Once this extension is in place, the project will seek renewal 
of the project Crediting Period up to 25-October-2141, representing 
the maximum total Crediting Period allowable of 100 years, as per 
VCS V4.7 section 3.9.3.

2.1.14 Physical Parameters (CCB, G1.3) 
2.1.14.1 Project location  
SPCP is situated in Kapuas Hulu District of West Kalimantan Province (Figure 1), encompassing two 
sub-districts: Putussibau Utara and Embaloh Hilir Sub-district (Figure 2). SPCP is geographically located 
between 0⁰ 51’ - 1⁰ 08’ N and 112⁰ 37’ - 112⁰ 59’ E, covering two distinct and ecologically significant 
ecosystems; peat swamp forest and dryland forest (Figure 9). 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
19 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 2.  SPCP area based on administration jurisdiction 
2.1.14.2 Topography  
The landscape of SPC can be categorized as a peat-covered wetland that evolved on a tertiary 
geological basin (the East Ketungau a.k.a the Mandai basin), situated at about 37 meters above mean 
seal level (with minimum and maximum elevations of 25 and 237 m-msl respectively). The peat basin 
of the landscape is slightly sloping from the northeast to the southwest at 0.08% rate. Local terrain is 
dominated by flat slopes (0 — 0.25%) on peatlands (to the southwest) and undulating hilly terrain (>2%) 
on hilly areas to the northeast (see also Figure 3 and Figure 4). 
Topography of the landscape strongly affects its hydrology and ecology. Sitting at more elevated 
position than those of coastal peatlands, the landscape had been given far earlier onset of peat 
accumulation (about 40,000 years BP, i.e. when today’s SEA coastal peatlands were still free of 
mangrove and tidal flats). Having developed earlier allowed the peat substrates to evolve into higher 
maturity state (i.e. Sapric) and relatively lower hydraulic conductivity (ca. 1.6 m.day
-1
). Closer vicinity to 
mountain ranges makes annual precipitation of the landscape much higher (>5,000 mm) than those in 
coastal peatlands (usually 1500 – 3000 mm). 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

2.1.14.3 Soil characteristics 
Based on field survey data, the project area is predominantly characterized by peat soils, covering 
approximately 17,003.09 hectares (60%). The classification of peat soils follows the FAO definition, 
which sets a minimum threshold of 0.4 meters of organic material. Peat soil is primarily concentrated in 
the central and southern parts of the project area. In contrast, mineral soil—covering approximately 
11,503.09 hectares (40%)—are mainly located along the main riverbanks that traverses the project 
area and in the hilly regions in the north. Field measurements confirm peat depths ranging from 0.4 
meters to 21.54 meters. Further details on peat soil distribution are provided in Section 3.2.1.2. 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
22 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 5. Soil characteristics in SPCP area 
 
2.1.14.4 Climate  
The Project Area lies within the equatorial weather region of West Kalimantan and falls into wet tropical 
climate (Af) class in the Koppen, and A under Oldeman classification systems. Based on the weather 
records from 2003-2022 in Pangsuma Meteorological Station in Putssibau, Kapuas Hulu District (16 
km from the project boundary) by the Indonesian Agency for Meteorology, Climatology, and Geophysics 
(BMKG), the average temperature around the project area is relatively stable all-year at around 27°C 
(Error! Reference source not found.). The maximum and minimum temperature are also relatively stable 
at around 32°C and 23°C respectively, thus the diurnal temperature range is around 9°C. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
23 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 6. Average monthly temperature in Kapuas Hulu District (2003-2022) 
As weather observation data is sparse and limited, rainfall data was obtained from satellite 
precipitation products, from The Climate Hazards Group InfraRed Precipitation with Station data 
(CHIRPS) dataset. The dataset combines satellite observations with station data and can be used for 
hydrological simulation (Funk et al., 2015).  
According to Aldrian & Susanto (2003), the project area lies in a region with semi-monsoonal rainfall 
patterns, where there are two rainfall peaks within the year. The CHIRPS data (Figure 7) from 1983-
2002 show the pattern, where there are two peaks (October and December-January). However, as 
climate changed, the pattern has lost in the last 20 years before project start (2003-2022). The peak of 
the rain season is now in November-December (ca. 420 mm/month). Nevertheless, the peak of the dry 
season is still the same in July (ca. 250 mm/month). 
In addition to monsoon, the rainfall in the project area is affected by interannual weather events such as 
the El Niño Southern Oscillation (ENSO) and the Indian Ocean Dipole (IOD) (Kurniadi et al., 2021). For ENSO, 
El Niño events can cause decreased rainfall, while La Nina can cause the opposite effect. For IOD, positive 
phase leads to decreased rainfall, while negative phase leads to increased rainfall. 
The project area has a humid climate with the average relative humidity is relatively stable at around 83-
86% all year (data obtained from BMKG). The annual relative humidity pattern follows similar pattern to the 
rainfall; maximum in the end and start of the year, while minimum at the middle of the year. 
  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
24 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
  
 
Figure 7.  Average monthly rainfall and relative humidity around project area (Kapuas Hulu District) 
 
2.1.14.5 Hydrology 
Three main rivers traverse the landscape and serve as the natural drainage for the water balance of its 
hydrological system: the Palin, Seluan, and Sibau rivers. Below the foothills of the Betung Karihun 
range, peat soil has developed on top of a sandy loam deposit, and together they make a two-layered 
aquifer system and store fresh water in their soil pores.  
Most of SPCP area is still covered by good forest stand, which strongly shapes the hydrology of the 
peatland. Trees on peatland elevate water loss through transpiration, but at the same time also provide 
shading and maintain a unique moist, cool and wind-free microclimate on forest floor, reducing soil 
evaporation. Trees also act as the source of organic matter for the sustainability of the peat soil, 
notably being the main aquifer in this hydrology. 
Typical tropical peat swamp forest, like the SPCP, is characterized by a shallow water table depth, with a 
yearly average around 20 cm below soil surface. In dry seasons, when evapotranspiration exceeds 
rainfall, the water table depth usually drops deeper but rarely beyond 50 cm below the soil surface
8
. In 
wet seasons, soil pores are quickly filled to saturation and saturation-excess, owing to shallow water 
table, which causes flood and overland flow to take place. 
 
8
 As shown in 21 dipwells covering 2023 – 2024 period 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
25 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Water balance 
9
 of this tropical peat swamp forest is dominated by rainfall (5,878 mm yr
-1
) as the main 
water input and evapotranspiration (4,072 mm yr
-1
) as the main water loss components. Surface flow is 
estimated at 1,550 mm yr
-1
 and groundwater flow at 256 mm yr
-1
. The dominance of 
evapotranspiration in the water balance is attributed to the high atmospheric evaporation demand and 
shallow water table nature of the peat swamp forest. The low contribution of groundwater flow is due to 
flat topography (on peatlands) whereby steeper hydraulic gradients are impossible to form naturally. 
 
2.1.14.6 Types of Vegetation 
The project area encompasses two distinct ecosystems: the peat ecosystem and the dryland 
ecosystem, both of which are predominantly characterized by forest land cover. These ecosystems play 
critical roles in maintaining biodiversity, supporting unique flora and fauna, and regulating 
environmental services such as carbon storage and water management. The peat ecosystem is notable 
for its high organic matter content and its function as a significant carbon sink, while the dryland 
ecosystem provides a habitat for various terrestrial species and supports different ecological dynamics. 
  
Figure 8.  Aerial view of vegetation conditions within the project area 
 
The dryland ecosystem spans approximately 11,503 hectares, located in the northern part of the 
project area. Based on land cover classification, this ecosystem comprises three land cover types: 
secondary dryland forest (11,378 Ha), shrubland (40 Ha), and water body (86 Ha). The Secondary 
 
9
 Calculated by using Penman-Monteith evapotranspiration model, calibrated storage coefficient 
parameter, and in situ rainfall, RH and temperature records (2024) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
26 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Dryland Forest represents a significant portion, characterized by dense tree coverage that supports a 
variety of flora and fauna adapted to relatively drier conditions. Shrubland represents non-forest areas, 
sparsely covered by herbs, woody shrubs or consists of barren land.  
The peat ecosystem covers a significantly larger area within the project, estimated at approximately 
17,003 hectares, and is in the middle and southern part of the project area. Recognized as a unique 
and ecologically vital system, tropical peatland ecosystems play a critical role in global carbon 
dynamics, serving as one of the planet's largest terrestrial carbon stores. By sequestering vast amounts 
of carbon in its dense organic layers over millennial timescales, it mitigates rises in greenhouse gas 
emissions and supports climate regulation. This ecosystem is comprised of three land cover types: 
Secondary Peat Swamp Forest (16,970 Ha), swampy shrubland (15 Ha), and water body (18 Ha). The 
Secondary Peat Swamp Forest represents a significant portion, characterized by dense tree coverage 
that grows on peat soil and swampy conditions. Tree species growing in peat swamp forests exhibit 
unique morphological and physiological characteristics that enable them to survive in extreme 
environmental conditions—conditions that are vastly different from those found in dryland ecosystems. 
These traits are essential for thriving in waterlogged, acidic, low-oxygen, and nutrient-deficient soils that 
define peat swamp environments. Field surveys identified common tree species in peat swamp forest 
including Gonystylus bancanus, Koompasia malaccensis, Shorea   belangeran, Dyera polyphylla, 
Alstonia pneumatophora, Campnosperma spp, and Mellahoornea walichii.  
Historically, the forest has experienced degradation in the past due to timber extraction by a logging 
company. Before PT ASK obtained the license, the previous logging company operating in the area 
primarily harvested high-value timber species, including Shorea spp., Dipterocarpus spp., and 
Koompasia malaccensis (see Section 2.1.15.3 for details).  
Swampy Shrubland typically represents non forest area sparsely covered by herbs, ferns, or shrubs that 
grow on peat soil and swampy condition. Common plant species found in this area include Melastoma 
malabathricum, Stenochlaena palustris, Pteridium aquilinum, Nephrolepis biserrata, Mikania 
micrantha, Scleria sumatrensis, and Phragmites karka. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
27 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 9. The Vegetation Characteristic of Project Area 
A field survey conducted in 2023 identified at least 131 tree species from 35 families within the 
Permanent Sampling Plots (PSP). Further analysis shows that 86 tree species are found in dryland 
forests, while 90 species are present in peat swamp forests, with several species occurring in both 
ecosystems. Detailed information on these tree species can be found in Appendix 10: List Flora found 
within project area. 
2.1.15 Social Parameters (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G1.3) 
2.1.15.1 Communities and Project Area 
There are no settlements within the concession area. The project area is neighbored by 14 villages, 
predominantly inhabited by the Dayak community, an indigenous group with a strong cultural and 
historical connection to the land. The local economy is largely reliant on natural resources, with 
agriculture and fishing serving as the primary means of livelihood.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
28 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 10. Settlement neighboring the project area 
While most of the population consists of indigenous Dayak people, a small number of migrants from 
other regions have settled in the southern part of the area. Farming is generally small-scale and 
subsistence-based, with villagers primarily cultivating paddy rice using traditional shifting cultivation 
techniques. Fishing remains an essential economic activity, typically carried out in nearby rivers using 
handcrafted manual tools, reflecting the community’s deep-rooted knowledge of the environment and 
sustainable resource use. 
Communities surrounding the project area can be classified based on two primary patterns of land use 
and adaptation for economic activities.  
• In the southern part, the majority of communities rely on fishing as they primary livelihood, engaging 
in both capture fisheries and aquaculture, including cage and pond-based fish farming. The Kapuas 
River with its tributaries are a valuable natural asset for the people in this area, providing an 
abundant and diverse supply of fish that supports household income. 
Fish availability and sustainability are relatively well maintained through the establishment of 
designated catch zone and a restricted (prohibited) zone fishing areas, which is currently still 
implemented. Agricultural practices, both plantations and rice cultivation, are scarcely found in this 
region. This is due to not only to the community’s strong fishing-oriented culture, but also the area’s 
unsuitability for agriculture as it is highly to prone to flooding caused by the overflow of the Kapuas 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
29 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
River. In the past 3-4 years, the region has seen a significant increase in the cultivation of kratom 
trees (Mitragyna speciosa), which is a typical plant in Kapuas Hulu that has emerged as an 
alternative source of income.  
• In the northern part, people generally practice shifting cultivation to grow paddy and other 
commodities. However, the cultivation cycle has become relatively short, with farmers returning to 
previously used plots within 2-3 years. This practice is predicted to decline over time, as many 
people are starting to be reluctant to open new land for farming. Contributing factors include the 
increasing distance from their settlements to available land, rising costs of land preparation and 
harvesting, and limited labor availability. Additionally, more profitable and accessible livelihood 
alternative such as horticulture, poultry, livestock farming (e.g., cattle or pigs) are becoming more 
attractive. Despite this shift, some communities in both the northern and southern regions continue 
to rely on mixed sources of income from non-timber forest products (NTFPs). The most prominent 
among these is the collection of natural honey (Madu Lalau) and resin. On a smaller scale, products 
such as rattan and tengkawang fruit are also harvested. 
2.1.15.2 Land Use and Economic Activities  
Local communities have long benefited from the forest for both timber and non-timber forest products 
(NTFPs), which play a vital role in their daily lives. Timber is primarily used for constructing traditional 
houses “Rumah Betang”, crafting household tools, and making canoes, while non-timber products such 
as rattan, honey, medicinal plants, and resin provide additional sources of income and materials for 
traditional practices.  
The forest plays a vital role in sustaining the community’s dietary needs and cultural traditions, serving 
as a key source of food and spiritual connection. Local hunters rely on traditional methods to catch 
deer, wild boar, and small mammals, providing a ready supply of protein for their households. Beyond 
sustenance, hunting is deeply intertwined with cultural practices, as certain animals are hunted for 
traditional ceremonies and rituals, reinforcing the community’s ancestral customs. 
The Kapuas River, along with several smaller rivers flowing through the project area—Seluan River, Uluk 
Palin River, and Sibau River—plays a crucial role in the community’s fishing activities, providing both 
food security and economic sustenance. Fishing is traditionally carried out using handcrafted nets, 
traps, and bamboo fishing tools, techniques that have been passed down through generations. Due to 
the river-wetland landscape, fishing is particularly prevalent among communities in the southern 
villages, including Keliling Semulung, Pala Pintas, Nanga Palin, Nanga Lauk, and Nanga Nyabau, where 
water bodies serve as primary sources of livelihood. 
In certain areas of the project site, slash-and-burn cultivation has been sporadically practiced by local 
communities. The governor has taken side of the practice as stipulated in the Governor regulation 
103/2020 allowing the communities to apply burning methods within a safe threshold. This traditional 
agricultural method involves clearing small plots of land, burning vegetation to enrich the soil, and 
cultivating crops before allowing the land to regenerate. While this practice has historically been part of 
sustainable shifting cultivation, its impact varies based on land availability and fallow periods. Slash-
and-burn farming is more commonly observed in the northern villages, including Benua Tengah, Sungai 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
30 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Uluk Palin, Seluan, Tanjung Lasa, Nanga Awin, Tanjung Beruang, and Jangkang, where communities rely 
on this technique for subsistence farming. 
The high market demand for cash crops has also influenced land use in the northern villages. Due to 
the lucrative nature of commodities such as oil palm, rubber, and kratom, some of community 
members have converted small areas into plantations. 
Additionally, certain locations within the project area hold significant cultural value. One such site is 
Bukit Tendek, located in Nanga Nyabau village, which has been carefully protected and preserved by 
the local community for generations. This sacred site serves as a place for cultural purposes and 
spiritual rituals. 
2.1.15.3 Relevant Historic Conditions 
The project area is part of a larger forested regional area in Kapuas Hulu District, which has a long 
history of commercial timber utilization. Since the 1970s, the area was under the management of Bumi 
Raya Utama Wood Industries, a timber concession holder that operated under a license issued by the 
government. The company played a significant role in the region's forest industry, extracting timber for 
commercial purposes. In 2004, Bumi Raya Utama Wood Industries secured a license extension that 
would allow operations to continue until 2038. However, the company ceased operations in 2013 and 
formally returned the concession to the government. Following the withdrawal of Bumi Raya Wood 
Industries, the area remained vacant and open for new concession applications from the private sector. 
During this period, the social conditions in the project area were closely tied to the commercial timber 
industry. Many local communities depended on logging and timber-related work for their livelihoods 
including becoming the company’s employees, while traditional activities like farming and NTPF 
gathering continued on a smaller scale. Land tenure became increasingly complex as commercial 
operations overlapped with traditional community areas.  
The project area is traversed by a national road, which was re-constructed in the 1990s, providing 
access to the region. The company’s logging operations further enhanced accessibility by developing 
additional routes and infrastructure to support timber transport. In 2023, the national government 
undertook significant upgrades to the road, greatly improving its condition and making the area much 
more accessible. 
Recognizing the potential of the site, the previous management of PT ASK initiated the application 
process for a new timber concession in 2019. After meeting the necessary regulatory requirements and 
approval processes, PT ASK was successfully granted the concession license in October 2021. 
2.1.15.4 Socio-cultural Information  
Fourteen (14) villages neighboring the concession area of PT ASK consists of two sub-districts, 
Putussibau Utara and Embaloh Hilir. In Putussibau Utara Sub-district there are nine (9) villages; Sibau 
Hulu, Tanjung Lasa, Nanga Awin, Jangkang, Tanjung Beruang, Seluan, Sungai Uluk Palin, Benua Tengah, 
and Nanga Nyabau. These villages are ethnically composed of the Dayak Kantuk, Dayak Taman, Dayak 
Tamam Balo, Dayak Iban, Malay and Javanese tribes. Meanwhile in Embaloh Hilir sub-district there are 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
31 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
five (5) villages; Nanga Palin, Keliling Semulung, Kirin Nangka, Pala Pintas, and Nanga Lauk. These 
villages are dominated by Malay and Dayak Kantuk tribes. 
According to data from the Central Statistics Agency of Kapuas Hulu District in 2022, the population of 
14 villages neighboring with the concession area was 9,688 people, consisting of 4,894 males and 
4,794 females. The population composition of the population of Kapuas Hulu District by age based on 
age group shows that the largest percentage of the population is in the 15-39 and 40-64 year age 
groups.   
The Community Welfare Indicator of Kapuas Hulu District, published by the Central Statistics Agency in 
2022, highlighted that the condition of population's education can be assessed through education 
completed by the population aged 7-24 years and older. Among this population group, 41.57 percent 
had completed elementary school or an equivalent level, 11.65 percent had completed junior high 
school or an equivalent, and 16.15 percent had completed high school or a higher level of education. 
At the sub-district level (Putussibau Utara and Embaloh Hilir), the majority of the population completed 
elementary school. Based on baseline assessment in 14 villages, the availability of educational 
facilities, accessibility, and awareness of the importance of education remain very limited. Educational 
infrastructure, teaching quality, and community participation levels still require further improvement. 
Field findings in Landau Ipoh Hamlet, Nanga Awin Village, an elementary school had to close due to the 
absence of teachers, primarily because of the village’s remote location. To access higher educational 
facilities, the students within 14 villages need to travel to the district capital, Putussibau.  
Surrounded by rivers, swamps, and forests, communities living in 14 villages are relatively vulnerable to 
various diseases.  Baseline assessment identified the common diseases experienced by communities 
are diarrhea, itching/skin infection, respiratory difficulty, coughing, and tetanus. The conditions are 
exacerbated by the lack of available health facilities. Health services are only accessible at the sub-
district level through community health centers, while auxiliary community health centers and Polindes 
(village maternity clinic) in the village are not operating due to the absence of health workers. The lack 
of healthcare facilities and personnel has a significant impact on community health, particularly for 
pregnant women and children. To access adequate maternal health services, residents have to travel 
long distances to Putussibau city, often burdened by high cost for transportation. 
The community’s livelihoods in 14 villages mostly consist of fishermen and kratom (Mitragyna 
speciosa) farmers, with other source of income including vegetable gardening, honey collection, rattan 
harvesting, resin collection and rubber plantations. In the past two years, the income of kratom farmers 
has steadily declined, with the price decreasing to IDR 10,000 from IDR 16,000 per kilogram at the 
highest price point within six months. This decline was driven by decreasing demand from foreign 
markets, especially from the United States, where several states issuing regulations prohibiting kratom 
consumption. Additionally, the decision by the National Narcotics Agency (BNN) to classify kratom as a 
Class I narcotic and prohibit its consumption starting in 2024 has further reduced domestic demand.

2.1.17 Project Activities and Theory of Change (VCS, 3.6; CCB, G1.8) 
SPCP’s area faces increasing threats from deforestation, biodiversity loss, and carbon emissions due to 
unsustainable practices. The project addresses these challenges by integrating carbon finance, 
responsible resource use, and community development to establish a sustainable project zone. The 
SPCP is designed to address interconnected environmental, social, and economic challenges through a 
multi-dimensional approach centered on sustainable forest and peatland management, biodiversity 
conservation and community empowerment. 
 
Goal 1: Mitigate Carbon Emissions by preventing deforestation, peatland degradation, and wildfires 
while enhancing carbon sequestration through forest and peatland restoration and a scientifically 
robust MRV system  
 
The first goal of the project is to mitigate GHG emission through preventing forest and peatland 
disturbance, increase forest growth and avoid emissions from peatland restoration. This goal aligns 
with global climate action priorities, particularly the need to reduce greenhouse gas emissions from 
land-use changes.  The project will not claim carbon credit from its effort in increasing forest growth. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
34 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Outputs and Activities 
Key activities under this goal include: 
1. Eliminate logging, diverting planned deforestation from timber plantation and selective logging into 
fully protected forest. 
2. Implementation of Forest Protection Systems and Integrated Water Management: An early detection 
and response system will be established to safeguard forests and peatlands from deforestation, 
degradation, drainage and fires. This system will involve community and other supportive 
stakeholders’ (NGOs and GOs), ensuring local ownership and sustainability. 
3. Restoration of Peat and Forest Ecosystems: Degraded peatlands and forests will be rehabilitated 
through hydrological restoration and rewetting efforts. These activities will enhance carbon storage, 
reduce emissions, and restore ecological functions. 
4. Awareness Raising and Capacity Building: By enhancing local capacity for forest and peatland 
conservation, the project aims to foster a culture of environmental stewardship amongst key relevant 
stakeholders, The project will conduct community meetings and training sessions to raise awareness 
about the impacts of illegal logging, hunting, and slash-and-burn practices. The meetings will be 
conducted through adaptive methods where applicable: e.g. Participatory Rural Appraisal, Focus 
Group Discussion and/or Training of Trainers.  
5. Zero-Burning Agriculture and Fire Risk Reduction: Training programs will promote sustainable land-
use practices, such as zero-burning agriculture to minimize fire-related incidents. Inception of 
Hügelkultur, the use of mini hand tractor and gradually internalizing organic farming will be 
distinctive to the program. This will be complemented by the implementation of fire response 
systems and community patrols to monitor and address forest disturbances. 
Outcomes and Impacts 
The project’s outcomes include reduced deforestation and peatland degradation, enhanced carbon 
sequestration, and improved community and ecosystem resilience. These outcomes will contribute to 
measurable reductions in greenhouse gas emissions, aligning with international climate targets. 
Additionally, the project’s focus on community involvement ensures that conservation efforts are locally 
driven and sustainable. 
 
Goal 2: Improved Community Livelihoods through Sustainable Forest management, peatland 
restoration, and income-generating community-based enterprises linked to environmental conservation. 
The overarching goal of the project is to enhance community livelihoods by promoting sustainable forest 
and peatland management, and fostering income-generating, community-based enterprises tied to 
environmental conservation. This goal is rooted in the recognition that environmental sustainability and 
economic development are mutually reinforcing. By integrating conservation efforts with livelihood 
programs, the project seeks to create a virtuous cycle where ecological restoration supports economic 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
35 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
stability, and vice versa. Additionally, the project supports improved health services, education 
initiatives, and access to clean water as part of its broader development approach. These social 
supports are essential to underpinning sustainable development. 
 
Outputs and Activities 
To achieve this goal, the project will implement a series of targeted activities, including: 
1. Promotion of Sustainable Livelihood Programs: The project will provide support (financial, technical 
and capacity building) to establish community-based enterprises or groups, with a focus on 
sustainable livelihoods. Chicken coops and maize farms will be prioritized where they suit the 
communities’ needs and capacity. The establishment of these enterprises is expected to generate 
consistent income, enabling beneficiaries to meet their basic needs and contribute to local economic 
stability. 
2.  Facilitation of Market Linkages and Enhancing Product Value: The project will empower local 
businesses by connecting them to markets. The project will explore market potential and analyse 
opportunities for improvement. With improved market access and/or product value enhancement, 
the community will be more motivated, as it enhances profitability and builds economic resilience, 
enabling a solid base for sustainable financial growth. 
3.  Capacity Building for Environmental Protection: Training programs will be conducted to enhance local 
communities’ knowledge and skills in forest monitoring, peatland restoration, and sustainable 
resource management. This capacity-building effort will empower communities to take an active role 
in conservation, ensuring the long-term sustainability of restored ecosystems. 
4.  Employment in Forest Protection and Peatland Restoration: The project will create employment 
opportunities linked to environmental conservation, such as forest monitoring and peatland 
rehabilitation. These jobs will not only provide income but also foster a sense of ownership and 
responsibility toward natural resources. 
5.     Supporting health, education and access to clean water: The project supports improved health 
services, including community health campaigns, provision of nutritious food for children, and 
malaria prevention through fogging. It also strengthens education through the distribution of learning 
materials and awareness campaigns on healthy ecosystems. To promote safer and healthier 
household water use, the project facilitates access to clean water by developing deep wells. 
Outcomes and Impacts 
The expected outcomes include the establishment of financially stable community enterprises, 
improved local capacity for environmental protection, and the creation of sustainable livelihoods linked 
to conservation efforts. In the long term, these outcomes will contribute to improved community well-
being, reduced poverty, reduced dependency on unsustainable practices for livelihoods and enhanced 
ecosystem health. The project’s emphasis on inclusivity — particularly its focus on women and 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
36 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
marginalized groups — ensures that its benefits are equitably distributed, fostering social cohesion and 
resilience. 
 
Goal 3: Protect and restore critical habitats to support thriving ecosystems, halting hunting and 
poaching to conserve wildlife. 
The third goal focuses on biodiversity conservation, aiming to protect and restore critical Key Species 
and their habitats, halt hunting and poaching, and conserve wildlife. This goal underscores the intrinsic 
value of biodiversity and its role in maintaining ecosystem services. 
Outputs and Activities 
Key activities include: 
1.  Awareness Raising and Behavioural Change: Outreach campaigns will educate communities about 
the ecological and legal consequences of illegal hunting. These efforts will drive behavioural change, 
fostering support for wildlife conservation. 
2.  Biodiversity Monitoring and Law Enforcement: A systematic monitoring system will be established to 
track poaching activities populations through Hunting Respondent survey and wildlife populations 
using traditional surveys, as well as technologies such as Camera Trap and thermal imaging, 
Community-based patrols will enhance enforcement efforts, leading to a measurable reduction in 
illegal hunting. 
Outcomes and Impacts 
The expected outcomes include increased community support for wildlife conservation, stabilized or 
growing wildlife populations, and reduced poaching incidents. These outcomes will contribute to the 
long-term preservation of biodiversity and the ecological integrity of the project area. 
2.1.18 Sustainable Development Contributions (VCS, 3.17) 
The project is committed to actively contribute to multiple Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) in 
alignment with Indonesia’s Presidential Regulation No. 59/2017 on SDG implementation by integrating 
well-being programs, environmental conservation, economic empowerment, and governance initiatives 
as below:  
• To reduce poverty (SDG 1), the project enables Community Enterprise Program Development 
by providing in-kind support and training, empowering communities to generate income 
through sustainable means. It prioritizes support for the poorest households, ensuring 
inclusive economic growth and reducing inequality. Furthermore, local wisdom is recognized 
and integrated into sustainable development initiatives. 
• To enhance food security and promote sustainable farming (SDG 2), the project provides 
agricultural training to improve yields, establishes demonstration plots, and encourages crop 
diversity.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
37 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Education and awareness-raising (SDG 4) are also prioritized, with initiatives to support 
students and increase environmental awareness within communities. Through these 
multifaceted efforts, the project strengthens local resilience, promotes sustainable 
development, and ensures long-term environmental and social benefits. 
• In safeguarding water resources (SDG 6), the project protects watersheds through forest 
conservation, restores peatlands, thereby enhancing water quality for local communities.  
• To foster economic resilience (SDG 8), the project supports small-scale enterprises engaged 
in eco-friendly products and sustainable agriculture, providing alternative income sources 
while promoting responsible resource use.  
• The project also advances forest management innovation (SDG 9) by integrating technology 
for fire detection and carbon monitoring, enhancing conservation efforts.  
• Additionally, the project promotes sustainable resource use (SDG 12) by utilizing agricultural 
waste to support regenerative agricultural practices, reducing environmental impact and 
improving soil health.  
• Farmer groups are supported in adopting Zero Burning Agriculture, reducing environmental 
impact and promoting responsible land management (SDG 13, SDG 1). Additionally, training 
on agriculture and sustainable livelihoods equips communities with knowledge and skills to 
improve productivity and sustainability. 
• To protect biodiversity and preserve ecosystems (SDG 15), the project contributes to the 
protection of endangered species and the conservation of their habitats within globally 
recognized High Conservation Value Areas. By implementing habitat restoration and species 
protection strategies, the project ensures the long-term sustainability of key species and the 
ecological health of the landscape

2.1.21 Benefit Permanence (CCB, G1.11) 
SPCP is designed to ensure the long-term permanence of its climate, community, and biodiversity 
benefits through a comprehensive strategy encompassing legal, institutional, ecological, and socio-
economic intervention. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
44 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The project is implemented under an initial 28.75-year term. To ensure continuity and permanence of 
benefits, the project proactively plans to seek an extension of up to 90 years prior to the expiry of this 
initial term, in line with prevailing regulations. This extended timeline underscores the project's 
commitment to long-term sustainability. 
SPCP actively engages in advocacy with government authorities to promote full legal protection of any 
remaining intact peatland ecosystems. As an alternative to project extension, the formal designation of 
the area as a legally protected zone is pursued to institutionalize conservation gains and ensure their 
permanence beyond the life of the project. 
The community development program implemented by project aims to foster sustainable, forest-
compatible economic alternatives that strengthen the local economy. With this condition, community 
dependence on destructive practices such as illegal logging, hunting, or land clearing is expected to 
significantly decline, reducing or eliminating the penetration into forests and peatlands within the 
project area. In contrast, empowered and economically supported communities will actively support 
project efforts to protect and restore the project area into the future. 
Throughout the project period, SPCP will actively engage with key stakeholders including local 
communities, government agencies, and civil society, to ensure that project programs are aligned with 
other stakeholders. This collaborative approach is intended to embed the project's interventions into 
local systems, increasing their sustainability and relevance throughout the implementation period. 
SPCP implements a robust, credible and transparent Measurement, Reporting, and Verification (MRV) 
system to ensure accurate tracking of its climate, community, and biodiversity outcomes. This MRV 
framework enhances the credibility and reputation of the project, thereby facilitating collaboration, 
partnerships, and transactions with wider international stakeholders, including markets and 
institutions. This strengthens the project’s long-term viability and aligns it with global standards of 
accountability and transparency. 
SPCP continues to advocate for the recognition of the long-term societal, economic, and environmental 
benefits of protecting peatland ecosystems. By integrating these values into broader policy and 
planning frameworks, the project seeks to secure enduring support for conservation goals. 
Through these integrated measures, SPCP aims to secure the permanence of its climate, community, 
and biodiversity benefits, ensuring they endure both during and beyond the project’s operational 
lifetime. 
2.1.22 Financial Sustainability (CCB, G1.12) 
The project has secured stable and sufficient financing to support the implementation of all planned 
activities throughout the entire crediting period. This ensures the successful execution of key initiatives, 
including forest conservation, biodiversity protection, community development, and climate adaptation 
efforts.

2.2 Without-project Land Use Scenario and Additionality  
2.2.1 Conditions Prior to Project Initiation and Land Use Scenarios without the Project 
(VCS, 3.13; CCB, G2.1) 
The project area consists of two distinct ecosystems: peat swamp and dryland forest, both of which are 
predominantly covered by natural forest. Further details on the area’s climate, hydrology, topography, 
historical conditions, soils, vegetation, and ecosystems are provided in Section 2.1.14. Historically, the 
area was managed for timber extraction by a private company from the 1970s until the concession was 
returned  to  the  government  in  2013.  Following  the  return  of  the  concession,  the  area  remained 
unlicensed. In 2019, PT Annisa Surya Kencana (PT ASK) initiated the process of applying for the area and 
formally obtained a concession license in 2021.   
Following  the  issuance  of  the  license,  the  area  was  initially  planned  for  logging  and  the  conversion  of 
natural  forest  into  industrial  timber  plantations.  However,  the  acquisition  of  the  company  by  new 
ownership in 2022, shifting the business model from forest exploitation to protection and conservation, 
established the foundation for the project’s implementation.  This demonstrated that the project was 
developed  after  planned  logging  and  forest  conversion  were  in  place  and  was  specifically  designed  to 
prevent the anticipated deforestation and forest degradation. 
In  the  absence  of  the  project,  eight  alternative  land  use  scenarios  were  identified:  selective  logging, 
industrial timber plantation, commercial oil palm plantation, smallholder agriculture, mining, protected 
forest,  social  forestry,  and  unprotected  forest.  Based  on  the  application  of  methodology  VT0001,  as 
detailed   in   Section 3.1.5.2,   the   baseline   scenario   was   selected as selective   logging   and   the 
establishment of industrial timber plantations. 
2.2.2 Most-Likely Scenario Justification (CCB, G2.1) 
See Section 2.2.1 above and Sections 3.1.4 and 3.1.5 below for more details of the justification of the 
most likely without project scenarios. The most-likely without-project scenario (Logging and conversion 
to industrial tree plantation) was identified using VT0001: Tool for the Demonstration and Assessment 
of Additionality in VCS Agriculture, Forestry and Other Land Use (AFOLU) Project Activities (Version 3.0) 
which constitutes a credible, well-documented and published methodology, as per the requirements of 
the CCB standard v3.0. 
2.2.3 Community and Biodiversity Additionality (CCB, G2.2) 
Without SPCP, forest conversion into timber plantation and planned selective logging on peatlands 
would have proceeded as planned under the technical proposal and the concession license. This would 
have led to the destruction of peat swamp forests, significant greenhouse gas emissions, and the 
irreversible degradation of critical ecosystems. Local communities would have faced major disruptions 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
46 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
to their traditional livelihoods, including the loss of access to non-timber forest products (NTFP), 
fisheries, medicinal plants, and clean water sources provided by the intact peatlands forest. Economic 
opportunities linked to the forest would have slumped, replaced by insecure, low-wage labor in logging 
or plantation operations. The clearing and draining of peatlands would also have increased community 
exposure to peat fires, flooding, and land subsidence. Furthermore, the timber extraction would likely 
have exacerbated land tenure disputes and social conflicts in the villages surrounding the area. 
Through SPCP, communities would benefit from sustainable livelihood programs such as agriculture 
farming, agroforestry program, employee opportunity, and community-based forest management 
(engaged patrol or survey, restoration and conservation). The project is committed to developing the 
local community’s awareness on natural resource management, reducing conflict risks, and delivering 
socio-economic benefits that would not have occurred without project intervention. 
In the absence of the project, biodiversity would have been severely affected. The planned logging and 
forest conversion activities would have destroyed large areas of peat and forest, leading to habitat loss, 
fragmentation, and degradation for numerous species. Among the most severely impacted would be the 
critically endangered orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus pygmaeus), which relies on contiguous, undisturbed 
peatland forests for feeding, nesting, and movement. Habitat loss would have resulted in sharp 
declines in orangutan populations, increased human-wildlife conflict, and a heightened risk of local 
extinctions. SPCP is committed to safeguarding biodiversity by preserving critical habitats, maintaining 
landscape connectivity, and preventing ecological processes from being disrupted by drainage, fire, 
logging or conversion. By protecting peatland ecosystems, the project enhances ecosystem resilience 
and ensures the continued existence of the threatened species that would not have been possible 
under the business-as-usual scenario. 
Although national laws and regulations call for the protection of peatlands, enforcement within the 
project area has historically been weak. In practice, existing legal frameworks did not effectively prevent 
degradation and deforestation of peatland (Gunawan et al.,2024; Mizuno et al., 2023). Without SPCP, 
business-as-usual activities would result in irreversible environmental destruction, social damage, and 
biodiversity loss. Under its program, the project will strengthen local enforcement, promote sustainable 
peat-forest management, and align community development with conservation efforts. 
2.2.4 Benefits to be used as Offsets (CCB, G2.2) 
The SPCP ensures that all community and biodiversity benefits generated by the project are solely 
dedicated to its intended objectives. These benefits will not be used as offsets in any other program, 
project, or crediting mechanism. The SPCP does not seek to transfer, trade, or allocate these benefits 
beyond its defined scope 
2.3 Safeguards and Stakeholder Engagement   
2.3.1 Stakeholder Identification (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB G1.5) 
This project seeks to identify all stakeholders who have a relationship with the project and conduct an 
assessment of their rights, interests, and relevance to the project. This assessment has included both 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
47 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
institutional/non-community stakeholders and community stakeholders. The identification of 
stakeholders was carried out in stages in West Kalimantan Province and then through stakeholders in 
the districts. Institutional/non-community stakeholders (such as government and non-government 
related organizations) were initially identified through Focus Group Discussions (FGDs) attended by the 
North Kapuas Hulu Forest Stakeholder Unit (KPH) as stakeholders in the forestry sector.  
Local community stakeholders were carried out through consultation stages with institutional/non-
community stakeholders in the province and in the Regency and Sub-district, to explore deeper 
information regarding the relationship and connection of the community with the project area. The 
results of this consultation were that the project area was in 2 (two) sub-districts consisting of 14 
(fourteen) villages. Coordination began through discussions with the sub-districts, North Putussibau 
Sub-district and Embaloh Hilir Sub-district. Through these discussions and coordination, stakeholder 
involvement at the village level was identified. In meetings with stakeholders such as socialization and 
coordination, the level of interaction and dependence of local communities on the project area was 
known, including threats and challenges faced by the community, and through community approval, a 
socio-economic baseline assessment was conducted in 14 villages surrounding the project area.   
Through the socio-economic baseline assessment, stakeholders were identified and categorized into 
two main groups: community and non-community stakeholders, as detailed in Appendix 4: Stakeholder 
Description Table. The assessment revealed that all communities surrounding the project area have 
direct interests in the forest areas, due to the division of village territories that incorporate parts of the 
forest within their administrative boundaries. Community stakeholders include various groups with 
distinct interests such as farmers, fishermen, loggers, NTFP collectors, hunters, and marginalized 
groups including women, persons with disabilities, the elderly, and the poor. These also include 
indigenous communities residing within the 14 villages in the project area. Non-community 
stakeholders consist of government institutions at the village, district, and provincial levels, as well as 
NGOs and private sector entities. The identification of stakeholders, including those recognized by 
customary structures, was conducted through coordination and consultations with both community 
representatives and government bodies. 
2.3.2 Stakeholder Descriptions (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G1.6, G1.13) 
Based on the stakeholder identification process described in Section 2.3.1, the following is a summary 
of the stakeholders identified.  
a. Communities are the residents of the 14 (fourteen) villages adjacent to the project zone. 
b. Community groups – There are 6 community groups comprising of members of the 
villages with distinct interests and relevance to the project. 
c. Other stakeholders are the entities beyond the “communities” whose are affected by 
and/or may affect the project. The entities identified are from government sector, civil 
society groups, customary group/institution and the private sector. 
A detailed description of stakeholders is presented in Appendix 4: Stakeholder Description Table. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
48 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2.3.3 Stakeholder Access to Project Documents (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
A hard copy of the Project Description (PD) and Monitoring Report (MR) is kept in SPCP office and can 
be accessed by communities and other stakeholders identified. In addition, executive summaries in 
both English and Indonesian will be uploaded on the company’s website for broader accessibility. As 
part of its commitment to transparency, SPCP encourages and welcomes stakeholders to freely access 
project documents. The project also actively provides project documentation through its community 
engagement activities, including project socialization and consultation elaborated in Section 2.3.4. 
2.3.4 Dissemination of Summary Project Documents (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
SPCP is fully committed to ensuring that the project document is accessible and disseminated to the 
stakeholders. The dissemination to the community will be done through locally relevant channels, such 
as village meetings and community gatherings. The project will also share the documents with other 
relevant stakeholders through meetings and other appropriate occasions.  
To ensure stakeholder access to project documents, the SPCP Office will keep a complete printed 
version of the Project Description (PD) and Monitoring Report (MR) in English for public viewing. The 
summary of these documents will be translated into Indonesian and made available in both printed 
document and presentation material. This will be disseminated to community leaders, communities, 
and other stakeholders through meetings or other appropriate occasions. Additionally, the executive 
summaries in both English and Indonesian will be uploaded on the company’s website for broader 
accessibility. 
2.3.5 Informational Meetings with Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
ASK initiated a series of introductory and socialization meetings in July 2022, starting at the sub-
district level. These were subsequently followed by more detailed meetings at the village level and 
broader community engagements. Throughout this process, ASK coordinated closely with relevant 
stakeholders and conducted informational sessions, including meetings related to company 
introduction, Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC), development of livelihood program, and the 
socialization of SPCP. Full details of these meetings are provided in Appendix 5: List of . 
2.3.6 Risks from the Project and No Net Harm (VCS, 3.18, 3.19) 
Both natural and human-induced risks associated with the SPCP have been systematically identified, 
assessed, and quantified using the online NPRA (Non-Permanence Risk Analysis) tool. A detailed 
breakdown of these risk assessments can be found in Appendix 1 Project Risks Table. Additionally, 
potential risks that may impact stakeholder participation are specifically addressed in Section 4.5.3. To 
safeguard the well-being of employees and vulnerable groups, SPCP has implemented comprehensive 
safety measures, which are outlined in Section 2.3.19.  
2.3.7 Community Costs, Risks, and Benefits (CCB, G3.2) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
49 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The project has taken comprehensive steps to ensure that communities are fully informed about the 
potential costs, risks, and benefits associated with their participation in efforts to reduce forest 
degradation, biodiversity loss, and unsustainable land use. Information was disseminated through 
inclusive, participatory processes, using culturally appropriate communication methods in local 
languages to reach all segments of the community, including marginalized groups. 
A key area of focus is the development of alternative livelihoods, such as livestock farming (poultry and 
ruminants), fish farming, and sustainable agriculture. In the case of livestock-based enterprises, one of 
the primary risks identified is the availability and affordability of animal feed. Communities were 
informed that over-reliance on commercial feed – often expensive and inconsistently supplied due to 
the long distances from provincial distributors or ports – could undermine the sustainability and 
profitability of livestock ventures. These challenges were openly discussed, and potential mitigation 
measures, including local feed production and feed substitution strategies, were explored with 
community input. 
Additionally, the project’s efforts to reduce illegal logging and hunting, a common source of income for 
some individuals, may initially lead to short-term income losses. In response, the project developed a 
targeted livelihood-enterprise development program that prioritizes individuals previously involved in 
logging and hunting, offering alternative income opportunities through employment in forest protection, 
biodiversity conservation, and company field operations. These interventions are designed to transition 
communities toward long-term, stable and sustainable livelihoods that align with conservation goals. 
In regions where slash-and-burn agriculture is practiced, the project has acknowledged the risk of 
reduced income during the transition to more sustainable practices. This issue was transparently 
communicated, and communities were presented with support options, including a zero-burning 
agriculture program, capacity-building for improved farming techniques, and facilitation of market 
access for new products. These strategies aim to stabilize household incomes while promoting 
sustainable land management. 
Across all proposed livelihood interventions including agriculture-based, poultry-based, and aquaculture 
programs, the project is conducting participatory risk-benefit assessments. These assessments help 
ensure that appropriate mitigation and adaptation strategies are in place and reflect community input 
and priorities.

To ensure transparency and stakeholder participation, the project proponent will conduct targeted 
socialization and consultation activities with local communities and relevant stakeholders regarding the 
validation and verification processes. These activities will be publicly announced at least 30 days in 
advance. These activities are designed to ensure that stakeholders clearly understand the purpose and 
procedures of third-party validation and verification under the VCS and CCB Standards.

2.3.11 Continued Consultation and Adaptive Management (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
SPCP is committed to upholding the principle of Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC), recognizing 
that it is an ongoing and evolving process that must adapt to the changing dynamics of local 
communities. To ensure continuous communication and meaningful consultation, the project has and 
will employ multiple engagement approaches: 
1. Project Socialization: SPCP ensures that all stakeholders, including communities, government 
agencies, and local organizations, are fully informed about the project's objectives, activities, and 
expected outcomes. Project socialization is an essential ongoing process aimed at fostering 
transparency, building trust, and encouraging active participation.  
2. Community-Based Reporting: A dedicated village facilitator (Fasdes - Fasilitator Desa) and local 
village companion (PLD - Pendamping Lokal Desa) will report monthly on project progress, 
challenges, community needs, and concerns, particularly those of vulnerable groups. These reports 
will be systematically recorded in the project filing system and used to inform both formal and 
informal discussions, including focus group meetings and broader community consultations. Once 
key points are validated by the wider community, they will be integrated into the ASK socio-
economic work plan. 
3. Regular Community Engagement: ASK staff will conduct regular visits to each target community to 
discuss various aspects of the project, including the design, implementation, and progress of 
livelihood strategies. Constructive and actionable feedback gathered during these visits will be 
incorporated into the ASK work plan. 
4. Grievance Mechanism: A grievance mechanism will be maintained to ensure that community 
members have a formal avenue to voice concerns, raise grievances, and receive responses 
properly. 
SPCP follows a fully adaptable and responsive approach that involves regularly monitoring progress, 
gathering feedback from stakeholders, and assessing the impact of activities. It ensures that any 
dynamic changes or challenges, such as shifts in local conditions, unforeseen environmental impacts, 
or new regulations, are quickly identified. By doing so, the project can adjust its strategies to remain 
aligned with its objectives. If issues arise, such as changes in community needs or external factors 
affecting project implementation, the project will adapt its approach to effectively address these 
challenges.   
2.3.12 Stakeholder Consultation Channels (CCB, G3.5) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
56 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Since mid-2022, discussions and coordination with the village government, traditional leaders, and 
community leaders have been established by PT ASK both formally and informally, including 
coordination related to field work that will be carried out by involving the surrounding village community. 
Formal discussions are carried out in public spaces in the village or sub-district, and informal 
discussions are carried out according to the activities carried out that are related to the community in 
the village. 
Every socialization activity not only involves the village government, but also the community, by always 
ensuring the involvement of traditional leaders, women's leaders, youth leaders, people with 
disabilities; to be involved in socialization and discussion activities so that information is conveyed 
equitably to all groups. In formal activities, attendance lists are always available, and documentation is 
kept by the relevant department. 
2.3.13 Stakeholder Participation in Decision-Making and Implementation (VCS, 3.18, 
3.19; CCB, G3.6) 
Scope of community participation in decision-making and implementation covers different levels of 
engagement: 
1. At the project zone level, SPCP engages representatives of all villages. The exhibits of the kick-
off meeting in July 2022 are available. 
2. At the village level, SPCP convened a series of meetings to discuss and signed FPIC by the legal 
representatives of the village. SPCP allows the villages not to agree in the first meetings and 
seek amicable solutions to maintain the engagement. Example: FPIC meeting in Nanga Nyabau, 
Nanga Awin, Sungai Uluk Palin and Benua Tengah.  These meetings occurred between July 
2023 – May 2024. 
3. Prior to initiating an operational activity, SPCP consistently engage the villages reps to obtain 
inputs and decision for the implementation. These participations are recorded in the SPCP 
community dashboard.  
4. The project respects decisions from the village’s representatives, that are usually represented 
by the village head and/or village parliament, and/or village customary head.  The 
representatives reserve the right to ample time for understanding the nature of project’s 
activities, considering the impacts consolidating villagers’ inputs and are under no pressure to 
give their approval. 
5. The project strictly prohibits any violation against Ethics and Compliance policy in any 
engagement with the community and their representatives to obtain approval. This includes 
gratification, bribery or gift/hospitalities.

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

The Project ensures that no entity at the community level is discriminated against based on ethnicity,
