any anti-discriminatory practices encountered in project engagements or activities. 
2.3.15 Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.18.4; CCB, G3.8) 
SPCP has established grievance redress procedures as briefly summarized below: 
Development process 
SPCP engages with stakeholders, particularly local communities, in 
ways that respect their culture and traditional way of life. Such 
condition is addressed by project in the development of the 
project’s grievance redress procedure, which is designed to be 
accessible, culturally appropriate, and trusted.  
Complaints can be submitted verbally or in writing via project staff, 
village facilitators, or a dedicated phone line. The procedure 
provides a clear opportunity to raise concerns or complaints. The 
complaints are then systematically followed by the project to ensure 
timely and effective responses. In certain cases, traditional conflict 
resolution methods (such as mediation by customary authorities) 
are applied to resolve issues. This ensures that grievances are 
addressed fairly, promptly, and in alignment with local values and 
customs.  The project enables anonymous reports embedded in the 
web portal that is secured from unauthorized access. Project’s 
commitment to protect complainants' identities will be made public.  
A grievance committee will also be established through a credible 
and transparent process. 
Grievance redress 
procedure 
Complaint Recording and Classification. Reported public complaints 
are recorded, reviewed and classified according to the problem, 
then grouped according to the categories of supervision and non-
supervision. 
Initial Assessment and Evidence Gathering. Gathering evidence to 
substantiate public complaints. The process of proving public 
complaints is carried out through confirmation and clarification. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
58 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Acknowledgement and Initial Response. An official acknowledgment 
is issued to the complainant within 3 working days. An initial 
response outlining next steps is provided within 7 working days from 
acknowledgment. 
Complaint resolution.  The project team attempts to resolve the 
issue directly within 14 working days from the start of investigation. 
If direct resolution fails, the team will proceed with mediation. In 
certain conditions, mediation is needed with the involvement of a 
neutral third party, ideally within 30 working days from complaint 
registration.  The third party will have to obtain approval from the 
complainant to ensure they are neutral; this will be case-sensitive. 
 
If mediation is unsuccessful, the project may escalate to arbitration 
or, as a last resort, to the courts, in accordance with applicable legal 
and contractual frameworks. 
Resolution and Closure. Once resolved, the case is closed in 
agreement with the complainant. A formal closure notice is issued, 
and feedback is collected to improve future complaint handling. All 
documentations related the case are stored for audit, learning, and 
transparency 
2.3.16 Accessibility of the Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.19; 
CCB, G3.8) 
The project published and socialized the grievance system to all staff, communities in the concession 
area and Stakeholders through various media such as staff Meetings, social media such as WhatsApp, 
websites, and meetings with the community. Communities will have contact of both the village 
facilitator (Fasilitator Desa or Fasdes) and village local staff (Pendamping Lokal Desa or PLD) and the 
ASK Focal Point in the form of mobile phone numbers and email addresses.  
The project developed a clear and structured mechanism for handling complaints and resolving 
conflicts that may arise during its implementation. This mechanism outlines the step-by-step process 
for affected individuals or groups to submit their concerns, ensuring transparency, accessibility, and 
fairness. This includes designated contact points (contact number of Village Facilitator and ASK Focal 
point) and timelines for the response. To ensure community awareness and participation, the project 
conducted socialization sessions in villages, where local residents were informed about their rights, the 
available channels for lodging complaints, and how their concerns will be addressed.  
2.3.17 Worker Training (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.9) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
59 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The project is committed to providing training programs that enhance community participation and 
skills development. These programs will be designed to meet the specific needs of local communities, 
ensuring practical and effective learning opportunities.  
As of the preparation of this document, the project has conducted training sessions, including the 
following training: 
Training on Laying Duck Farming 
The training was conducted in Keliling Semulung Village on August 22–23, 2024, with the 
participation of 22 community members. The main objective was to enhance participants’ 
knowledge and technical skills in laying duck farming, covering key topics such as breed 
selection, feed and nutrition management, disease prevention, and egg production techniques. 
This capacity-building initiative has strengthened the community’s ability to implement and 
sustain laying duck farming practices in the village. 
Training on Laying Chicken Farming 
The training was held in Pala Pintas Village on August 20–21, 2024 and was attended by 33 
community members from two villages—Jangkang and Pala Pintas. The program aimed to 
strengthen participants’ capacity in managing egg-laying chickens by covering essential topics 
such as breed selection, feed and nutrition management, housing systems, disease prevention, 
and egg handling techniques. This training helped the community in implementing laying chicken 
farming in these villages. 
Training on Zero Burn Agriculture (ZBA) 
As part of the ZBA initiative, training was conducted in January 2024, targeting five villages—
Tanjung Lasa, Sibau Hulu, Tanjung Beruang, Jangkang, and Benua Tengah. This training marked 
a critical stage in the implementation process, equipping participants with the necessary 
knowledge and technical skills to adopt sustainable, fire-free land preparation methods. The 
training was attended by 15 community members. Delivered by experienced ZBA consultants, the 
training covered key aspects of ZBA practices, including soil management, organic inputs, 
planting techniques, and environmental safeguards. Supported by this training, ZBA participants 
have capacity to implement these sustainable practices in their respective villages. 
Training on Fire Prevention and Suppression.  
In January 2023, a total of 30 participants (comprising 16 staff members from ASK and 14 
community representatives from 14 villages) took part in a fire prevention and mitigation training 
held in Kedamin Hilir, Putussibau Selatan Sub-District. The training was facilitated by Manggala 
Agni, the official government agency responsible for forest fire management in Indonesia. 
The training aimed to strengthen participants’ understanding of forest fire risks and to equip 
them with practical skills in fire prevention and suppression techniques. To ensure ongoing 
preparedness, ASK has continued to conduct annual internal refresher sessions. These efforts 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
60 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
are intended to maintain the operational readiness of the fire response team and enhance the 
overall resilience of the project area against potential fire incidents. 
Additionally, the project prioritizes Health, Safety, Security, and Environment (HSSE) training, with a 
strong focus on fire prevention-response and other field activities. The safety of staff and communities 
is a top priority, with training efforts first directed toward high-risk activities to minimize hazards and 
ensure a secure working environment. 
2.3.18 Community Employment Opportunities (VCS, 3.19.13; CCB, G3.10) 
SPCP is dedicated to supporting the people living within the project zone and surrounding regions by 
creating fair and inclusive employment opportunities. Job openings are available to all, regardless of 
gender, age, social class, or ethnicity, ensuring equal access to work. The project prioritizes hiring from 
within the project-zone communities, recognizing that their involvement is crucial for the long-term 
success and sustainability of SPCP initiatives. 
All employees and contractors, whether in long-term or short-term roles, receive fair employment terms 
that align with prevailing regulations and local working conditions. Job vacancies are widely advertised 
through multiple channels, including online job provider/services, social media, the company website, 
and direct community outreach. To ensure maximum participation, SPCP field staff and facilitators visit 
all villages to announce certain job opportunities, allowing village governments to discuss role 
requirements and qualifications with community members. 
SPCP expects that around 60% of project field staff will be recruited from the project-zone communities 
and other regions within West Kalimantan Province. The remaining project staff will come from other 
parts of Indonesia, ensuring a diverse and skilled workforce to support project implementation. 
2.3.19 Occupational Safety Assessment (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.12) 
The Occupational Health and Safety Management System is part of the general risk management 
system of the project, which includes policies and commitments, planning, implementation, 
measurement, and evaluation. To determine the performance of the occupational health and safety 
management system applied at SPCP by Government Regulation of the Republic of Indonesia Number 
50 of 2012. 
For the project, a suitable and sufficient risk assessment must be carried out prior to a particular 
activity or task being carried out to eliminate, reduce or suitably control any associated risk to the 
health, safety and wellbeing of people involved with (or affected by) the task/activity in question. 
Once completed, a risk assessment should be reviewed periodically (proportionate to the level of risk 
involved) and in any case when either the current assessment is no longer valid and/or at any stage 
there have been significant changes to the specific activity or task. Relevant risk assessments should 
be reviewed following an accident, incident or ill-health event in order to verify if the control measures 
and level of evaluated risk were appropriate or require amendment. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
61 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
SPCP has completed a five-step process for risk assessment. This provides a useful checklist to follow 
to ensure that the assessment is suitably comprehensive. It involves: 
1. Identifying potential hazards 
2. Identifying who might be harmed by those hazards 
3. Evaluating risk (severity and likelihood) and establishing suitable precautions 
4. Implementing controls and recording your findings 
5. Reviewing your assessment and re-assessing if necessary. 
During day-to-day field activities, project staff are regularly exposed to a wide range of challenging 
natural conditions. These include physically demanding tasks carried out in wet, humid, and high-
temperature environments, often within remote peatland and forest areas. In addition to the physical 
strain, there are several significant safety and health risks associated with work. These risks include 
forest-land fires, potential accidents during water-based transportation, and interactions with illegal 
loggers, which may pose safety threats. Staff may also face encounters with wild animals, as well as 
exposure to insect bites and allergens, which can result in adverse health effects. 
To minimize and manage these risks, the project has implemented a set of comprehensive safety and 
risk mitigation measures, including but not limited to the following: 
1. Accredited firefighting training for SPCP’s personnel responsible for forest protection by relevant 
authority (Manggala Agni) on specific methods in suppressing fires in the peatlands and human-
induced burning.  SPCP also applies mandatory fire drill on monthly basis. 
2. Developing procedure and conducting in-house training/briefing on tackling illegal activities by 
adopting VPSHR approaches. 
3. In house training to mitigate risk from the snakebite and other potential risks from wild animals. 
4. Providing first aid kit for staff to carry into the forest which also includes a snake-bite kit. 
5. Ensuring members of survey team entering forest or remote location have been trained in medical 
emergency response and they have been assigned a first aid kit.  
6. Providing emergency communication tools and navigation tools such as GPS and satellite telephone. 
This is a part of the project’s emergency protocol. These are essential for ensuring effective 
coordination, rapid response, and personnel safety during field emergencies. 
7. Providing personal protective equipment such as safety helmets, rubber shoes, safety vests, 
uniform, heavy duty gloves, portable water bottles/bags, and life jackets. 
8. Providing additional logistics such as fuel, propeller for a boat, and water and meals enough for 
three extra days. 
9. Providing proper training on safety procedures, evacuation, communication, equipment uses, and 
shelter making to ensure worker safety and mitigate potential risks inherent to certain field activities 
such as fire suppression and surveys. Provide an assessment of substantial risks to worker safety 
that could arise due to project implementation.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
62 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
10. Establish a 'stop work' policy that allows project staff to suspend activities when encountering 
conditions that may pose danger or significant risk to their safety. This policy is a critical component 
of the project's health and safety framework, empowering staff to prioritize their well-being and 
prevent accidents during field operations. 
11. Providing safety training and preparedness in firefighting and fire management prior to the fire or dry 
season is a key component of the SPCP strategy. This proactive approach is intended to maintain 
and enhance the readiness of fire response teams, ensuring they are well-equipped and well-
coordinated ahead of high-risk periods. Early preparation also allows the project to identify and 
mitigate potential risks that may arise during fire suppression efforts or other related emergency 
activities 
The project has established a credible HSSE policy and ensured its full implementation to strengthen 
the company's HSSE system. As part of this commitment, PT ASK successfully obtained SMK3 (Sistem 
Manajemen Keamanan dan Keselamatan Kerja) certification (mandatory) and ISO 45001:2018 
certification, demonstrating its dedication to maintaining high standards of health, safety, security, and 
environment (HSSE). 
SPCP is committed to continuous improvement of its HSSE policy, procedures, and implementation.  
Internal audit is periodically conducted on an annual basis to improve the corrective action from the 
surveillance audit by the third-party auditor.  Improvements are also made to respond to emerging 
requiring attention or follow-up from an HSSE standpoint. 
2.4 Management Capacity  
2.4.1 Project Governance Structures (CCB, G4.1) 
The project is implemented and managed by the Indonesian company PT Annisa Surya Kencana (ASK), 
which is responsible for overseeing all operational, administrative, and strategic aspects of the project. 
PT ASK ensures that the project is executed in accordance with its objectives, regulatory requirements, 
and financial management standards. This includes supervising day-to-day activities, ensuring 
compliance with prevailing regulations, and maintaining accountability for project outcomes. 
To support technical aspects of the project, PT ASK collaborates with PT Karbon Natura Indonesia (KNI), 
which provides specialized expertise in areas such as carbon accounting, environmental monitoring, 
and sustainable land management. PT KNI offers scientific and technical guidance to enhance project 
effectiveness and ensure adherence to best practices in conservation and carbon offset initiatives. 
A simplified organizational structure outlining the roles and responsibilities of PT ASK is presented in 
Figure 13 below.

2.5.3 Human Rights (VCS, 3.19)  
The project is committed to recognizing, respecting, and protecting the rights of Indigenous Peoples, 
Local Communities, and customary rights holders by ensuring their full participation in decision-
making. The project acknowledges and supports the United Nations Declaration on the Rights of 
Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP) and ILO Convention 169 by implementing Free, Prior, and Informed 
Consent (FPIC) processes. The project also uses community meetings, allowing communities to actively 
share their voice in the development of programs and initiatives that directly address their needs. 
The project promotes inclusive, community-led decision-making, ensuring that all voices—especially 
those of women and marginalized groups—are heard and valued. It provides opportunities for skill 
development, leadership, and capacity building, empowering women to participate, contribute, and 
advocate for their needs and aspirations. Additionally, the project is committed to preserving local 
values and traditions, respecting human dignity, and improving the living standards of the most 
vulnerable households in its target communities. 
2.5.4 Indigenous Peoples and Cultural Heritage (VCS, 3.18, 3.19) 
The project is committed to safeguarding and promoting the cultural heritage of Indigenous Peoples 
and local communities living in surrounding project area, where the Dayak people form the dominant 
ethnic group. Recognizing the importance of traditional knowledge, governance systems, and cultural 
practices, the project integrates local customs into conservation and livelihood initiatives, ensuring that 
community-led approaches align with long-standing traditions. 
1. Supporting Traditional Livelihoods and Indigenous Knowledge 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
69 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The project promotes sustainable agroforestry by incorporating Dayak traditional ecological knowledge, 
which has been passed down for generations to maintain forest health. 
Traditional rotational farming (ladang shifting agriculture) is acknowledged and adapted to align with 
sustainable land management, ensuring food security while preventing deforestation. 
2. Strengthening Traditional Governance and Decision-Making 
The project is in coordination with customary Dayak leadership, traditional leaders (Temenggung), and 
village elders to support the development of community-based programs in the villages. 
3. Preserving Sacred Sites and Indigenous Spiritual Practices 
Sacred forests and culturally significant landscapes or buildings are mapped and protected from land 
conversion and encroachment, maintaining areas vital for rituals, ancestral worship, and traditional 
medicine. 
2.5.5 Statutory and Customary Property Rights (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
2.5.5.1 Statutory Rights 
ASK obtained a license based on the Decree of the Minister of Environmental and Forestry 
SK.965/MENLHK/SETJEN/HPL.0 /10/2021, granting a Business Permit for the Utilization of Timber 
Forest Products in Natural Forest over a production forest area of 33,605 hectares in Kapuas Hulu 
District, West Kalimantan Province. 
For the AFOLU project, a portion of the concession area, totaling 28,605 hectares, constitutes the 
Project Area. The project area has been determined due to its significant environmental value, 
predominantly composed of natural forests and peatlands. Originally, ASK's license permitted selective 
logging in the remaining forested area and the establishment of timber plantations. Under its new 
management, ASK transitioned its forest utilization through selective logging and timber plantation to 
conservation of forest and peat. 
The company has obtained approval from The Ministry of Environment and Forestry to apply Multiple 
Forestry Business, which includes permissions to utilize the license area for environmental services 
(including carbon credits).  
In the project area, it has been confirmed that there are no third-party rights legally recognized by 
applicable laws. There are no legal demands that can burden and hinder the project activities to be 
carried out because legally the Siluk project has obtained forest management rights through a forest 
utilization business permit (PBPH) based on SK.965/ MENLHK/ SETJEN/ HPL.0/10/2021 dated 
October 25, 2021. 
2.5.5.2 Customary Rights 
Based on the social baseline assessment, it was identified that some communities reside within the 
company's concession permit area but outside the designated project area. These communities are 
generally located in non-forested or non-agricultural areas. Through participatory mapping processes, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
70 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
the project has recognized the close ties and dependencies of local communities and Indigenous 
People (IPs) on both the project area and project zone.  
While the project is not in the position to formally recognize customary rights, it is committed to 
upholding the sustainability of Indigenous Peoples’s rights, as long as they do not conflict with 
applicable laws. To this end, the project will maintain continuous consultation and collaboration with 
local communities to ensure they have meaningful opportunities to participate and benefit from the 
project activities.   
The project will be implemented with full commitment to recognizing and respecting the rights of 
communities and stakeholders directly connected to the project area, including customary rights where 
they exist. It will promote inclusive participation and create opportunities for collaboration with 
communities and stakeholders in promoting conservation and environment-based initiatives.  
The project respects customary rights and commits to pursue mutually beneficial collaboration and 
consult with local communities to ensure that opportunities for communities to benefit from project 
activities are secured. 
2.5.6 Recognition of Property Rights (VCS, 3.7, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1)

2.5.7 Free, Prior and Informed Consent (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G5.2) 
Free, Prior and Informed Consent (FPIC) is a reference for development activities and utilization of 
natural resources that will impact the community and certain communities. In the SPCP, there is no 
land, building, or site owned by individuals, government and indigenous communities that are directly 
or indirectly affected. The project has implemented the FPIC principle in all its activities in 14 villages in 
the concession area, where the Village Government, community and indigenous communities are given 
the right to accept or reject the project to be implemented. 
Currently, there are eight Villages that have given their approval by signing the FPIC MOU, Sibau Hulu 
Village, Tanjung Lasa, Nanga Palin, Keliling Semulung, Pala Pintas, Tanjung Beruang, Jangkang and 
Nanga Lauk. Six other villages: Nanga Awin Village, Kirin Nangka, Seluan, Sungai Uluk Palin, Nanga 
Nyabau and Benua Tengah are still in the process of implementing FPIC which is targeted in 2025. 
Eight villages that have given their consent and signed the FPIC MOU have been in collaboration with 
the project to implement community empowerment programs such as business development in the 
fields of corn farming, laying hens and broilers. In addition, in the eight villages, beneficiary community 
groups have been formed, capacity building in the form of group institutional strengthening training, 
corn cultivation training, laying hen and broiler training, and training in non-burning agricultural 
cultivation. Routine assistance for groups is also provided by the project through accompanying 
facilitators in each village. The 6 (six) remaining villages that have not yet signed an MOU have 
understood the nature of the project, however there is a fraction in the village that applies conditions in 
which the project has not yet been able to meet or deliver.   

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
71 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Description of process 
for obtaining consent 
During the first operational quarter, PT Annisa Surya Kencana deployed 
a small team to identify and engage prominent district and regency 
(kecamatan) figures. This foundational effort aimed to introduce the 
company’s presence and mission while gathering authentic feedback. 
A newly formed Community Development Team (initially termed 
"community organizers") followed up by distributing engagement 
documents to local stakeholders. These materials outlined the project’s 
short- and long-term plans, emphasizing that—unlike typical PBPH 
(Perizinan Berusaha Pemanfaatan Hutan) operations—SPCP would not 
involve logging. 
Two regency-level kick-off meetings were held in July 2022, attended by 
village representatives and FORKOPIMDA (local authorities). Subsequent 
village meetings preceded the social baseline assessment, which ran 
from May to December 2023. The assessment aimed to collect direct 
feedback from communities on their expectations for a mutually 
beneficial partnership with the project. 
 
Findings were shared with respective villages to inform the drafting of 
FPIC (Free, Prior, and Informed Consent) terms and agreements. These 
agreements now serve as the foundation for designing community-
enterprise programs, ensuring tangible benefits in exchange for local 
support. 
 
SPCP prioritizes clarity, ensuring the village members fully grasps the 
program’s scope—including its potential social, economic, cultural, and 
environmental impacts. Detailed explanations are provided to empower 
the village members to make informed decisions about their 
involvement. 
Crucially, the program will only move forward after the village formally 
grants its approval. This consent-based approach underscores the 
project’s commitment to ethical, community-driven development.  
Outcome of FPIC process 
Following the Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC) process, both 
parties formalize a Community Enterprise Development Agreement. This 
contract outlines: 
▪ Specific program activities, 
▪ Responsibilities of each party, 
▪ Benefits for the village, and 
▪ Monitoring mechanisms to ensure accountability. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
72 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
▪ Both parties commit to fostering a respectful, sustainable, and 
equitable partnership. This includes safeguarding shared 
infrastructure and resources while upholding mutual interests 
throughout the program’s implementation. 
The SPCP, within its capacity and aligned with project needs, will provide 
financial and capital support to facilitate the program’s success—without 
replacing the village’s own contributions and active participation. 
 
2.5.8 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (VCS, 3.18, 3.19;) 
This project does not impact property rights (see Section 2.5.9 below) however, it does adopt a policy of 
benefit sharing for affected stakeholders and local communities living in proximity to the project. The 
project’s approach to benefit sharing is described in more detail in Section 4.5.6. 
2.5.9 Property Rights Protection (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.3) 
SPCP will not involve any voluntary or involuntary removal or relocation of individuals, households, or 
communities. The project activities also do not interfere with or prohibit community livelihood activities 
such as fishing, honey and resin collectors in the concession area, as long as these activities do not 
violate the law. Sacred sites and traditional rituals located and carried out in the concession area are 
recognized and respected as part of local cultural wisdom. The project is committed to providing 
support for the preservation of culture carried out by the community in 14 villages in the concession 
area. 
2.5.10 Illegal Activity Identification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G5.4) 
Based on the results of the identification that has been carried out, there are several small-scale illegal 
activities taking place in the project area. Illegal activities such as illegal logging and hunting still often 
occur which can have a negative impact on forest sustainability and biodiversity. Efforts to overcome 
this are carried out by conducting routine patrols and building guard posts that will be placed at the 
main access locations in the project area. Routine patrols are not only carried out by the company 
individually but also involve communities in the villages as part of the collaboration of forest security 
activities. Later, a forest security unit will be formed in each village that will collaborate with the 
company for various forest security activities. In addition, the development of community economic 
activities to find alternative income that is no longer oriented towards logging and hunting. 
2.5.11 Ongoing Disputes (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.5) 
SPCP has recorded a potential dispute from an article published on equatoronline.id on 25 March 2024 
along with related posts on social media (Instagram and Facebook) regarding the removal of boundary 
markers in Tanjung Lasa by a village member.  March 26, 2024, to address the situation, the project’s 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
73 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
management assigned an internal team to carry out verification and fact findings.  This process 
occurred until 4 April 2024 with the following findings from various parties:  
Boundary Demarcation Consultant:  
The boundary demarcation is conducted by a consultant who is contracted by the project. The 
consultant stated that the boundary survey has official permits from the Ministry of Environment and 
Forestry (KLHK) and the Pemdes (Village head). The survey team included village representatives, 
community leaders, and local residents.  Miscommunication about the boundary survey triggered 
protests and the removal of markers.  Of the installed markers in Tanjung Lasa, three were removed by 
a village member. The consultant had informed the villagers that the survey’s purpose is not to seize 
land but to map PT ASK’s concession boundaries and identify community-managed areas.    
Information from the Village Government (Pemdes):    
- The Pemdes will facilitate a socialization activity on boundary demarcation once the survey is 
completed and will invite community members, especially those managing land within the 
concession area, as a priority.    
- Some residents with land near the work area began visiting their fields to check if markers had 
been installed.    
- The village head (Kades) will contact the consultant for follow-up on the boundary survey.    
Information from Putussibau Police Resort (Polres):    
- Recommended involving “bhabinkamtibmas” (community policing officer) in village activities.    
- The police support company or institutional activities as long as they comply with the law.    
Statement from Temenggung Banua Sio (Dayak’s leader):    
- Received reports from Tanjung Lasa and Sibau Hulu residents about marker installation without 
prior notice.    
- Advised dialogue to resolve the issue and offered to facilitate a meeting.    
- Emphasized the need for pre-survey socialization to the broader community, not just village 
officials.    
- Recommended a closed meeting between PT ASK, community representatives, Pemdes, and 
traditional leaders to address Tanjung Lasa’s concerns.    
Additional Community Perspectives:    
- A customary leader shared that before the news viral, the village member who remove the poles, 
had informed him about marker installations near the Pangkaran reservoir. He noted that there is 
an opposition group in the village which is led by the BPD chairman and a small group whose land 
was marked. He also added that the BPD chairman had tried to collect signatures for a petition.    

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
74 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
- Another prominent village member added that most supporters of the BPD chairman’s protest 
were his extended family in RT III (53 people), while only three in RT II joined. RT is a sub 
administration under the village.  This prominent village member suggested the project to exclude 
the community group who protested and remove the pole markers. 
- Community leaders, traditional figures, and the Pemdes pledged continued support for PT ASK’s 
programs if they benefit Tanjung Lasa.    
Conclusion for this dispute:  
Marker installation has been temporarily halted to mitigate any adverse social risk.  
To address any potential disputes, ASK has developed a grievance mechanism that allows community 
from the 14 villages or other stakeholders to submit concerns or claims, which are then assessed and 
responded properly. This includes open dialogue with concerned parties, documentation of grievances, 
investigation, and negotiated resolution.  
The project is committed to ensuring that no activities are undertaken that could prejudice the outcome 
of any unresolved disputes related to the project.

2.5.13 Double Counting and Participation under Other GHG Programs (VCS, 3.23; CCB 
G5.9) 
2.5.13.1 No Double Issuance 
Is the project receiving or seeking credit for reductions and removals from a project activity under 
another GHG program, or any other form of community, social, or biodiversity unit or credit? 
  ☐   Yes    ☒   No 
No, the project is not receiving or applying for credits related to greenhouse gas (GHG) reductions or 
removals under any other GHG program. The project is also not pursuing any form of recognition or 
credits, including those related to community, social, or biodiversity units. 
2.5.13.2 Registration in Other GHG Programs 
Has the project registered under any other GHG programs?  
  ☐   Yes    ☒   No 
Is the project active under the other program? 
  ☐   Yes    ☒   No 
SPCP is not currently registered with, nor is it seeking registration under, other GHG programs. However, 
as stipulated in Presidential Decree 98/2021 and the Ministry of Forestry Decrees No. 71/2017 and 
No. 7/2023, all carbon projects within the country are required to register in the Indonesian Registry 
System (SRN) and comply with applicable regulations regarding the Economic Valuation of Carbon 
(NEK-Nilai Ekonomi Karbon). 
Registration in the SRN involves opening a registry account, being officially listed, submitting a DRAM 
(Project Design Document or equivalent), undergoing validation, and having the emission reductions 
independently verified. In compliance with these regulations, SPCP intends to open a registry account 
and be listed in the SRN system. However, it does not plan to proceed further with the validation and 
verification steps. 
The regulation also mentions the possibility of a Mutual Recognition Agreement (MRA) between SRN 
and other carbon standards, including Verra’s VCS. As of the development of this document, 
discussions are still ongoing, with the government showing positive support for the MRA to accelerate 
carbon project development in Indonesia. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
76 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The project maintains that participation in the voluntary carbon market can support the achievement of 
Indonesia’s Nationally Determined Contributions (NDCs) without resulting in double counting or double 
claiming of Voluntary Carbon Units (VCUs) or carbon credits. 
2.5.13.3 Projects Rejected by Other GHG Programs 
Has the project been rejected by any other GHG programs? 
  ☐   Yes    ☒   No 
2.5.14 Double Claiming, Other Forms of Credit, and Scope 3 Emissions (VCS, 3.24) 
2.5.14.1 No Double Claiming with Emissions Trading Programs or Binding Emission Limits 
Are project reductions and removals or project activities also included in an emissions trading program 
or binding emission limit? See the VCS Program Definitions for definitions of emissions trading program 
and binding emission limit. 
☐  Yes   ☒  No 
The GHG emission reductions from SPCP will not be included in any other emissions trading program or 
subject to binding emission limits. SPCP is committed to avoiding double counting emissions. 
The project aims to generate Voluntary Carbon Units (VCUs) for retirement by international parties that 
are not obliged to contribute to other countries’ Nationally Determined Contributions (NDCs). This 
approach ensures that the Indonesian government is not required to make Corresponding Adjustments 
in its GHG reduction reports against the country's NDC targets. Consequently, the carbon finance and 
implementation support provided by the project will contribute to Indonesia’s NDC goals and align with 
the objectives of the Paris Agreement. 
All credits generated by the project will be reported to Indonesian authorities in accordance with the 
Minister of Environment and Forestry Regulation P71/MENLHK/SETJEN/KUM1/12/2017, Presidential 
Decree No. 98/2021 on Economic Valuation of Carbon, Ministry of Environment and Forestry Decree 
No. 21/2022 on the governance of Economic Valuation of Carbon, and any relevant future regulations. 
2.5.14.2 No Double Claiming with Other Forms of Environmental Credit  
Has the project activity sought, received, or is planned to receive credit from another GHG-related 
environmental credit system? See the VCS Program Definitions for definitions of GHG-related 
environmental credit system. 
☐  Yes   ☒  No

2.6 Additional Information Relevant to the Project  
2.6.1 Leakage Management (VCS, 3.11, 3.15) 
Activity-shifting leakage is deemed negligible (zero) as the agents of deforestation and degradation do 
not have access and control to operate in other areas beyond the SPCP project area. Ecological leakage 
is also considered negligible, as the project activities are not expected to cause significant ecological 
displacement. Nonetheless, the project will continue to monitor for any potential ecological impacts 
beyond the project area.  Market leakage (LK-ME) is monitored and reported in accordance with the 
applicable methodology. Any potential impacts on supply and demand resulting from the project 
interventions will be assessed and transparently documented through periodic monitoring reports. 
See further in section 3.2.3. 
2.6.2 Further Information  
No additional information is required. All relevant details that may impact the project's eligibility, the 
greenhouse gas (GHG) emission reductions or carbon dioxide removals, as well as the quantification of 
those reductions or removals, have been comprehensively addressed in the relevant sections of this 
document.

3.1.3 Project Boundary (VCS, 3.12) 
3.1.3.1 Spatial Boundary 
Section 2.1.16 provides the detailed geographic location of the project, including administrative 
boundaries, spatial extent, and precise geographic coordinates.  
In accordance with VCS guidance, the spatial boundary is defined as the geographic area 
encompassing all land where project activities are implemented and where GHG emission reductions or 
removals occur. For the SPCP, applied this spatial boundary includes all areas where emissions would 
have occurred in the absence of the project, including areas of planned deforestation (APD), planned 
degradation (APDeg), planned degradation with strip planting (APDeg_SP), and wetlands restoration 
and conservation (WRC), as illustrated in Figure 14 and Table 3 below.

considered negligible. Therefore, no adjustments to the project boundary are deemed necessary.

3.1.4 Baseline Scenario (VCS, 3.13) 
In the absence of project intervention, the project area is expected to be logged and converted into 
industrial plantations, in line with the plans outlined in the technical proposal and concession letter. As 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
89 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
a result, the peatland ecosystem would be significantly disturbed due to infrastructure development 
required to support logging and land conversion operations. 
Following a systematic and step-by-step approach using VT0001 (Tool for the Demonstration and 
Assessment of Additionality in VCS AFOLU Project Activities), the baseline scenario has been 
determined as avoiding planned deforestation and degradation, and conserving and restoring the 
peatland ecosystem. 
A detailed explanation of the baseline scenario selection is provided in Section 3.1.5.  
3.1.5 Additionality (VCS, 3.14) 
The comprehensive and step-by-step process for demonstrating the additionality of SPCP is outlined in 
section 3.1.5.1 and 3.1.5.2 below. 
3.1.5.1 Regulatory Surplus (VCS, 3.14) 
Is the project located in an UNFCCC Annex 1 or Non-Annex 1 country? 
☐   Annex 1 country  ☒   Non-Annex 1 country 
Are the project activities mandated by any law, statute, or other regulatory framework? 
☐  Yes   ☒  No 
If the project is located inside a Non-Annex 1 country and the project activities are mandated by 
a law, statute, or other regulatory framework, are such laws, statutes, or regulatory frameworks 
systematically enforced?  
☐  Yes   ☒  No 
SPCP is implemented in a country classified as a non-annex I Party under the United Nations 
Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC). The project activities undertaken by SPCP are 
entirely voluntary and are not subject to any legal mandates, statutes, or regulatory frameworks. 
3.1.5.2 Additionality Methods (VCS, 3.14) 
The baseline scenario for the SPCP project was established using VT0001: Tool for the Demonstration 
and Assessment of Additionality in VCS AFOLU Project Activities. The determination of the baseline 
scenario followed a systematic and step-by-step approach as outlined in detail below. 
Step 1: Identification of alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU project activity 
In this step, alternative land use scenarios that represent plausible baseline scenarios have been 
determined following the sub-steps: 
Sub-step 1a: Identify credible alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU project activity

                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

Scenarios Land use options 
Selective logging 
(status quo) 
Continuation of the pre-project land use (partial). A substantial portion of 
Kalimantan’s peat and dryland forests, including the project area, has been 
managed by private companies for timber extraction since the 1970s. In 
accordance with prevailing regulations, these operations follow a selective 
logging approach, harvesting only trees that exceed a specified diameter 
threshold. This system is intended to minimize environmental impact and 
promote sustainable forest management. The practices continue at present with 
legacy concession granted extension for its operation for another 90 years. See 
timber concession distribution in West Kalimantan in Figure 15. 
Industrial timber 
plantation (status 
quo) 
Continuation of the pre-project land use (partial). This type of land use is 
widespread across Kalimantan, including the Kapuas Hulu district. Since the 
early 2000s, plantations have expanded significantly, thriving on both mineral 
soils and peatlands. However, plantations established in peatland areas often 
involve significant environmental modifications, primarily through the 
construction of canals to lower the water level. Secondary forest and non-forest 
area will be subjected to Industrial Timber Plantation. See industrial timber 
plantation distribution in West Kalimantan in Figure 15. 
Commercial oil 
palm plantation 
Oil palm plantations are widely commercially established across both dryland 
and peatland areas in Kalimantan. However, under government regulations, their 
development is legally restricted to the areas for other purposes (Areal 
Peruntukan Lain), not permitted within forest area (Kawasan Hutan). If oil palm 
plantation were to exist in a Forest Area it would be under illegal/conflicting 
presence or in the process of conversion to Other Purposes Area. See oil palm 
distribution in West Kalimantan in Figure 15.

Scenarios Land use options 
Small-holder 
agriculture/ 
plantation 
This land use is widespread throughout West Kalimantan. Most plantations 
operated by smallholders in West Kalimantan primarily cultivate oil palm (Elaeis 
oleifera), rubber trees (Hevea brasiliensis), and kratom (Mitragyna speciosa). 
Such agricultural practices are more commonly found in non-peat areas as 
agriculture on peat demands higher cost and resources. It tends to be 
concentrated near key access points, such as roads, rivers, and settlement, 
where transportation and the logistic deployment are more feasible. 
Mining Mineral deposits can occasionally be found along rivers, as well as in certain 
sandy ridges, heath forests, and mineral-rich soil areas on the edges of 
peatlands. In Kalimantan, these resources have long attracted both legal and 
illegal miners. Mining activities in these areas are typically short-term and 
nomadic, with miners relocating once deposits are depleted. Some gold mining 
activities are currently in operation in Kapuas Hulu district.  
Protected forest 
(project) 
Proposed project activity on the land within the project boundary performed 
without being registered as the VCS AFOLU project. Designating protected forests 
is one option for areas with unique characteristics, critical ecosystem functions, 
high ecological value, and key biodiversity habitats. Such areas can be 
designated as national parks or other protected zones, but this requires a formal 
process involving regulatory approvals. Once designated, protected status 
effectively prevents commercial land use, such as timber harvesting or 
conversion into plantations. However, legal protection does not guarantee the 
prevention of illegal activities, such as illegal logging and land encroachment, 
which remain ongoing challenges  
Unprotected 
forest 
Unprotected areas do exist, often remaining in a state of status quo condition, 
making them vulnerable to various disturbances. These areas are frequently 
targeted for illegal activities such as logging, land encroachment, and 
unsanctioned agricultural activities. Since 2011, some of these areas meeting 
certain criteria (deep peat and primary forest) have been included under a 
moratorium on new licensing, effectively restricting legal commercial 
exploitation. However, in practice, enforcement remains weak, providing minimal 
protection against illegal activities. As a result, these landscapes continue to 
face significant threats from deforestation, habitat destruction, and 
unsustainable land use practices.

Following this assessment, it was found that four scenarios — industrial tree plantations, selective 
logging, protected forest and unprotected forest — adhere to all applicable legal and regulatory 
frameworks. These activities operate within the established legal boundaries and align with government 
policies on land use and resource management.  
In contrast, commercial oil palm cultivation does not meet legal requirements, as this activity is not 
officially permitted within the project area. Despite their illegality, smallholder agriculture are assumed 
to continue to exist due to weak regulatory enforcement and governance challenges. Because they 
remain prevalent in the region, they have been temporarily retained for further consideration to ensure 
a realistic and contextually relevant analysis.

Based on step 1b, the baseline scenario is defined as the continuation of pre-project land use, with

2021.

retained for further evaluation in the barrier analysis as outlined in Step 3 (Barrier analysis). While 
these activities are not legally permitted, they remain prevalent in the region due to weak law 
enforcement and regulatory gaps. 
Step 2. Investment analysis 
This step assesses whether the proposed project activity, without revenue from GHG credit sales, is 
economically or financially less attractive than at least one alternative land use scenario. As prescribed 
by the tool, investment analysis was conducted alongside the Barrier Analysis (Step 3) to ensure a 
comprehensive assessment, even though the tool suggests them as alternative approaches. 
Sub-step 2a. Determine appropriate analysis method 
Per VT0001, as the VCS AFOLU project generates no financial or economic benefits other than VCS 
related income, the simple cost analysis approach was selected (Option I). 
Sub-step 2b: Apply simple cost analysis (Option 1) 
As demonstrated by project budget and financial forecasts made available to the auditors (see Sections 
2.1.22 and 2.4.5 and NPRR supporting evidence), SPCP has no source of revenue other than VCS 
related income. In comparison, the baseline activity is predicted to be a profitable venture, as 
demonstrated by documentation made available as part of the NPV comparison presented in support of 
the Non-Permanence Risk Report (Internal Risk/Opportunity Cost). As per the evidence provided, it is 
concluded that the proposed VCS AFOLU project produces no financial benefits other than VCS related 
income. Per VT0001 this permits the assessment to proceed to Step 4 (Common practice analysis), 
however Step 3 Barrier analysis is also included for completeness. 
Step 3. Barrier analysis 
As previously elaborated in Step 1b, four scenarios were included in the barrier analysis: the pre-project 
land use of industrial tree plantations and selective logging (established as the baseline scenario), 
along with smallholder agriculture and smallholder plantation.

Smallholder 
agriculture/plantation 
The primary barriers to this scenario are legal and land tenure constraints. 
It could only occur if the existing concession license were disregarded.  
Smallholder agriculture in the Project Area faces significant investment and 
technical challenges. Productive cultivation is not viable in peatland, as it 
requires heavy fertilization and extensive drainage, both of which demand 
substantial capital and pose serious environmental risks. In nearby dryland 
areas, smallholder farming is typically carried out using conventional 
methods on small, scattered plots, leading to low productivity. 
Given these constraints (legal, tenure, high cost and low productivity), 
smallholder agriculture/ plantation in the project area considered to be not 
feasible. 
Protected Forest 
(project) 
There are major financial barriers to implementing forest protection and 
restoration activities without carbon finance. Managing a site of this scale 
over the long term requires stable, high-level funding that far exceeds what 
philanthropic donations can typically provide, especially since such funding 
is rarely sustained over time. 
Institutional barriers also exist. The project area falls within the production 
forest estate and is legally designated to generate revenue for the state. As 
such, securing long-term management rights depends on presenting a 
credible business plan—this is a core requirement under the licensing 
conditions and essential to gaining government support.

the alternative land use scenarios (except the proposed project activity)

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The analysis in Sub-step 3b confirms that smallholder agriculture/plantation and protected forest (the 
project activity in the absence of carbon finance) are unfeasible due to multiple barriers. As a result, 
the baseline scenario remains the continuation of industrial tree plantations and selective logging.  
Step 4. Common practice analysis 
The project area consists of peat swamp forest ecosystems located within the area legally designated 
for production use. Under prevailing regulatory and land-use frameworks in Indonesia, such areas are 
expected to be developed for commercial purposes, particularly for logging activity and the 
establishment of industrial timber plantations using fast-growing species. This pattern of peatland 
utilization is consistent with regional land-use trends and is widely practiced across Sumatra and 
Kalimantan, where large areas of peat swamp forest have already been cleared and drained to support 
pulpwood or oil palm operations. 
Outside of areas formally designated as protected (e.g., conservation forests or national parks), there 
are very few examples of conservation being implemented within production zones—especially on 
peatlands—without the support of carbon finance or philanthropic intervention. The opportunity cost of 
conserving high-value peatland areas is high, and without alternative revenue streams, license holders 
have no economic incentive to forego conversion. In rare cases where conservation has occurred, it has 
typically involved small-scale donor-funded projects or one-off corporate offsets that are not replicable 
under current policy and market conditions. 
Moreover, local governments often expect production areas to contribute to regional revenue targets, 
and therefore tend to discourage conservation that does not align with licensed productive use. As 
such, the long-term protection of peat swamp forests within the project area is not consistent with 
business-as-usual practices and would not be implemented in the absence of targeted support through 
carbon finance. 
Given this context, the proposed REDD+ activity, avoiding planned deforestation-degradation and 
conserving-restoring peatlands ecosystem represents a clear deviation from common practice in the 
region. The project therefore meets the criteria for additionality under the Verra REDD+ Methodology 
Framework (VM0007), supported by the application of the BL-PL module and a with-project scenario 
(WRC) that would not occur without intervention. 
3.1.5.2.1 Detail description of the baseline management  
The baseline management plan is established based on two official documents: the Technical Proposal 
submitted by PT ASK in 2019 and the concession license issued by the Ministry of Environment and 
Forestry (MoEF) in 2021 (Figure 16). These documents serve as the foundation for defining land use 
without project intervention within the project area. Copies are available to auditors upon request. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
98 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 16.  Technical proposal of PT ASK 
The Technical Proposal presents a detailed zonation that designates the area into 1) protected areas, 
2) non-productive areas, and 3) productive areas. Concession holders are required to set aside 
protected areas based on ecological, hydrological, and biodiversity criteria. These include riparian 
zones, steep slope areas, and High Conservation Value (HCV) areas. Based on spatial analysis, 
protected area is estimated to be 1,285.68 Ha (5% of project area). Non-productive areas include 
degraded land, areas unsuitable for forest utilization, Permanent Sampling Plot (PSP), and germplasm 
area. Based on spatial analysis, non-productive areas are estimated to be 677.07 Ha (2% of project 
area).  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
99 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 17. Non-productive and protected areas designated by technical proposal 
As for the productive area, this refers to the area where regulated harvesting or forest utilization will 
take place. As outlined in the Technical Proposal, the productive area is the area designated for three 
primary silviculture system: selective logging (TPTI-Tebang Pilih Tanam Indonesia), selective logging and 
Strip line planting (TPTJ-Tebang Pilih Tanam Jalur), and industrial timber plantations (THPB-Tebang 
Habis Permudaan Buatan), each with specific spatial and temporal allocations. Selective logging is 
planned within designated zones, following a rotational harvesting schedule, while industrial timber 
plantations are established in other areas for commercial timber production. The Selective Logging and 
Strip Planting System (TPTJ) is applied within designated zones which involve selectively harvesting 
mature trees while replanting in designated strips to promote the growth. Based on spatial analysis, 
productive areas are estimated to be 26,542.93 Ha (93% of project area). In addition to this 
designation, the project area includes 17,003.09 hectares of peatland located within the TPTI and 
THPB areas, subjected to infrastructure development to support logging activities and forest 
conversion. 
This designation as explained earlier serves as the basis for determining project activity category and 
proceeding further with GHG emission calculation to be applied in SPCP as outlined below: 
• REDD-APDeg (Reducing Emissions from Forest Degradation in areas designated for selective 
logging or TPTI) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
100 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• REDD-APDeg_SP (Reducing Emissions from Forest Degradation in Areas Designated Selective 
Logging and Strip planting or TPTJ),  
• REDD-APD (Reducing Emissions from Deforestation in the areas designated for Industrial 
Timber Plantation establishment or THPB).  
• WRC (Reducing Emissions from Wetlands Rewetting and Conservation in peatlands areas 
impacted by baseline logging operation and forest conversion) 
This distinction is crucial for accurately estimating greenhouse gas emissions within each land use 
category under the baseline scenario.

3.1.5.2.5 Reducing Emissions from Wetlands Rewetting and Conservation (WRC) in peatlands 
areas impacted by baseline logging operation and forest conversion 
3.1.5.2.5.1 Land and water management under baseline scenario 
Referring to Technical Proposal Document (Dokumen Rencana Teknis) there would be two types of 
relevant baseline land and water management which would affect GHG from peatland degradation in 
the absence of SPCP, namely THPB and TPTI (corresponding to APD and APDeg respectively). Tree cover 
removals in THPB/APD area would increase water loss in areas designated for deforestation (in planting 
blocks, roads and canals clearances) due to increased evapotranspiration and/or overland flow (more 
details are given in Section C Annex 3). Drainage canals would be constructed on THPB/APD blocks to 
maintain annual average water tables at least 80 centimeters depth below soil surface which is 
required for growing dryland species on Siluk peatland. Drainage spacing was estimated at ca. 100-
300 meters interval with ca. 2-3 meters canal width and 3 meters depth.  
Roads and railroads would be laid out through swathes of forest opening that, when constructed on 
peatlands, usually require some degree of stabilization for transportation security and reliability. 
Stabilization is usually done by means of compaction or vibration using heavy equipment (on mineral 
soils or shallow peat soils) and consolidation by using roadside drainage. Road surfaces are usually 
layered with compacted earth piles, timber piles or both, while railroads usually with timber piles. Map 
of baseline land and water management is given in Figure 24 below, 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
109 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 24. Baseline water management map
10
 
Compaction and consolidation increase bulk density of peat soil and, together with peat decomposition, 
cause land subsidence. In the baseline scenario, as land elevations drop the open swathes would 
become the sink for lateral water flows from adjacent land in wet seasons. Here the roadside drainage 
would play double roles: consolidating peat soil for the roads and intercepting lateral flows before 
reaching the road body then discharging the excess water to nearby outlets. 
Deforestation, roads and plantation canals in the baseline scenario would increase human access to 
the locations and their susceptibility to fire. Baseline agent would implement fire prevention practice, 
but as evidenced by historical hot spot data throughout similar land use, uncontrolled fire events would 
be inevitable, since many human-induced factors (such as tendency and common practice of fire use in 
the vicinity area) are beyond the baseline agent’s control. Each year, every new road or canal segment 
or deforested patch would increase fire probability in the corresponding location and its surroundings. 
Forest patches that would have been affected by fire were considered as deforested patch and 
consequently would also increase their fire probabilities. 
3.1.5.2.5.2 Land and water management under project scenario 
Hydrological integrity of SPC is reflected by two main indicators: forest cover and the presence-absence 
of artificial drainage. Most of the area, particularly on peatland, is still covered by good tree stands.

Drainage canals exist as the remains of roadside drainage constructed long ago (in 1980 - 1990) by a 
former logging company of the SPCP area. Total length of identified canals at present is estimated to be 
140,883 meters which are distributed into three segments in two main reaches: northern and middle 
reaches, as given in Figure 25. The middle reach canal encompasses two peat domes and crosses the 
Seluan river. 
All of the existing canals have not been maintained and have been subjected to gradual deposition over 
decades of abandonment. We estimated that initial canals were 3 to 5 meters wide and about 2 - 3 
meters deep. Today the dimensions have been reduced to about 3.2 meters in width and 0.56 meters 
in depth based on LIDAR data and preliminary surveys conducted in 2024/2025 (see also Table 16). 
General canal slope ranges from 0.12% to 0.9% (see also Figure 26).

Gradual deposition (or refilling) of canals has helped natural ecosystem recovery by reducing, or 
completely stopping, canal flow and promoting vegetation regrowth. Preliminary surveys show that most 
of the canal segments being surveyed have been devoid of significant flow. We cannot conclude yet 
whether this condition is permanent or merely an intermittent/seasonal pattern. In our ex-ante project 
scenario calculation, we conservatively assumed the existence of canal flow throughout the year. 
In order to sustain the hydrological integrity of SPCP area, we are committed to protect existing forest 
stand, especially on peatland, facilitate natural vegetation recovery, and reduce unnatural water loss 
from the water balance. A rewetting program has been planned for 2025 (segment 1), 2026 (segment 
2) and 2027 (segment 3). 
Technical design of the rewetting will mimic ongoing natural deposition of the canals. In principle, we 
are accelerating canal-refilling by piling in, or blocking, the channels with organic debris at select 
locations. Exact canal blocking coordinates will be assessed later based on slope and the presence-
absence of- and the magnitude of flow. Organic source will be selected in sustainable way, such as from 
discarded shrubs, bushes and weed debris by farmers. Tweaks and treatments may be required to

3.1.6 Methodology Deviations (VCS, 3.20) 
SPCP did not identify any methodological deviations in the application of VM0007. The project activities 
and calculations have been carried out in full compliance with the requirements and procedures 
outlined in the methodology. 
3.2 Quantification of Estimated GHG Emission Reductions and Removals 
3.2.1 Baseline Emissions (VCS, 3.15) 
This section outlines the baseline emissions for each of the four spatially defined project GHG 
accounting strata: APDeg, APDeg_SP, APD, and WRC (see Section 3.1.3.1). It begins with a description 
of the method for quantifying carbon stocks in aboveground vegetation, which applies to both APDeg 
and APD areas. Next, it details the stratification of peat biomass, specifically relevant to WRC areas. 
The section also presents a comprehensive assessment of baseline fire impacts across the entire 
Project Area. Finally, baseline emissions for each spatially explicit stratum are analyzed, following the 
methodologies outlined in the corresponding modules. 
3.2.1.1 Tessellation of Above Ground Forest Biomass 
The SPCP is committed to producing high-integrity carbon credits to fund the conservation of a unique 
and biodiverse inland tropical peat swamp forest ecosystem. In order to demonstrate accurate GHG 
reductions benefits, SPCP used Light Detection and Ranging (LiDAR) technology to precisely estimate 
project and baseline scenario carbon stocks/fluxes in the form of “wall-to-wall”, or tessellated maps of 
our project area using “module VT0005 for estimating above-ground forest biomass using remote 
sensing”. The resulting carbon estimation maps are categorized into three classes: (a) a carbon 
prediction map for all trees with a diameter greater than 10 cm, (b) a biomass prediction map for trees 
with a diameter exceeding 30 cm, representing the merchantable volume for peat swamp forest, and 
(c) a biomass prediction map for trees with a diameter greater than 40 cm, representing the 
merchantable volume for dryland forest. 
In the following section, the project outlines the approach and provides details of data collection and 
processing, see Figure 27 for an overview. While VT0005 refers to aboveground live forest biomass 
(ALFB), we use the term AGB instead.  
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
115 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
 
Figure 27. Data collection and processing flow diagram 
3.2.1.1.1 Field Inventory 
Following VT0005, SPCP uses 45 permanent sample plots (PSPs, distinct from baseline PSPs discussed 
in 3.1.5.2)  for  measuring  above  ground  biomass.  Forest  inventory  data  collection  was  conducted  from 
January to April 2023. The procedure of the establishment of PSP and parameter measurement in AGB 
forest inventory can be found in Annex 1. The forest inventory was carried out across five different strata, 
including secondary peat swamp forest, secondary dryland forest, shrub swamp, dryland agriculture, and 
bare  land. These  strata  were  defined  in  a  pilot  stratification  using satellite  imagery (Landsat  8  OLI 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
116 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
acquisition  on  July  22,  2022,  Path/Row  119/059),  which  was  classified  using  visual interpretation 
methods, and was validated and approved by the Ministry of Environment and Forestry in 2022 as part 
of  the IHMB  and IKHB process (government  approved  for  the  stratification  and  estimation  of  forest 
production and carbon potential). A total of 45 square nested plots, each covering 0.25 ha (50 x 50 m), 
were distributed using a stratified systematic approach. The required number of plots per stratum were 
calculated  using  the  Winrock Plot  Calculator
11
,  with  AGB  averages  and  standard  deviations  from  the 
Indonesian   National   Forest   Reference   Level   (FREL)   for   Deforestation,   Forest   Degradation   and 
Enhancement of Forest Carbon Stock, and a target confidence level of 90%, and error level of 10%.   
Within each plot, all trees with ≥10 cm diameter at breast height (DBH) were tagged and the DBH was 
measured.  During  the  inventory,  both  local  and  scientific  species  names  were  identified,  and  general 
information regarding the condition of the plots was recorded. The full standard operating procedure is 
available in Annex 1.   
The trees are also marked with permanent plastic tags which are carefully nailed into each tree.  At each 
plot corner, the coordinates were measured using a Garmin GPS (reported accuracy – 10 m) and were 
marked using reflective targets (1 x 1 m) secured into the ground so that the exact plot location can be 
easily  recognized using  LiDAR  intensity  signal. The  project recorded taxonomic information,  taking  leaf 
samples wherever possible for all tree species.

3.2.1.1.2 Above-Ground Biomass (AGB) 
The project aimed to produce tessellated carbon and volume density maps. AGB was calculated at the 
plot level according to the allometric equations from Manuri et al. (2013) utilizing measured diameter 
at breast height. The allometric equation was identified as the most suitable model for estimating tree 
biomass and carbon stock of the project area because they were developed within the same ecosystem 
type and geographic region: secondary dryland forest and secondary peat swamp forest in Kapuas Hulu 
Regency, West Kalimantan. Furthermore, the equation uses DBH as the single primary variable, as tree 
height and wood density were excluded due to its high measurement error rate, and it contributes to 
significant uncertainties in biomass estimation. Plot-level carbon was calculated by multiplying AGB by 
0.47 (carbon fraction, IPCC). 
Dryland forest equation
