Peatland forest equation 
ln퐴퐺퐵=−1.7767+2.4551ln퐷 
 
3.2.1.1.3 Merchantable Volume (MV) 
Using the same PSP inventory data, merchantable volume was calculated at the plot level. The 
identified tree species were reclassified from the species to family level and subsequently categorized 
based on their species group and protected status. This follows the List of Groups and Types of Wood as 
outlined in the annex of the Ministry of Forestry Decree No. 163/Kpts-II/2003 concerning Grouping of 
Timber Species as the Basis for Imposition of Forestry Fees. The wood classification is divided into 
three main categories including a Meranti group (Commercial Group One), a Mixed Forest Wood 
(Commercial Group Two), and an Ornamental Group.  
The protection status of identified tree species is determined in accordance with the Regulation of the 
Ministry of Environment and Forestry No. P.20/MENLHK/SETJEN/KUM.1/6/2018 concerning Protected 
Plant and Wildlife Species. This regulation lists fifteen protected tree families, including 
Dipterocarpaceae, which predominantly constitutes the forested area within the PT ASK concession. 
However, none of the protected species within this family were found within the concession area. Based 
on the annex of the regulation, all 8.301 identified trees within the PT ASK concession area has been 
classified as non-protected species. 
In the calculation of timber merchantable volume, the diameter at breast height (DBH) limitation 
applied is 30 cm for wetland ecosystems and 40 cm for dryland ecosystems. These criteria align with 
the Regulation of the Ministry of Forestry No. P.65/MENHUT-II/2014 about Revision of Ministry of 
Forestry Regulation No. P.11/MENHUT-II/2009 concerning Silviculture System on Timber Forest Product 
Utilization License in Production Forest. According to this regulation, secondary peat swamp forests are 
managed under the Indonesia Selective Cutting and Planting System (TPTI), which implemented on a 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
118 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
30-year harvest rotation, with trees eligible for harvesting having a DBH of at least 30 cm. In secondary 
dryland forest, the applicable silviculture system is the Selective Cutting and Line Planting (TPTJ), with a 
rotation period of 25 years and a DBH requirement of at least 40 cm for harvesting.  
The calculation of merchantable volume is limited to trees classified under the Meranti Group 
(Commercial Group One), Mixed Forest Wood (Commercial Group Two), and Ornamental Wood 
(Commercial Group Three). This limitation is implemented as the calculation is intended to quantify 
emissions resulting from timber harvesting activities. Moreover, forest utilization business permit 
(PBPH) does not harvest protected tree species or those classified under the Ornamental Group. This 
practice aligns with the Regulation of the Ministry of Environment and Forestry No. 
P.20/MENLHK/SETJEN/ KUM.1/6/2018, which prohibits the exploitation of protected species.  
 
3.2.1.1.4 LiDAR data acquisition and processing 
LiDAR technology utilizes laser pulses to precisely measure the distance between the sensor and target 
objects. When this measurement is repeated hundreds of thousand times per second from a drone 
flying above the canopy, it is possible to accurately and precisely model the three-dimensional structure 
of the forest across the landscape (Figure 29). Forest structure correlates strongly and directly with AGB 
and forest carbon, especially compared to optical remote sensing methods, which actually measure 
light reflectance from different plant materials and tend to saturate at higher biomass levels. This 
method enhances the accuracy of carbon stock estimation and distribution, supporting robust forest 
monitoring and reporting. LiDAR can also be used to generate high quality digital terrain models (DTM), 
because the laser pulses penetrate through vegetation canopies to capture the ground surface 
coordinates. DTMs can be processed further to build a peat depth model by combining it with field peat 
depth sampling, thus enhancing the accuracy of hydrological modelling and carbon stock 
estimation in peat area (Section 3.2.1.2).  
 
Figure 29. Example of a digital surface model of an example high- and low-density forest area 
In September and October 2024, LiDAR data were collected in transects distributed across the entire 
site, covering approximately 30% of the total project area (11,882 ha in total). The project opted for this 
extensive and comprehensive transect network of remote sensing sampling units (RSSUs) to ensure 
that spatial autocorrelation would be adequately captured in the sampling design. This was calculated 
in a post-hoc analysis following generation of the tessellated biomass map and showed that our LiDAR 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
119 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
transects cover approximately 700 km across the entire site, significantly longer than the 1,206 m 
range of biomass spatial autocorrelation (r = 12 pixels) which was calculated using VT0005 Section 
5.1.2.1. Using these equations, and very conservative values for mean Aboveground Live Forest 
Biomass (AGB = 254 t d.m. ha
-1
), spatial correlation factor (ρ(d) = ρ(1) = 0.78), number of pixels per 
RSSU (m =  2(r) = 24), the recommended number of RSSUs of 24 pixels was 163, equating to a 
minimum survey area of 3,923 ha.  
The project used a DJI M350 Matrice multirotor drone and Zenmuse L2 LiDAR scanner (Figure 30) to 
collect point cloud and RGB ortho mosaic data. This sensor has a vertical/horizontal precision of 4-5 
cm, can record 5 pulse returns (giving better canopy penetration), and collects 240,000 points per 
second. The drone was flown at 180 m at 10 m s
-1
 forward ground speed, with terrain following enabled 
for all transects. We maintained 50% lateral overlap between out and return legs to maximize data 
quality and coverage. The lidar datasets were corrected using post-kinematic processing (using Badan 
Informasi Geospasial ground station corrections, station ID: CPUT). In total, the locations of 13 
independent control points were recorded using a GNSS receiver to assess geometric accuracy of the 
LiDAR data, resulting in a combined X, Y, Z RMSE of 0.09 m for the entire survey. A full survey report is 
available in Annex 4.   
 
Figure 30. Drone operations from a clearing near the project area forest 
The resulting LAS file was then noise filtered and classified using Terrasolid software. A digital terrain 
model was produced at 0.5 m resolution (for peat surface modelling), and used to normalize the point 
cloud using the LidR package in R.   
3.2.1.1.5 Canopy Height Model (CHM) production and tessellation 
Above-ground biomass can be predicted successfully from canopy height model (Figure 32). While there 
are two global CHMs available freely online: META (Mapping Ecosystem and Tree Architecture) from the 
World Resources Institute (WRI), and the dataset from Lang et al (2023) these models are not as 
accurate or precise as a CHM produced from airborne LiDAR (see Table 17 for references). However, as 
our LiDAR dataset is not wall-to-wall, the project leveraged our LiDAR dataset as a training and testing 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
120 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
response variable in a predictive Random Forest model, using these global CHMs among other wall-to-
wall satellite predictors to produce a corrected wall-to-wall CHM.  
Firstly, a 50 m CHM was produced using the LiDAR dataset alone for the transects where data were 
available. This was achieved by normalizing the point cloud (DTM subtracted from point elevations) at 
10 m resolution to account for microtopographic heterogeneity. After normalization, the CHM was 
produced at 50 m resolution (as per VT0005) using the lidR package in R. Secondly, a stack of wall-to-
wall predictors was prepared in Google Earth Engine (see Table 17), clipped to a 1 km buffer extending 
beyond the project boundary. Thirdly, a training and testing dataset was created by extracting CHM 
values for each pixel (n=44,086) as the response variable, with predictor pixel values as the 
explanatory variables. We created a predictive model with (70/30 split training/testing) in the random 
forest and caret packages in R. With such a large amount of data points, cross-validation was not 
necessary. The resulting wall-to-wall CHM is highly accurate RMSE= 2.6 m, R2= 0.87, bias= 0.01 m, 
(Figure 32), and was then used as the sole predictor in the parametric predictive model used to upscale 
AGB (Figure 36).

wall/tessellated Canopy Height Model. These processed datasets were projected to WGS84 UTM 51N, 
resampled to 50 m (minimum mapping unit) and clipped around a 1 km buff

Using this regression slope, we calculate error statistics which can be used in AGB uncertainty 
estimation. The error of the wall-to-wall CHM is RMSE = 2.6 m, which translated to AGB using the 
regression slope, equates to approximately 20 tC ha

 error. 
3.2.1.1.6 Above-ground biomass upscaling 
To develop a robust biomass estimation model, predictor variables were extracted at PSP locations. 
These predictors included spectral bands from Sentinel-2, backscatter values from PALSAR, and canopy 
height estimates derived from LiDAR and other remote sensing sources (Table 17).  A correlation 
analysis revealed that the tessellated CHM (previous section) was the best predictor of AGB and MV, 
and so was the only predictor used. Figure 31 and Figure 36 demonstrate the spatial relationship 
between canopy height and AGB, where the average canopy height within 50 m pixels predicts 
aboveground carbon.  
The project implemented a parametric biomass modeling approach consistent with VT0005, using a 
simple linear regression model to predict aboveground live forest biomass (AGB) from tessellated  
CHM variable. Given our limited AGB dataset from PSPs (45 locations), this restricted both the training 
and testing potential of a predictive model, even with cross-validation there is a risk of high variance 
error metrics due to the limited validation dataset. To maximize the potential of both our PSP data, and 
LiDAR-predicted CHM, we assessed the precision of our linear regression model  (Biomass ~ 
Predicted_CHM), using Monte Carlo cross-validation validation (Cross-Validation Visualized: A 
Narrative Guide to Advanced Methods),with 10,000 iterations and a 70/30 train/test split (far 
exceeding the VT0005 requirements of K ≥10 fold cross validation). For each iteration, RMSE, R², and 
Bias were recorded, and their means were summarized to produce robust model diagnostics. This 
approach was selected because it has greater sampling diversity (10,000 iterations of training and 
testing datasets used) than (e.g.) k-fold cross validation, leading to more stable error estimates and a 
better approximation of real-world variability.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
124 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 34. 3D point cloud of a high and low density forest area (as in Figure 33), coloured by above 
ground carbon density (50 m).  The areas with highest average canopy also have the highest carbon 
density (dark red) 
 
Figure 35. Point cloud cross section of the sample high and low density forest areas shown in Figure 32, 
coloured by aboveground carbon (50 m resolution) 
Following model validation, the final regression model was fit using the training dataset and applied to 
the wall-to-wall predictor raster to produce a continuous AGB (carbon) prediction map. This satisfies 
VT0005 requirements for generating a pixel-wise biomass surface using a consistent, interpretable 
parametric modelling framework.

3.2.1.1.7 Estimation of Aboveground Biomass and Uncertainty 
Following X-UNC, uncertainty in baseline carbon stock in eligible pools should be calculated by 
propagating errors across pools and sources of errors. For the carbon in aboveground biomass (C
AB-tree
), 
the sources of error for establishing AGB stock are as follows: a) field data collection errors, b) 
allometric model errors, c) LiDAR data collection errors, d) LiDAR data processing, e) errors outside of 
LiDAR predictions.  
Here the project describe how the project has made every effort to minimise uncertainty in its project 
design and standard operating procedures (SOPs).  
a) Asner et al (2013)
15
 state that “Measurement errors of trees in the field are generally thought to 
make a vanishingly small contribution at the plot level”. In general, manual measurements are 
considered the “reference” datasets against which remote or modelling assessments are 
compared. Therefore, these errors are considered minimal, and are accounted for in the AGB 
regression error estimate.   
 
15
 High-fidelity national carbon mapping for resource management and REDD+ | Carbon Balance and Management 
(Asner et al. 2013) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
128 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
b) The most appropriate allometric equations were selected because they were derived from 
destructive harvests conducted locally in both peatland and dryland forests of Kapuas Hulu 
Regency. Further the allometric models only use DBH, so errors in (e.g.) height or species 
identification are avoided. These errors are considered minimal, and are accounted for in the AGB 
regression error estimate.   
c) LiDAR data collection errors are deemed de minimis because it is a high precision survey 
technique, with an average XYZ error of 0.08 m compared to GNSS-derived ground control points 
(see survey report in Annex 4). Furthermore, using VT0005 we calculated that a minimum survey 
area of 3,933 ha was required, however we collected more than 3 times this area with LiDAR data 
(11,882 ha) systematically in transects across the entire site. We then used the LiDAR and satellite 
datasets to create a wall-to-wall canopy height model (56,222 ha) which is over 14 times greater 
than the minimum survey requirements. These errors are considered de minimis given high 
accuracy statistics and large sample size and wide distribution of the data.  
1. Further errors can be borne from misalignment of forest inventory PSP locations in the LiDAR 
datasets- whereby the canopy causes GPS errors and so the wrong field biomass values are 
assigned to the wrong canopy metrics in the LiDAR data. We avoided this misalignment through 
laying reflective targets at the corners of PSPs, and then identifying the corners of the plots in 
the LiDAR data by filtering by “intensity” (i.e. a metric of a target surface’s reflectivity, which is 
higher than vegetation or the forest floor).  Therefore, these errors have been reduced as far as 
possible.  
2. The quality of the CHM relies heavily on the production of a good quality digital terrain model 
(DTM). The underlying DTM derived from LiDAR was produced using Inverse Distance Weighting 
methods, then manually filtered, noise controlled and corrected by professional data analysts, 
resulting in a 0.5 m DTM suitable for hydrological modelling. These errors have been accounted 
for in the CHM prediction uncertainty.  
d) Errors arising from the creation of a wall-to-wall CHM were estimated using the LiDAR dataset to 
validate model performance. As these errors are not intrinsic, and propagate through to AGB 
calculations, these were included in the uncertainty calculations.  
To holistically capture uncertainty across the modelling chain, we implemented a nested Monte Carlo 
simulation (10,000 iterations) propagating errors from both stages: 
1. CHM prediction uncertainty: (±2.6 m RMSE) 
2. AGB regression uncertainty: (±40.99 tC ha
-1
) 
In each iteration, this involves the simulation of a site-wide CHM using the wall-to-wall CHM which is 
perturbed by random noise (each pixel could be perturbed by noise randomly drawn from a normal 
distribution with standard deviation = 2.6 m). This creates a perturbed CHM map (first stage of error), 
which is then used to create a site-wide AGB map by applying the regression coefficients from the 
predictive model. Secondly, this map is perturbed once again using random noise representing the AGB 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
129 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
regression uncertainty (again, random number drawn from the normal distribution with -0.77 tC ha
-1
 
bias and 40.99 tC ha
-1
 standard deviation). 
This simulation approach allowed for a realistic propagation of prediction uncertainty at both CHM 
prediction and AGB regression stages across the AOI and enabled estimation of a 95% confidence 
interval for total carbon (in tonnes C). As per the requirements in VT0005, the project generates 
uncertainty estimates in the form of percentages (95% CI), R code and model outputs are available to 
the VVB auditors.

3.2.1.2 Stratification of Peat  
3.2.1.2.1 Distribution, thickness and carbon stock of peat 
This project adopted FAO and USDA definition of peat soils (minimum 0.4 m thickness of organic 
deposit) as the threshold value to differentiate between peat and mineral soils (Soil Survey Staff, 
2022). Peat soil stratification was performed by following field methods set out by VMD0016. Peat 
depths were sampled in 2021 – 2023 at 120 locations using a manual peat auger along 7 transects 
arranged perpendicular to the expected topographic contour of the peat domes. The transects were 
placed randomly and manually/visually distributed on map as even as possible. At each location, at 
least 3 samples were taken and averaged to control local heterogeneity. The SPCP firstly created a 
peatland extent dataset by comparing a modelled peat dome surface (Dupuit – Forchheimer parabola 
to simulate a simple hydrology and long-term equilibrium dome-shaped feature of Siluk peatlands) with 
a corrected digital terrain model (DTM), whereby DTM values significantly higher than the predicted 
peat dome model had low probability of being peatland. Peatland extent was conservatively assumed to 
be limited to a 100 m buffer around riverbanks. Data from field measurements were used to develop a 
peat soils distribution map, whereby peat thickness was calculated as the difference between a 
modelled basin elevation (as a function of distance from peatland edge) and peat surface (from the 
DTM). More details on peat thickness modelling are given in Annex 3. As shown in peat distribution map 
(Figure 39) below, it can be concluded that 60 percent (17,003.09 hectares) of the project area is 
covered by peat soils, where 41 percent (11,709.67 hectares) being at least 3 meters thickness. Peat 
thickness distributions are summarized in Table 20 below.

(Al-Shammary, et.al., 2018).

The total carbon stored in peat soils (thickness >=0.4 m) of the project area at the project start date

the carbon stock in peat soil in the Project Area are shown in Figure 39. 
 
3.2.1.2.2 Spatial and temporal discretization  
To represent two-dimensional features, a grid-based system was used. The area was divided into 
regular square cells, each with a size of 3 hectares (173.205 x 173.205 meters). This same grid layout 
was applied to all 2D features, including the project area, surface elevation, peat thickness distribution, 
land cover, and others. 
For linear features like roads and canals, a separate one-dimensional (1D) partitioning method was 
used. These features were broken down into segments with nodes spaced 100 meters apart. Although 
these features are linear, their surface area (a 2D attribute) was recorded in attribute tables. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
134 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
To ensure accurate alignment between 1D and 2D features, all positions were referenced using 
geographic coordinates. Grid conformity was maintained by calculating the proportion of area of each 
feature that falls within individual grid cells. This approach ensures that the spatial data is accurately 
represented, which is essential for correctly estimating peat emissions. 
Temporal discretization was done by partitioning carbon accounting time step (year) into 12 sub-steps 
(monthly) for baseline scenario and 366 (daily) for project scenario. Time-serial variables involved in 
hydrological modelling (like rainfall, evapotranspiration, river water levels, etc.) were conformed 
accordingly by using this time partition scheme. More details on hydrological modelling are given in 
Annex 3.

Calculation of carbon stock change in Dead Wood (C
DW
)  
Biomass loss in the form of Residual Damage (RSD) to surrounding vegetation, commonly referred to as 
logging damage, is an unavoidable consequence of timber harvesting, regardless of the method 
employed. In accordance with the equation (14) of VMD0006, it is accounted for as Dead Wood (DW). 
This inevitable disturbance contributes to additional carbon emissions and must be included in 
calculations to ensure accurate estimates of greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions. 
For the calculation of carbon stock change from residual damage, a factor of 2.3 was applied, based on 
the study by Pinard and Putz (1996). However, since the technical proposal specifies the 
implementation of Reduced Impact Logging (RIL), a reduction in residual biomass loss is expected. To 
account for this, the calculation incorporated findings from Budiaman et al. (2023), which indicate that 
RIL practices result in a 10.8% reduction in biomass loss from residual damage compared to 
conventional logging. 
The areas subjected to planned degradation within TPTI area 
Area of baseline planned degradation (AA
planned
) within TPTI area is limited solely to the productive 
forest area designated for logging. Areas affected by infrastructure development are treated as 
deforestation and are therefore accounted for under Planned Deforestation (Section 3.2.1.5).

Merchantable volume timber within TPTI (APDeg) area  
Based on forest inventory data combined with LiDAR-based analysis, the total merchantable timber 
volume within the TPTI area is estimated at 2,256,914 m³. Of this, approximately 1,937,961 m³ (86%) 
is located in Peat Swamp Forest (PSF), while the remaining 318,953 m³ (14%) is found in Dry Land 
Forest (DLF). As mandated by government regulations, merchantable volume includes only commercial 
tree species that meet specific diameter thresholds: trees with a DBH of 30 cm or greater in PSF, and 
DBH of 40 cm or greater in DLF. Species with protected status are excluded from the calculation. Table 
21 and Table 22 below present the merchantable volume allocations in the annual logging plans for 
Peat Swamp Forest and Dry Land Forest, respectively.

The merchantable volume outlined above cannot be directly counted with the actual volume of 
extracted timber. In accordance with government regulations and standard practices, the merchantable 
volume must be adjusted by applying a Safety Factor and an Exploitation Factor to derive the extracted 
volume (V
ex
). This adjusted volume is then used in the subsequent calculations, as described in the 
preceding sections.

Referring to Section 3.1.3.3, for planned deforestation case, baseline carbon stock changes in litter 
(∆C

calculation. Meanwhile, baseline carbon stock change in tree belowground biomass (∆C
BB_tree
) is 
excluded in peat swamp forests to avoid double counting with peat biomass but included for dryland 
forests.

As defined in the BL-PL, post-deforestation carbon stocks represent the long-term average carbon 
stocks on the land use following deforestation. Under the baseline scenario, and as specified in the 
technical proposal, the post-deforestation carbon stocks are below: 
• Plantation area within THPB boundary. Post deforestation land use in this area refers to Sengon 
(Paraserianthes falcataria) plantation, applying 10 years rotation. According to Junaedi and 
Rizal (2019), long term average carbon stock of sengon plantation estimated to be 26.02 tC ha
-
1
. 
• Planting strip area within TPTJ boundary. Post deforestation land use in this area refers Meranti 
(Shorea spp) plantation with the rotation of 25 years. Based on analysis using related reference 
(Winarti et. Al, 2023, Wahyudi and Panjaitan, and Musakir), long term average carbon stock of 
Meranti plantation estimated to be 44.48 tC ha
-1
. 
• Canal infrastructure in THPB and TPTI. Post deforestation land use in this area refers to water 
body. Therefore, long-term average carbon stocks assumed to be zero. 
• Road infrastructure and railway. Post deforestation land use refers to Non-Forest vegetation 
with the C stock of 23.02 tC ha
-1
. 
The areas subjected to planned deforestation  
Area of baseline planned deforestation (AA
planned
) includes the entire THPB area (4,228.95 ha), as well 
as portions of the TPTI (822.58  ha) and TPTJ (704.66 ha) area where infrastructure development 
occurs, specifically areas designated for the construction of canals and railways (see Figure 45 and 
Table 26).

Baseline carbon stock change in belowground tree biomass (∆C
BBTree, bsl
) 
The baseline carbon stock change in belowground tree biomass (∆C
BB,tree,BSL
) is estimated at 
281,077.46 tCO₂e over the duration of the project period. This emission represents the loss of carbon 
stored in tree root systems in mineral soil areas as a result of planned deforestation activities under 
the baseline scenario. Of the total emissions from belowground biomass, some 228,047.94 tCO₂e 
(81.1%) occurred in the THPB area, 7,514.18 tCO₂e (2.7%) in the TPTI area, and 45,515.34 tCO₂e 
(16.2%) in the TPTJ area. Table 29 presents the baseline carbon stock change in belowground tree 
biomass based on designated area throughout project period.

3.2.1.6 Baseline emission of Wetlands Restoration and Conservation (WRC) areas 
Calculation was carried out by using spatial-temporally explicit method, i.e. by dividing model area 
(project area is subset of it) into 23,493 number of cells, each of 3 hectares in size in a standard finite 
difference schematization. Based on their role in the system we divided our model into 3 parts that 
were interconnected and working together in unison: water balance, carbon balance, and soil 
mechanics models. 
Water balance model was used to simulate water tables by incorporating Darcy’s Law (Darcy, 1856) for 
saturated flow, Philip (1957) and Penman-Monteith (Monteith, 1965) models for unsaturated soil water 
depletion and evapotranspiration, Saint Venant (1871) and Manning (1891) equations for surface flow. 
The essence of this model is that water storage of each cell fluctuates as a function of rainfall input, 
water removal by evapotranspiration, water output flux to- and input from adjacent cells. All those 
behaviours are also governed by aquifer properties, climate, and topography. Water balance with finite 
difference method has been developed for more than a century and has gained a strong foothold in the 
application of hydrology. Among authors who justified its applications (out of extremely exhaustive list) 
are Remson, Hornberger and Molz (1971), Goode and Appel (1992), Belitz and Phillips (1993), 
Anderman and Hill (2000), Chiang and Kinzelbach (2000), Bear and Cheng (2010). A review on 
commonly used models for peatlands is given by Mozafari, et al. (2023). 
Carbon balance model comprised of simple peat decomposition and combustion models, by which 
carbon and dry mass losses were simulated to reduce aquifer volume and to alter topography of the 
peatland. These alterations in turn would also alter peatland water balance behaviour. Peat 
decomposition rates (as GHG emissions in unit mass per unit area per unit time) were modelled as a 
linear function of water table depth. Equivalent subsidence of the GHG emissions were reverse 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
163 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
calculated by using GHG molecular mass, peat bulk density and carbon content. Meanwhile, 
combustions were modelled as burn scars (in unit length) on soil surface, that were later converted to 
GHG emissions (in unit mass per unit area per unit time) by using IPCC default factors. 
In the soil mechanic model, we simulated the reduction of aquifer volume, and the subsidence of soil 
surface associated with the changes in mechanical balance in peat soil as the results of water losses 
and increased mechanical load (consolidation, compaction, and shrinkage). Aquifer volume reductions, 
when not accompanied by mass reductions, would result in increased bulk density. The latter would 
affect the rate of GHG emissions from peat combustions. We employed Terzaghi’s model for 
consolidation (Terzaghi, et.al., 1959, Verruijt, 2001) and simple linear models for compaction (Proctor 
test) and shrinkage (shrinkage box test), both later were empirical. 
Project duration was divided into 1 year time-steps (Carbon Accounting Step), while for hydrological 
modelling (see more details in Annex 3) the carbon accounting time step was further divided into 12 
sub steps (monthly) for baseline scenario and 366 sub-steps (daily) for ex-ante project scenario. 
Baseline scenario calculation was done in one run covering 100 years period. For ex-ante project 
scenario, the calculation was done in one run covering 1 year period, assuming the results would be 
very similar to those of the next iterations providing no significant changes would occur in the 
hydrological system other than rewetting impacts. For simplicity, rewetting impacted areas were 
assumed to manifest within 1000 m distance both sides from the rewetted canals. Assuming 
successful rewetting program, peat emissions within the rewetting impact areas were deemed zero 
starting from the onset times of each corresponding rewetted canal segments. In Figure 46 we show 
the conceptual framework of model inputs, processes and outputs. 
 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

Calculations were carried out in a spatially explicit, dynamic fashion to ensure the following restrictions 
in our model:  
(1)  Carbon stock depletion is consistent with subsidence 
Spatial-temporally explicit scheme helps our model in tracking where, when and the quantity of carbon 
release and stock changes. In this way, every unit of emission is always associated with equivalent unit 
of dry mass and carbon stock losses from existing stocks, and equivalent unit of subsidence of peat 
soils.  
(2)  Total carbon emissions do not exceed total carbon stock or total available stock for emission. 
Carbon stock may be depleted after prolonged emissions. Once this happens carbon emissions from 
peat oxidation are no longer possible. Peat depletion manifests as subsidence. Every time land 
subsides the soil surface gets closer to the peat base. In many cases, peat bases are lower than the 
minimum possible drainage depth (i.e., Drainability Elevation Limit, DEL a.k.a. ultimate drainage base). 
Carbon stock below DEL is not available for emissions, unless the responsible baseline agent is proven 
to be able and intent to apply mechanical pumping for years in order to maintain water table levels well 
below the DEL. In this project we conservatively assumed that the baseline agent is not able or willing 
to apply mechanical pumping in the future. 
Available stock for emissions in each grid cell was determined based on corresponding surface 
elevation, DEL, and peat thickness. It is equivalent with the term depletable peat volume per unit area, 
or available peat thickness for subsidence, and can be written as follows: 
퐿

created using LiDAR data along the survey transects (from ground-classified LiDAR returns), and using 
an iterative gap-filling technique (whereby the Forest and Buildings Removed Digital Elevation Model 
dataset was corrected using distance-weighted blending, so that the LiDAR data were treated as 
reference data (never corrected), and the FABDEM data were locally integrated to produce a seamless 
DEM). The DEL was calculated as a function of distance from the closest water body. More details on 
surface elevation and DEL mapping are given in Annex 3. For each cell, if DEL was at higher elevation 
than peat basal contact, then 푍

Water table depths were estimated by using a spatially explicit, dynamic, hydrological model over the 
baseline period. Main input for this model includes precipitation (measured on site, and Climate 
Hazards Group Infrared Precipitation Station data, CHIRPS), aquifer properties (among others: hydraulic 
conductivity measured in situ) and canal hydraulic properties (among others: measured dimension and 
slope). As many as 21 dipwells were installed to measure actual water table depth in the field from 
November 23, 2022– April 22, 2025.  Model parameters were calibrated by using water table depth 
data and the least square method. More details on hydrological modelling are given in Annex 3.  
A general rule was applied for every cell that as the water table drops below DEL, the water table at that 
position was limited to DEL (i.e., the lowest possible water table at a position respective to its 
surrounding natural drainage).  
Drainage, fires, intense drying and mechanical compaction induces subsidence of peat surface. 
Subsidence attributed directly to drainage comprises consolidation and loss to microbial decomposition 
of peat soil. Fires also induce peat loss, but at a faster rate than microbial decomposition does. Intense 
drying induces shrinkage, and mechanical compaction causes compaction of peat soils. 
Subsidence due to peat decomposition for each cell was reverse-calculated using equivalent dry mass 
loss and bulk density profile of peat soil (see also Annex 3):

Equivalent subsidence due to peat combustion for each cell was directly derived from burn scar depth 
plus associated shrinkage that follows. Burn scar depths were set to be dependent on cells’ individual 
burning history as listed in Table 31.  
With the application of drainage in the baseline scenario peat soils were expected to undergo rapid 
initial subsidence in the few weeks to few months after first drainage and is considered as one-time 
event. This rapid subsidence was assumed to be completed within a year. The location and timing of 
cells experiencing initial subsidence were set to match the time of the onset of drainage. Rapid initial 
subsidence comprises consolidation, and shrinkage (if also followed by deforestation). Compaction is 
simulated in areas with heavy traffic or intense human access along roads/railroads and managed 
deforested areas. 
Both shrinkage and compaction were calculated by using measured shrinkage and compaction 
properties of peat soils and are treated as one time event. The depth of peat soils undergone shrinkage 
and compaction is limited to the upper phreatic zone. In this project an estimate maximum value of 40 
cm was used both for shrinkage and compaction depths

Soil deformation such as those caused by peat decomposition, combustion, consolidation and 
shrinkage reduce the volume of peat soil. Volume reduction with the absence of soil mass loss 
increases soil bulk density of peat soils. Meanwhile, Volume loss accompanied by mass loss may or 
may not change bulk density but certainly will reduce dry mass density. Dry mass density of peat soil at 
any cell at any time was calculated as follows (more details are given in Annex 3):

Baseline Fire Modelling 
The SPCP approach for modelling fire occurrence combined logistic regression to estimate extent of fire 
occurrence and Monte Carlo simulations to predict the distribution of fire occurrence in the baseline 
period, full details of the approach are found in Annex 3. The logistic regression component produced 
risk maps based on fire hotspot data (2015 – 2024) from MODIS and VIIRS, as well as the following 
variables: (1) Distance to frequently accessed drainage canals, (2) Distance to less frequently accessed 
drainage canals, (3) Distance to normal roads and highways, (4) Distance to small tracks and dirt 
roads, (5) Distance to navigable rivers, (6) Distance to less navigable rivers, (7) Distance to settlements 
(village, cities/towns), (8) Distance to farms and other agricultural lands, (9) Land cover type (forest or 
non-forest) over a 1,574,923.4 ha focal area. Focal area delineation was based on consideration on the 
anthropogenic (ethnicity, state of development the area) and landscape similarities to the project area. 
As proven in a regression analysis, anthropogenic factors play the most prominent role in determining 
hotspot distribution. 
From this analysis, a value of 3.82% was derived as the annual percentage of area burnt (AAB%) and 
was then used to determine a threshold for annual area burnt (AAB) under baseline scenario as follows: 
퐴퐴퐵=퐴퐴퐵%×퐴

would have become an O-layer of a mineral soil, and the peat emission would cease

Baseline fire events were simulated using a Monte Carlo simulation. Using parameters calculated in 
logistic regression process, for each year in the baseline period starting from the start date, Fire Scores 
for every cell in 퐴
퐹푖푟푒
 were calculated by using Fire Probability values and random numbers as follows. 
퐹푆
푖
=
푐×푅푎푛푑
푖
+푃
푖
푐+1
 
Where:   
퐹푆 
: Fire score (-) 
푃 
: Fire probability (-) 
푅푎푛푑 
: Random number (decimal) 
푖 
: Index of grid in Project Area susceptible to fire  
푐 
: Random weight coefficient (integer, empirically fitted: 100) 
The random weight coefficient was optimized by minimizing residuals of Observed Mean Distance 
(OMD) statistic between modelled hot spot (in model area) versus historical data (in the focal area) by 
trying values between 1 - 200. It was found that at c ≥ 100 the residuals have become small enough 
and convergence, and can be applied in our model 
For each year, the fire scores were then sorted in descending order and the upper portion of the sorted 
rows, from the first row to the 푁
퐹푖푟푒
 row, were selected as the realization of the fire incidents for the 
corresponding year.  
Every realization of fire incident in individual cells was recorded during the simulation over baseline 
period. Every time the same cell was burnt it added to its cumulative count history, and the burn scar 
depth parameter value for the respective cell was adjusted accordingly. Conservatively, no peat 
combustion was assumed to take place wherever peat thickness was less than 0.2 meters. 
Model accuracy was assessed using a confusion matrix approach, whereby modeled/predicted 
locations of fire events were compared to historical hotspot detections (from the last 10 years). As the 
accuracy score is sensitive to sample size, the sample size was increased until the accuracy stabilized 
(where sample size has no effect). This modelling approach correctly predicted hotspot locations 75.5% 
of the time, when compared to historical observations (Annex 3 for further details).

3.2.2.1 Project emissions from REDD area (TPTI, TPTJ, THPB)  
3.2.2.1.1 Project emissions as a result of deforestation 
Based on historical records, deforestation has occurred within the concession area, indicating a 
potential risk of continued forest loss. Accordingly, emissions from deforestation are anticipated and 
included in the project scenario for the REDD area. The project aims to implement a range of 
interventions and mitigation measures to gradually reduce and ultimately halt deforestation within a

3.2.2.1.2 Project emissions as a result of forest degradation  
Per VMD0015, a ground-based monitoring approach was adopted. As part of this approach, a 
Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA) will be conducted with communities living around the project area to 
assess whether there is a potential risk of forest degradation by illegal logging.  
Should the Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA) identify a potential risk of illegal logging activities, a field 
sampling process will be carried out to assess the area of possible forest degradation. The specific area 
potentially subject to due to forest degradation (A
DegW,i
) will be determined based on the estimated 
distance of logging penetration from various access points, using data collected during PRA. These 
access points may include roads, rivers, previously cleared areas, or any other identified entry routes 
that provide access to the forest. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
181 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The area potentially subject to forest degradation (A
DegW,i
) will be sampled by surveying several 
transects of known length and width across the access buffer area (equal in area to at least 1% of 
A
DegW,i
) to determine the presence or absence of new tree stumps. Where limited sampling provides 
evidence that trees are being removed in the buffer area, then systematic sampling will be 
implemented based on a detailed sampling plan. The sampling plan will be designed using plots 
systematically placed over the buffer zone so that they sample at least 3% of the area of the buffer 
zone (A
DegW,i
). Per VMD00015, the field survey will be carried out every 5 years. During the survey, the 
diameter of tree stumps will be measured and conservatively assumed to be the same as the DBH. 
Especially for tree with buttress, the project will determine a ratio of the diameter at DBH to the 
diameter of buttress at the same height above ground as the measured stumps, The same allometric 
equation used for baseline carbon stock calculation would be used to estimate the carbon stock loss 
due to logging.  
The net carbon stock changes as a result of degradation is calculated by equation (8) of VMD0015.

SPCP is committed to eliminating illegal logging within the project area by implementing a 
comprehensive approach that includes community development, capacity building, awareness raising, 
and engagement of local authorities. Through community development initiatives, the project will 
provide alternative livelihoods and economic opportunities to reduce dependency on illegal logging. 
Capacity building efforts will support local communities (prioritizing loggers) with the necessary skills 
and resources to support the project in relevant programs including forest monitoring and patrols. 
Additionally, awareness campaigns will educate communities on the importance of protecting natural 
resources and promote sustainable land-use practices. By integrating these strategies, SPCP is 
confident that illegal logging can be fully eradicated within ten years from the project’s start date. 
Applying equation (8) VMD0015, ex-ante GHG emissions resulting from illegal logging within REDD area 
under the project scenario are estimated to be 169,833.33 tCO
2
e, occurring between 2023 and 2034.

3.2.2.1.3 Net carbon stock change as a result of natural disturbances 
Historical records indicate that the project area has not experienced natural disturbances such as 
windstorms, wildfires, or insect outbreaks. However, uncontrolled burning have been documented in the 
past within and surrounding areas, identifying it as a potential risk that must be taken into account in 
the project scenario. 
The project assumes that uncontrolled burning would affect approximately 87 hectares of Forest and 
61 hectares of Non-Forest areas. Based on this assumption, and using the methodological approach

2031 (see Table 36).

3.2.2.1.4 GHG emissions as a result of deforestation and degradation activities 
Considering its exclusion in the baseline scenario, greenhouse gas emissions resulting from 
deforestation and degradation activities within the project area under the project case (GHG
P-E,I,t
) are 
likewise excluded from the calculation. This approach ensures consistency and comparability between 
the baseline and project scenarios, aligning with the principle of conservativeness in emissions 
accounting.

3.2.3 Leakage Emissions (VCS 2.5, 3.2, 3.6, 3.15, 4.3) 
3.2.3.1 Activity Shifting Leakage 
Since this project was built on the acquisition of an existing logging company, whose land and water 
management schemes were designed in advance, the baseline scenario of SPCP is therefore 
categorized as a planned deforestation/drainage with identifiable unique agent, i.e. the logging 
company PT Annisa Surya Kencana. Furthermore, PT ASK does not manage any other forest concession 
areas. With a new company goal and a new management in place, with no intention to resume the 
baseline land use nor to create the same baseline land use somewhere else, this project deemed zero 
activity shifting leakage throughout project period. Referring to VMD0009 (LK-ASP) module, the 
following table provides a summary of the leakage emissions of the project. 
Table 39. Summary of the leakage emissions of the project

3.2.3.2 Ecological Leakage 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
194 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Water management of SPCP is very unlikely to cause harmful ecological effects on its surrounding area, 
on the contrary it may provide ecological benefits. The water balance of ombrogenic tropical peatlands 
are mostly governed by rainfall and evapotranspiration while groundwater flow usually contributes less 
than 7 percent of the balance (Hirano, et al., 2015; Ritzema and Jansen, 2008; Wosten et al., 2006; 
Toby, et al., 2024). Based on 2024 data from our rainfall station it can be shown that 
evapotranspiration and surface flow make 69.3% and 26.4% contributions, respectively, while 
groundwater flow contributes 4.4% to the water balance. 
Rewetting activities such as canal blocking, in essence, work by improving total storage in peat aquifer, 
with minor effects on groundwater flow to the surrounding. Increasing aquifer storage in an area does 
not affect its adjacent area on a large scale. There may be significant water table raise along its 
periphery to a limited extent, within few meters to few hundred meters, with diminishing impacts with 
distance. This water table raise would benefit vegetation growth instead of being harmful and may also 
reduce peat emissions in the affected area. 
Nevertheless, following VMD0044 (LK-ECO) module, this project will install up to 10 monitoring wells in 
peatland surrounding the project to be able to detect ecological leakage. For ex-ante calculation, GHG 
emission from ecological leakage is deemed zero. 
3.2.3.3 Emissions from market effect leakage (LK-ME)  
REDD projects that prevent emissions from deforestation driven by timber harvesting as part of the 
baseline activities are required to account for market leakage emissions occurring within the national 
boundaries. Additionally, if such leakage-driven deforestation is expected to result in peatland 
degradation, the associated emissions must be included under LK-ME, in accordance with Equation (1) 
of VMD0011.

Under baseline scenario, SPCP would have avoided emissions by preventing selective logging (APDeg 
and APDeg_SP). As a result of project implementation, a reduction in wood harvest within the project 
area is expected. This may potentially lead to a shift in timber production to other areas of the country 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
to compensate for the reduced supply. Based on this, the emission from market effect leakage through 
decreased timber (LK
MarketEffects,timber
) is calculated. As the baseline does not include deforestation 
driven by fuelwood or charcoal production, emissions from these activities (LK
MarketEffects,FW/C
) are 
excluded. Since 2019, Presidential Instruction No. 5 permanently prohibits new timber production 
licenses on peatlands in Indonesia. In line with this, the Ministry of Environment and Forestry (KLHK) 
regularly updates the Indicative Map for the Suspension of New Permits (PIPPIB) every six months. 
Given these legal restrictions, it is neither legally nor practically possible for the SPCP to cause 
deforestation leakage on other peatlands. Therefore, emissions due to market-effects leakage through 
decreased timber, fuelwood and charcoal harvest resulting in increased peatland drainage  
(LK
MarketEffects,Peat
) is excluded. Based on this, Net greenhouse gas emissions due to market-effects 
leakage (∆C
LK−ME
) is equal to total GHG emissions due to market-effects leakage through decreased 
timber harvest (LK

).

Calculation of LF
ME 
was carried out by assessing the proportion of total biomass made up of 
merchantable commercial species (푃푀푃푖) for each stratum (Forest Type; Peat Swamp Forest or PSF 
and Dry Land Forest or DLF). This is then compared to the average proportion of merchantable biomass 
across each forest type (푃푀퐿퐹푇). Using field data, this is estimated that PMP for PSF is at 24.5% and 
for PMPDLF at 18.9%. For calculating PMLFT, national average logging potential for dryland forests 
based on official government data was used, with relevant forest biomass values sourced Forest 
Reference Emission Level (FREL, 2022). Since PMLFT exceeds PMP for both forest types by more than 
15%, therefore a default LF
ME
 value of 0.2 is applied.

As detailed in Section 2.3.6, the total Project Non-Permanence Risk Buffer has been determined at

withholding is applied exclusively to the net greenhouse gas (GHG) emission reductions and removals, 
and explicitly excludes any emissions attributable to leakage as presented in Table 45.

3.3.3 Monitoring Plan (VCS, 3.16, 3.20) 
3.3.3.1 REDD Monitoring Plan 
Monitoring Forest Degradation 
As required by VMD0015, the SPCP will conduct a Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA) to assess the 
potential for illegal logging within and around the project area. If the PRA indicates a risk of forest 
degradation, a preliminary field sampling will be undertaken. 
The area that is potentially subject to degradation firstly needs to be delineated (ADeg,i). An output of 
the PRA will be a distance of degradation penetration from all access points (access buffer), such as 
roads, pathway, and rivers or previously cleared areas, to the project area. The area subject to 
degradation shall be delineated (ADegW,i) based on an access buffer from all access points to the 
project area, with a width equal to the distance of degradation penetration. ADegW,i shall be sampled 
by surveying several transects of known length and width across the access-buffer area (equal in area 
to at least 1% of ADegW,i ) to check whether new tree stumps are evident or not.  
If the limited sampling finds trees being removed in the buffer area, then a more systematic sampling 
will be implemented. The sampling plan will be designed using plots systematically placed over the 
buffer zone so that they sample at least 3% of the area of the buffer zone (ADegW,i). The diameter of all 
tree stumps will be measured and conservatively assumed to be the same as the DBH. 
The mean above ground carbon stock of the harvested trees will be conservatively estimated to be the 
total emissions and to all enter the atmosphere. This sampling procedure will be repeated every five 
years and the results annualized by dividing the total emissions by five. 
The Net carbon stock changes as result of degradation is calculated by applying the equation (8) of 
VMD0015 below. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

Monitoring Deforestation 
Deforestation monitoring will be carried out through the interpretation and analysis of satellite images 
to identify areas showing signs of forest loss. For this purpose, SPCP will regularly collect satellite 
imagery throughout the monitoring period and collect additional data to track deforestation within the 
project area. This process will adhere to the guidelines set out in the GOFC-GOLD Sourcebook for REDD 
(2016), ensuring consistency and accuracy in mapping and calculating deforestation. Additional data 
sources (including Synthetic Aperture RADAR, drone imagery, LiDAR data) will also be utilized to 
enhance the analysis. Once deforestation is detected, the project will conduct field verification to 
confirm the findings and assess on-the-ground conditions. When applicable, drone surveys will be used 
to support precise delineation of deforested areas.  
The Net carbon stock changes as result of deforestation is calculated by applying the equation (3) of 
VMD0015 below.

Monitoring Burnt Areas  
The project collects satellite hotspot data on a daily basis (from NASA FIRMS). For every fire incident 
within the project area, SPCP will map the affected areas and delineate burn scars. Initial mapping will 
be conducted through spatial analysis using satellite imagery and hotspot data, primarily from MODIS 
and NOAA. This will be followed by field verification to confirm the extent of the burnt area and assess 
the impact of fire on biomass. When applicable, drone will be utilized to support accurate delineation of 
burn scars. To guide this process, the project has developed a Standard Operating Procedure (SOP) for 
monitoring and assessing fire-affected areas. 
Emission due to biomass burning is calculated by applying the equation (1) of VMD0013 below.

3.3.3.2 WRC Monitoring Plan 
Water table depths are used as proxy for calculating GHG emissions in the project area and will be 
monitored by using a hydrological modelling approach. The project will use a network of automatic and 
manual dipwells to log water table depth at regular intervals throughout the project lifetime, and 
detailed procedures of water table monitoring can be found in Annex 7. Model outputs include daily 
water table depth raster. Model validity and accuracy will be assured in the parameter calibration 
process by using directly measured water table depth data. For this purpose, several dipwells will be 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
214 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
installed in the project area and will be measured by combining manual (weekly or two-weekly interval) 
and automatic methods (30-minutes interval) throughout the year. Daily rainfall will be measured using 
two automatic rainfall stations. A map of monitoring devices is shown in Figure 47. Physical parameters 
required for this modeling, which are hydraulic conductivity and storage coefficient, will be measured at 
random locations, and will be determined later. 
 
Figure 47. Location of Dipwells in the project area for WRC Monitoring 
This hydrological model will be set-up in a spatially distributed dynamic schematization and will include 
water balance components: rainfall, evapotranspiration, groundwater flow, overland flow and open-
channel flow. Peat thickness, DTM and rivers and canal networks will act as setup parameters. Canal 
water levels will be used as boundary conditions and will be measured using water level measurement 
devices (such as a staff gauge or AWLR) by combined manual (weekly/two-weekly interval) and 
automatic methods (30-minute interval). 
Drainage network conditions in the Project Area will be monitored throughout the year. The presence or 
absence of new illegal canals will be mapped (using ground surveys, as well as LiDAR surveys) and 
remedied by constructing canal blocking as soon as possible. Canal water levels of new illegal canals 
will also be monitored by installing appropriate water level measurement device. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
215

Daily water table depth rasters resulting from this modelling will be averaged into yearly water table 
depths. From this result, yearly CO

Up to 10 monitoring wells will be installed in select adjacent peatland areas at 20 – 100 meters 
distance from project boundary that would be affected by project’s rewetting measure. These wells 
served as supporting evidence for the presence or absence of ecological leakage. Exact locations for 
these monitoring wells will be determined later after completing the project rewetting design. 
3.3.4 Dissemination of Monitoring Plan and Results (VCS, 3.18; CCB, CL4.2) 
SPCP is committed to transparency and inclusive participation. The monitoring plan will be 
disseminated directly to communities and other relevant stakeholders in formats and languages suited 
to their needs. Printed and simplified summaries of the monitoring plan (in Bahasa Indonesia) will be 
shared during meetings with communities and through distribution at village offices. When appropriate, 
the project will also develop visual materials, such as infographics and posters, to clearly explain key 
components of the monitoring plan. 
In addition, the monitoring plan will be disseminated to relevant government agencies and adapted to 
meet the specific data and reporting requirements mandated for PT ASK, in accordance with its regular 
compliance obligations to the government. 
A summary of the monitoring plan will also be uploaded to the PT ASK website to ensure wider public 
accessibility and transparency. By making this information publicly available on the PT ASK website, the 
project ensures that stakeholders, including civil society, researchers, and interested members of the 
public, can easily access and understand how the project implementation are monitored. 
3.4 Optional Criterion: Climate Change Adaptation Benefits  
3.4.1 Regional Climate Change Scenarios (CCB, GL1.1) 
Without the project, forest and peatland degradation will accelerate, leading to severe climate change 
impacts in the region. As these ecosystems deteriorate, vast amounts of stored carbon will be released 
into the atmosphere, significantly intensifying global warming. This will drive rising temperatures, 
prolonged droughts, and increasingly erratic rainfall patterns, disrupting the region’s ecological 
balance. Without the protective function of healthy forests and peatlands as carbon sinks, West 
Kalimantan will face more extreme weather events, including heatwaves, extended dry seasons, and a 
heightened risk of wildfires. 
The absence of the project will also weaken the region’s natural ability to regulate water cycles. The 
loss of forest cover will result in more frequent and severe flooding during the wet season, while 
prolonged dry periods will lead to critical water shortages. Rising temperatures and shifting 
precipitation patterns will further increase the risk of drought, drying out degraded peatlands and 
making them more prone to uncontrolled fires. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
216 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
For local communities, these climate disruptions will severely threaten traditional livelihoods, 
particularly for farmers and fishers who depend on stable environmental conditions. Unpredictable 
rainfall and water shortages will reduce agricultural productivity, leading to crop failures and declining 
food security.  
3.4.2 Climate Change Impacts (CCB, GL1.2) 
Current and projected climate changes are already affecting and will continue to impact community 
well-being in the project zone and surrounding areas. Rising temperatures, prolonged dry seasons, and 
unpredictable rainfall patterns are leading to increased food and water insecurity. Agriculture, which is 
a primary livelihood for many local communities, is particularly vulnerable to climate variability. More 
frequent droughts and irregular rainfall disrupt planting and harvesting cycles, reducing crop yields and 
threatening food availability. 
Economic hardship is another major consequence of climate change, as declining agricultural 
productivity and resource degradation force communities to seek alternative livelihoods. However, 
without proper adaptation measures, many may turn into unsustainable activities such as illegal 
logging, poaching, or slash-and-burn farming, further accelerating environmental degradation. 
The project zone and surrounding regions are home to diverse ecosystems, including peatlands, 
tropical forests, and freshwater habitats that support numerous endangered and endemic species. 
However, climate change is increasingly threatening biodiversity by altering habitat conditions and 
exacerbating existing threats such as deforestation and land degradation. 
Rising temperatures and prolonged dry seasons are drying out peatlands, increasing their susceptibility 
to fires, which destroy critical habitats and release vast amounts of carbon into the atmosphere. These 
fires, along with more frequent droughts, directly threaten species that depend on intact forest 
ecosystems, including critically endangered orangutans (Pongo pygmaeus). Many of these species rely 
on dense forests for food and shelter, and habitat loss caused by climate change forces them into 
smaller, fragmented areas, increasing competition for resources and reducing genetic diversity. 
Flooding and changing water dynamics also affect freshwater biodiversity. Increased sedimentation and 
pollution from runoff reduce water quality, impacting fish populations and other aquatic species. 
Climate change also exacerbates human-wildlife conflicts as species are forced to move beyond their 
traditional habitats in search of food and water. This increases the likelihood of interactions with 
human settlements, leading to increased poaching, retaliatory killings, and habitat destruction 
3.4.3 Measures Needed and Designed for Adaptation (CCB, GL1.3) 
SPCP recognizes that climate change poses significant risks to both local communities and biodiversity 
within the project area. These risks include increased temperature variability, prolonged dry seasons, 
heightened fire risks in peatlands, reduced water availability, shifts in species distribution, and greater 
vulnerability of forest ecosystems. To address these challenges, SPCP has designed and implemented a 
range of targeted adaptation measures. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
217 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Given the increasing intensity and frequency of droughts due to climate change, peatland fires pose a 
major threat to both ecosystems and human health. In response, SPCP is implementing community-
based fire management strategies and establishing early warning systems and fire monitoring through 
remote sensing, drones, and community-based patrols. Additionally, SPCP is rewetting and 
hydrologically restoring degraded peatlands to reduce fire susceptibility and enhance ecosystem 
resilience. 
Climate change is expected to reduce the productivity of traditional land-based livelihoods, such as 
farming and fishing. To reduce dependency on forest resources and increase economic resilience, SPCP 
supports the community through Community Enterprise Program Development (CEPD) initiatives and 
provides employment opportunities by engaging local communities in forest protection, restoration, and 
conservation activities. The project also offers technical assistance and capacity-building to enhance 
livelihood productivity, particularly for those vulnerable to climate change impacts. 
Furthermore, SPCP enhances community-level adaptation by delivering climate change education and 
awareness programs designed for local contexts. These programs emphasize the link between 
ecosystem protection and long-term community resilience. SPCP also trains local communities on zero-
burning agricultural practices and sustainable forest management to reduce climate risks and improve 
adaptive capacities. 
 
4 COMMUNITY 
4.1 If theyWithout-Project Community Scenario  
4.1.1 Descriptions of Communities at Project Start (CCB, CM1.1) 
At the start of the project, a socio-economic baseline assessment was conducted between 2022 and 2023 
in  14  villages  within  the project area. This  baseline  aimed  to  provide  a  comprehensive  profile  of  the 
communities, capturing social, economic, cultural, and environmental dimensions for project planning and 
implementation. 
The  assessment  employed  a  mixed-methods  approach,  combining  qualitative  and  quantitative  data 
collection techniques. These included focus group discussions (FGDs), structured questionnaires, and in-
depth interviews. FGDs were conducted with a cross-section of community representatives, including village 
heads, customary leaders, women, marginalized groups, and other community members, to capture diverse 
community perspectives. Structured questionnaires were administered to a sample of 30 individuals per 
village,  collecting  data  on  household  income,  types  of  livelihoods,  age,  number  of  dependents,  and 
education  level.  At  least  30% of  respondents  were  women,  ensuring  gender-inclusive representation.  In-
depth  interviews  with  key  informants—such  as customary leaders  and  local  decision-makers—helped 
validate  and  enrich  the  information  gathered. Each  village  assessment  was  completed  over  a  5–7-day 
period, during which both primary data (sourced from local governments and communities) and secondary 
data (collected from existing reports and field validation) were compiled.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
218 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
As of 2022, the total population across the 14 villages was 9,688 people, composed of 4,894 males and 
4,794  females.  Most  residents  are  of  productive  working  age  (15–64),  providing  a  strong  labour  base. 
However, education levels remain low: 41.57% have completed only elementary school, 11.65% junior high, 
and  16.15%  high  school  or  higher.  Limited  school  infrastructure,  long  travel  distances,  and  teacher 
shortages hinder learning outcomes. 
Health  conditions  are  also  concerning.  Diarrhoea,  skin  infections,  respiratory  ailments,  and  tetanus  are 
common due to the humid terrain and lack of sanitation. Village health posts are largely non-operational, 
with residents depending on sub-district centres that are often remote and costly to access. 
The table below summarizes key demographic, economic, and service-related indicators at project start.

The baseline findings indicate that the 14 villages are located within two ecologically distinct areas.  
Putussibau Utara is predominantly forested, with communities whose livelihood are closely tied to 
forest-resources. In contrast, Embaloh Hilir is defined by a network of rivers and lakes that support 
aquatic-based subsistence systems. Across both areas, communities rely heavily on natural resources 
for daily life and economic activities. 
The villages are inhabited by diverse ethnic groups, including Dayak Kantuk, Dayak Iban, Dayak Taman, 
Dayak Tamambaloh, Dayak Ngaju, Malay, Javanese, Flores and others. These groups maintain strong 
historical, cultural and spiritual ties to the surrounding natural environment.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
219 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Governance in the villages operates under a dual system that blends formal administrative structure 
with strong customary institutions. The head of village (Kepala Desa), formally elected and recognized 
by the government, leads village administration, public services and development coordination in 
partnership with the Village Consultative Body (Badan Permusyawaratan Desa, BPD), a representative 
council ensuring community participation, oversight, and transparency. In parallel to this, the traditional 
system remains influential, led by Ketua Adat (customary leader) at the village level, supported by the 
Temenggung (subdistrict customary leader), and the Dewan Adat Dayak (DAD-district level council). 
These traditional leaders oversee matters of customary laws, uphold indigenous knowledge, safeguard 
collective rights, cultural preservation, land management, conflict resolution and ensure the 
enforcement of customary law through culturally appropriate sanctions.  
The baseline also highlights the following defining cultural and social characteristics of the 
communities that shape their identity and governance: 
• Language: predominantly Dayak languages, with distinct local dialects. 
• Mythology and History:  deep  spriritual ties  to ancestral spirits and the supernatural traditions. 
• Culture practices: rich expression of Dayak  music, dance, traditional clothing, weaving and 
carving craft. 
• Livelihood Systems: reliance on agriculture, plantations, hunting, fishing, gathering, and  
handicrafts. 
• Practices and Values: Social cohesion fostered through unity, mutual cooperation (gotong 
royong), and respect, reflected in communal ceremonies for weddings, harvests, and religious 
events. 
• Historical, Cultural, and Spiritual Sites: Sacred places such as Gale Buya in Nanga Awin hold 
profound cultural and spiritual importance. 
• Natural Resources relationship: a deep connection to forests, rivers, lakes, and land, which 
remain vital to daily life and cultural identity.

Baseline assessment confirmed the Dayak groups demonstrate deep reliance on natural resources.  
Three primary forms of dependence on the forest have been identified: 
• Economic Dependence: The forests supply timber, building materials, fuelwood, and non-timber 
products such as mushrooms, honey, resin, rattan, and medicinal plants, all essential for both 
subsistence and income generation. 
• Cultural and Spiritual Dependence: Forests are integral to traditional ceremonies and spiritual 
practices. Rituals like the Nyengkelan Buaya in Nanga Awin and Timang Lalu in Nanga Lauk 
reflects these deep connections. 
• Ecosystem Dependence: Forests contribute to biodiversity preservation, water cycle regulation, 
climate stability, and protection from natural hazards. 
The local economy is primarily based on agriculture, with kratom, field rice, corn, and vegetables as key 
crops.  Rubber plantations once played a significant economic role, but declining market prices have 
led the community to diversify. Fishing is a major livelihood, with species such as Toman, Belida, 
Jelawat, and Catfish commonly caught for consumption and sale. High-value species like Arwana 
present opportunities for aquaculture, though this requires substantial investment. Rattan handicrafts, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
221 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
including mats, hats, baskets, and bags are also economically promising, particularly with improved 
design and marketing support. 
Women play essential roles in sustaining the local economic and socio-cultural. They are actively 
involved in farming, fishing, food processing and handicrafts, contributing directly to household welfare. 
However, the degree of women’s participation varies between villages due to differing social norms and 
access to resources.  
Despite these strengths, the baseline assessment identified several critical challenges: 
• Heavy reliance on natural resources has leads to unsustainable practices and environmental 
degradation. 
• Poverty remains widespread, with limited access to education, healthcare, and infrastructure 
such as roads.  
• Illegal activities, including illegal logging and hunting, threating conservation efforts and create 
tensions between traditional use and environmental policies. 
• Shifting cultivation practices contribute to forest and peatland degradation, exacerbating 
ecological risks. 
This baseline findings offer critical insights into the social, cultural, economic, and ecological conditions 
of the target communities, providing a strong foundation for designing inclusive, contect-responsive and 
sustainable project interventions. 
 
4.1.2 Interactions between Communities and Community Groups (VCS, 3.19; CCB, 
CM1.1) 
As identified in the stakeholder analysis, at least six distinct community groups operate within the 
project area. These include farmers, fishermen, Non-Timber Forest Product (NTFP) collectors, loggers, 
hunters, and marginalized groups.  
The interactions among these groups are characterized by close, mutually beneficial relationships. For 
instance, fishermen supply a key source of protein to both local households and neighboring 
communities. NTFP collectors contribute valuable forest-based resources such as honey, resin, 
medicinal plants, and rattan which support local livelihoods and feed into broader community markets. 
In turn, these groups rely on local services and trading systems that are sustained by other members of 
the community.

4.1.4 Without-Project Scenario: Community (CCB, CM1.3) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
225 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
As described in Section 2.2.1, the project area was designated for selective logging and conversion to 
an industrial timber plantation. The likely without-project scenario for local communities would be as 
follows: 
Economic and livelihood impacts 
• Loss of traditional livelihoods: in the without-project scenario, many residents of the 14 
villages, who rely on forest products such as honey, resin, rattan, medicinal plants and fish, 
would face a declining availability of these resources. This would directly impact their income 
and food security. 
• Reduced agricultural productivity: Tree removal can lead to erosion and soil degradation.  In 
the long run, this lowers agricultural productivity, leading to food shortages and increased 
poverty among communities. 
• Dependence on unsustainable practices: In the without-project scenario, the logging company 
is likely to neglect community livelihood programs. This may drive communities to rely more on 
unsustainable    practices such    as illegal    logging,    land    burning, shifting    cultivation, 
unsustainable  oil  palm planting - particularly in  the northern areas-wildlife  hunting, and 
overfishing- as they seek to sustain their livelihoods. 
Social and cultural impacts 
• Erosion of cultural practices: In the without-project scenario, exploitation of forests and natural 
resources would undermine traditional knowledge and practices, such as the use of medicinal 
plants and construction of traditional houses (Betang), leading to gradual loss of cultural identity. 
• Conflict over resources: increased competition for dwindling natural resources may lead to 
conflicts between the company and communities, particularly over access to land, water and forest 
products. 
• Decline in health conditions: As access to clean water diminishes and reliance on unsafe and less 
nutritious food sources increases, overall community health may decline. Additionally, increased 
exposure to environmental hazards, such as smoke from forest fires could lead to respiratory or 
other health problems. 
• Increase vulnerability and poverty: In the without-project  scenario, with declining natural 
resources and traditional income sources, communities may face rising poverty levels, making 
them vulnerable to external shocks such as economic downturns or natural disasters.  
4.2 Net Positive Community Impacts  
4.2.1 Expected Community Impacts (CCB, CM2.1) 
The with-project scenario is designed to improve social and economic conditions by promoting 
inclusivity, active participation, and community’s empowerment. The expected outcomes are 
significantly positive (detailed in Table 48 Section 4.2.3), enhancing community well-being by enabling 
people to address the challenges they face. The project engages communities from the outset through 
the following methods:

• Program Planning – Developing a structured workplan with clear objectives, strategies, and 
steps tailored to community-specific needs 
• Community Participation – Actively involving community members in the planning, 
implementation, and monitoring processes to ensure solutions are locally relevant 
• Community Empowerment – Strengthening local capacity by providing knowledge, skills, and 
access to resources that enable communities to lead their own development.

economically disadvantaged groups. The program designs activities to match the specific needs, roles, 
and conditions of each group, helping to ensure that everyone can participate meaningfully and benefit 
fairly. This targeted approach increases the impact of the program and supports broader goals of social 
inclusion and sustainable development.

To support sustainable development and safeguard community well-being, the project will implement 
the following strategies: 
• Promote inclusive and gender-sensitive approaches by ensuring at least 30% of women 
representation in project-formed groups and decision making roles. Schedule meetings and 
activities at times and places that are accessible and covenient for women, considering 
childcare responsibilities and household obligation. 
• Actively engage marginalized groups (e.g., person with disabilities, elderly, poor and people with 
limited acccess to livelihood programs) to ensure their meaningful participation and inclusion in 
project activities. This approach fosters a safe, equitable environment and promotes leadership 
and participation from underrepresented community members. 
• Protection of High Conservation Value (HCV) areas such as sites of cultural, spiritual, and 
subsistence importance, especially peatland forests providing clean water, fish, and NTFPs. 
• Sustainable use: Promote responsible harvesting of local resources such as honey, rattan, and 
medicinal plants.  
• Community participation: ensure transparent and inclusive decision-making throughout project 
implementation. 
• Adaptive management: apply a flexible approach that integrates ongoing monitoring, 
community feedback, and continuous improvement.
