4.2.2 Negative Community Impact Mitigation (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.2) 
Some identified community groups within the Project Area are currently practicing logging and hunting 
activities that are considered illegal and ecologically unsustainable. Under the with-project scenario, 
these activities will be gradually phased out through close collaboration with the communities 
themselves.  The project will prioritize awareness raising and engaged these groups in various project 
components such as alternative livelihood initiatives, forest restoration and conservation efforts 
tailored to their interests and capacities.   
The project also recognizes the potential risk of conflict arising from perceived inequalities in the 
distribution of livelihood support, particularly during the initial implementation phase when 
interventions are carried out in a limited scale. To mitigate this, the project will adopt a phased 
expansion strategy, ensuring more community members are engaged throughout the project period.   
Additionally, to extend its impact across a wider population, the project will enhance outreach through 
education support, health awareness campaigns, and initiatives to improve access to clean water.   
4.2.3 Net Positive Community Well-Being (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.3, GL1.4) 
This project has conducted an analysis of the anticipated well-being impacts on all identified 
community groups within the project area. The assessment compares projected conditions under a 
“without-project” baseline scenario with those anticipated under full project implementation. 
In the without project scenario, without project intervention, as outlined in Section 4.1.4, the Siluk 
Project’s initial focus on selective logging and forest conversion is expected to negatively affect 
community well-being across economic, social, cultural, and health dimensions. Traditional livelihoods 
dependent on forest products would likely decline, and agricultural productivity would decrease due to 
land degradation. As a result, communities may increasingly rely on unsustainable practices such as 
illegal logging, shifting cultivation, wildlife hunting, and overfishing. 
These pressures are expected to lead to economic decline, erosion of cultural practices, growing 
conflicts over dwindling resources, and deteriorating health due to poor nutrition and environmental 
hazards. Vulnerable groups may experience deepening poverty and reduced resilience to external 
shocks such as climate change and economic instability. 
Under the with-project scenario, targeted interventions are introduced to promote sustainable 
livelihoods, strengthen community capacity, restore ecosystems, and generate employment 
opportunities. Key activities include the development of community enterprises, technical assistance, 
livelihood diversification, capacity building for women and youth, and job creation in restoration and 
rehabilitation programs.

4.3 Other Stakeholder Impacts  
4.3.1 Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, CM3.1) 
The SPCP is designed to promote constructive collaboration and complement the initiative of other 
stakeholders operating within the project zone, as detailed in Appendix 4: Stakeholder Description 
Table. Local NGOs working on environmental conservation and community development have been 
supporting the establishment of Village Forests (Hutan Desa/HD) and Customary Forests (Hutan 
Adat/HA) in areas adjacent to the Project Area. However, in accordance with national regulations, 
HD/HA designations cannot be established within areas covered by forest utilization licenses, including 
SPCP’s concession. This legal framework effectively prevents any spatial overlap. 
To ensure alignment and avoid potential conflicts, the SPCP has actively engaged with NGOs and 
relevant stakeholders. A key aspect of this coordination is the exchange of spatial data and clarification 
of land boundaries, which helps to ensure that current and future HD/HA applications do not interfere 
with project implementation. This ongoing collaboration demonstrates the project’s commitment to 
transparency, mutual understanding, and responsible land governance within the project zone.  
In addition to alignment with stakeholders, the SPCP is expected to generate several positive and 
sustainable outcomes.  Through initiatives such as Zero Burning Agriculture (ZBA) and forest fire 
management, the project will support district and provincial governments in reducing fire risks and 
associated environmental impacts. The CEPD program is aligned with poverty alleviation efforts in the 
region, directly addressing local socio-economic challenges. Furthermore, the project will stimulate the 
local economy by enhancing supply chain-market linkages and diversifying products. Collaborative 
community empowerment programs will build strong partnerships with NGOs and align with private 
actors and other organizations that share sustainability objectives. Collectively, these actions will 
deliver lasting benefits for local communities and enhance environmental resilience across the region.  
4.3.2 Mitigation of Negative Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, 
CM3.2) 
As described in Section 4.3.1, the project promotes collaborative partnerships especially on 
complementary efforts that contribute to improving overall well-being of communities in the project 
zone. Through such partnerships, impacts on other stakeholders are positive and no negative impacts 
have been identified requiring mitigation. The project proponents will continue to maintain regular 
engagement with other stakeholders, fostering open dialogue to build understanding, trust, and 
alignment with the project’s objectives and implementation.   

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
233 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
4.3.3 Net Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, CM3.3) 
The “without-project scenario” would involve the continuation of logging, canal construction, 
unsustainable agricultural practices, and forest degradation practices that provide short term financial 
gains at significant environmental costs. If the project is successful, non-community stakeholders stand 
to benefit from long term positive outcomes, as outlined in Section 4.3.1. These outcomes would 
represent substantial net-positive impacts for these stakeholders.   
4.4 Community Impact Monitoring  
4.4.1 Community Monitoring Plan (CCB, CM4.1, CM4.2, GL1.4, GL2.2, GL2.3, GL2.5) 
To ensure effective implementation and adaptive management, the SPCP has developed a participatory 
community monitoring plan align with project’s objectives. This plan systematically tracks project 
outputs, outcomes, and impacts on community well-being across the 14 target villages, with particular 
attention to differentiated effects on key targeted groups- including farmers, fishers, NTFP collectors, 
loggers, hunters and marginalized groups. 
Monitoring criteria are outlined in Table 49 and include both qualitative and quantitative methods. Data 
collection combines direct tracking of project activities with secondary sources such as village census 
records and government databases to ensure robust, triangulated analysis. 
A key focus is the evaluation of measures aimed at maintaining or enhancing High Conservation Value 
(HCV) attributes linked to community well-being. These include the protection of ecosystem services, 
cultural heritage sites, traditional knowledge systems, and equitable access to natural resources. 
Ground-truthing and spatial data verification will be conducted regularly, with community participation 
in identifying key HCV areas to prevent ecological or cultural degradation. 
To support Gold Level validation under the CCB Standards, the monitoring plan incorporates indicators 
that measure climate change adaptation and exceptional community benefits. For climate adaptation 
(GL1), the plan assesses household livelihood diversification through CEPD programs, sustainable 
practices such as zero burning agriculture, and the creation of green jobs through restoration and 
conservation activities.  
For exceptional community benefits (GL2), the plan uses disaggregated indicators to monitor the 
inclusion and well-being of women and other marginalize groups. This includes tracking their 
participation in livelihood programs, value chains and governance processes, as well as their access to 
ecosystem services that contribute to food security and income stability. 
Livelihood activities are monitored monthly, while all other aspects-including group governance, 
administration, financial reporting and overall progress are reviewed quarterly using standardized tools 
such as community groups surveys, focus group discussions, and key informant interviews. The results 
are shared transparently with communities to promote shared learning, strengthen accountability, and 
support evidence-based project adjustments.

4.5  Criterion: Exceptional Community Benefits  
4.5.1 Exceptional Community Criteria (CCB, GL2.1) 
According to the Human Development Index (HDI), Indonesia is classified as a country with Medium 
Human Development, ranking 108th out of 169 countries globally. The Indonesian Bureau of Statistics 
(Badan Pusat Statistik) defines the national poverty line for West Kalimantan Province as the minimum 
purchasing power required per capita to afford both staple food and non-food items, equivalent to IDR 
454,652 per month. A social baseline assessment conducted by the SPCP Project found that over 50% 
of village households earn an average monthly income of approximately IDR 2,000,000. With an 
average household size of 4.6 persons in West Kalimantan, this equates to a per capita income of IDR 
434,782, below the established national poverty line. Therefore, the SPCP Project Zone qualifies as a 
rural area where more than 50% of households live below the national poverty line, meeting the criteria 
for CCB Gold Level consideration under the Community Exceptional Criteria. 
To support community resilience and meet the CCB Gold Level Climate Change Adaptation criteria, the 
project implements integrated actions that strengthen sustainable livelihoods, enhance market access, 
build local capacities, and create green employment opportunities. Communities are supported to 
transition from environmentally harmful practices toward zero burning agriculture thereby reducing 
pressure on ecosystems. Improved market linkages promote income stability and economic security, 
while targeted capacity-building programs empower farmers, fishers, hunters, loggers, women and 
marginalized groups with skills in conservation and climate adaptation. Furthermore, employment 
opportunities in ecosystem restoration provide inclusive and sustainable alternatives to 
environmentally degrading activities. Together, these interventions enhance the community’s long-term 
adaptive capacity, strengthen socio-economic resilience, and improve well-being across all social 
groups (detailed in Section 4.2.3). 
4.5.2 Short-term and Long-term Community Benefits (CCB, GL2.2) 
The project is expected to generate both short-term and long-term net positive well-being benefits for 
communities. By aligning interventions with locally identified priorities and embedding sustainability 
principles into environmental and economic practices, the project will contribute to immediate 
improvements in livelihoods, capacity, and access to employment, while also strengthening long-term 
community resilience, resource security and intergenerational well-being. The anticipated well-being 
benefits are detailed in Section 4.2.3 and Appendix 4: Stakeholder Description Table. A summary of 
these short- and long-term benefits from project activities is presented in the table below.

4.5.3 Community Participation Risks (CCB, GL2.3) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
240 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The SPCP Project is designed to deliver a broad range of socio-economic benefits to communities, with 
a strong emphasis on reaching the most vulnerable and marginalized groups. The project’s approach, 
including outreach to the poor and socially excluded, is described in Appendix 4: Stakeholder 
Description Table. Positive outcomes are expected in the form of improved market access, increased 
employment, and strengthened community capacity. These impacts depend on inclusive participation, 
transparency, and effective implementation. Through the social baseline assessment, three key barriers 
were identified, each with planned mitigation strategies: 
• Limited Participation: Marginalized groups often reside in remote areas and lack access to 
information or community decision-making processes. Meeting participants are frequently 
selected by local leaders, which can exclude poorer or less connected households. To ensure 
inclusive representation, the project will engage village-level facilitators, apply participatory 
planning methods, and hold meetings at times and locations accessible to all groups. Active 
validation of marginalized group participation will help ensure their voices are integrated into 
planning and implementation. 
• Elite Capture: In the absence of transparency and balanced representation, there is a risk that 
decisions, such as allocation of project benefits could be influenced by individuals acting in 
their own interests. The project will address this by promoting diverse and equitable community 
representation, particularly from marginalized households. Public access to meeting records 
and decision-making processes will be ensured to create checks and balances, discourage 
favouritism, and foster trust across all stakeholder groups. 
• Weak Project Management: Inadequate human and financial capacity, along with poor 
oversight, may limit the project’s ability to deliver benefits effectively, especially to marginalized 
groups. To mitigate this, the project will provide training in financial and operational 
management, strengthen monitoring and evaluation frameworks, and implement adaptive, 
community-based management approaches. These steps aim to improve accountability, ensure 
resources are used efficiently, and build local capacity for long-term sustainability. 
4.5.4 Marginalized and/or Vulnerable Community Groups (CCB, GL2.4) 
SPCP, during the socio-economy baseline assessment, has included community members categorized 
as marginalized and vulnerable groups in the project area including women, persons with disabilities, 
people with limited access to information or decision making, and elders who often overlooked and 
excluded from development programs. The project specifically targets these groups to ensure their 
active participation in groups meetings, livelihood initiatives programs and decision-making processes.  
The approach for ensuring the inclusion of marginalized groups is detailed in Section 4.5.3, with an 
emphasis on promoting meaningful engagement and representation. 
The following table outlines the targeted community groups, highlighting the anticipated net positive 
impacts, strategies to enhance benefit access, and measures to identify and mitigate any potential 
negative impacts. 
Community Group  Women

participation.

from decision-making are monitored. Mitigation includes 
assigning local facilitators to support their involvement and 
ensuring age-sensitive formats for participation. 
 
4.5.5 Net Impacts on Women (CCB, GL2.5) 
Although women have historically been involved in the community level engagement, their roles remain 
limited. Decisions are predominantly made by men, whether in technical, administrative or financial 
matters. Women often serve only as institutional figures. To promote gender equality, amplify women’s 
voices, and encourage female leadership, the project will focus on several key areas:  
a. Economic empowerment   
• Improved livelihoods and increase income opportunities: through the project, women will 
be engaged in sustainable and diversified livelihood practices, including broiler and egg-
laying chicken farming, egg-laying duck farming, fish farming, maize farming, and zero-
burning land management, supported through CEPD programs.    
b. Social and Gender Equity 
• Reduction in gender disparities: the project is designed with a gender sensitive approach 
to reduce inequalities, ensuring women have equitable access to resources, land and 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
243 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
training opportunities. Women’s representation is prioritized, with a minimum quota of 
30% women required in every group. This requirement is formally stated in Cooperation 
Agreement/PKS for the CEPD programs. 
• Increased decision-making power: the project will promote women’s involvement in 
leadership and decision-making processes, empowering them to take a more active role in 
community governance and resources management. In the 14 villages, women’s role and 
presence are highly valued. The project has established CEPD groups, with women making 
up more than 50% of the members. One of these groups is led by a women. This structure 
promotes women’s active involvement in leadership and decision making processes 
related to community governance and resource management. Women also hold key roles 
such as business coordinators, secretaries and treasures, and are actively engaged in all 
stages of the project- from planning and implementation to monitoring.  
c. Capacity building  
• Skill development: the project includes training and capacity building programs, enabling 
women within the project area gain skills in sustainable practices, resource management, 
leadership and entrepreneurship. Additionally, women empowerment is enhanced by 
strengthening group’s capacity through training in administration and financial 
management, as well as technical training in chicken farming, maize farming and fisheries. 
Women’s groups are also encouraged to hold regular meetings for planning and evaluating 
activities.  
4.5.6 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (CCB, GL2.6) 
While the project does not impact property rights, it does fully acknowledge and respect that local 
communities have historically dependent on the project area for their livelihoods. The project also 
recognizes the strong cultural and socio-economic ties between the forest and the surrounding 
communities. In this context, SPCP is committed to ensuring that these communities continue to 
benefit from the project’s presence and activities. To support this commitment, the project prioritizes 
in-kind support as a primary mechanism for delivering these benefits, aligning with local contexts and 
community-defined priorities. 
This commitment is operationalized through a range of benefit sharing initiatives including community 
enterprise development programs, improved access to market, training in sustainable agriculture 
practices and capacity building support. These initiatives were designed through participatory 
approaches that incorporate community input gathered during consultations, with an emphasis on 
addressing immediate needs while fostering long term impacts on community well-being.  
Benefit allocation and eligibility criteria are communicated transparently through consultation 
processes to ensure community understanding and trust. The project also adopts adaptive 
management to continuously strengthen its benefit-sharing approach, ensuring they remain relevant to 
the evolving needs and priorities of the community throughout its implementation. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

4.5.7 Benefits, Costs, and Risks Communication (VCS, 3.18; CCB, GL2.7) 
Effectively communicating relevant and adequate information about project activities to community 
members is a critical component of the implementation strategy. Community members are engaged 
through various participatory methods such as community meetings, focus group discussions (FGDs), 
and workshops. These forums serve not only to inform but also to facilitate two-way communication, 
enabling community members to ask questions, seek clarification, and provide input on the information 
presented. 
As part of this process, written materials in Bahasa Indonesia, such as brochures outlining the overall 
project, including the Community Development Program, have been developed and distributed. These 
brochures are shared during initial socialization sessions and ongoing Free, Prior, and Informed 
Consent (FPIC) processes. They include essential information such as the company profile and a 
description of activities planned and those already undertaken. This approach ensures that community 
members fully understand the project’s objectives and methods, which is essential for informed 
participation, successful adoption of project activities, and improved long-term outcomes. 
4.5.8 Governance and Implementation Structures (CCB, GL2.8) 
To ensure inclusive and accountable community engagement, the project has established a community 
engagement governance framework. This includes the formation of a Community Engagement (CE) 
Department and a Social Performance (SP) Unit, which work together to promote transparency, 
participation, and responsiveness throughout project implementation. 
At the village level, the project has deployed facilitators across all 14 participating villages. These 
facilitators serve as direct liaisons between the project and local stakeholders, ensuring that 
community perspectives, needs, and priorities are consistently integrated into planning and 
implementation. Their presence helps build trust, fosters mutual understanding, and reinforce local 
ownership of project activities. 
To promote accountability and enhance responsiveness, the project is in the process of establishing a 
formal grievance mechanism, which is expected to be operational in 2025. This system will enable 
community members to submit complaints or concerns, which will be documented, tracked, and 
addressed in a timely and transparently manner. This mechanism is intended to play a key role in 
maintaining trust and ensuring the project remains aligned with community expectations. 
The governance framework is designed to support full and effective participation through regular 
village-level consultations, participatory decision-making processes, and ongoing capacity-building 
initiatives. The project also facilitates the formation of community groups — particularly in livelihood 
enterprise development and conservation-restoration activities — as platforms for aggregating local 
stakeholders. These groups serve as representative structures that enable collective decision-making, 
implementation, and monitoring, reinforcing self-governance and long-term sustainability.

• Community Enterprise Program Development, where communities actively shape and 
implement livelihood initiatives; 
• Regular feedback sessions and community meetings, providing a platform for dialogue on 
project implementation and addressing operational challenges; 
• Operational grievance redress channels, allowing concerns to be raised and resolved in a 
structured and timely manner.

Burning Agriculture (ZBA). This technique is expected to be implemented beyond the project 
area and adopted by other community members in surrounding regions.

5 BIODIVERSITY 
5.1 Without-Project Biodiversity Scenario  
5.1.1 Existing Conditions (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B1.1) 
SPCP is located within the Borneo Lowland Rainforests Ecoregion. This ecoregion encompasses an area 
with complex ecological characteristics, ranging from peat swamp forests to lowland tropical rainforests 
and mountains. The project zone and project area lie within the transition zone primarily consists of 
lowland tropical rainforests and peat swamp forests, serving as habitats for various endemic, Critical 
and Endangered species, such as Bornean Orangutan (Pongo pygmeaus pygmaeus), Proboscis Monkey 
(Nasalis larvatus), Bornean Banded Langur (Presbytis chrysomelas), Rhinoceros Hornbill (Buceros 
rhinoceros), Hairy-Nosed Otters (Lutra sumatrana), Wreathed Hornbill (Rhyticeros undulatus), Bornean 
Crestless Fireback (Lophura pyronota) and other RTE species (Baseline Study 2023).  
In the absence of the project, the forests of the project area were threatened by commercial logging 
and conversion of peatlands into timber plantations. Peatland will be drained leading to changes in the 
natural balance of the hydrological regime of the area and increase threat to fires during prolonged 
drought sessions. Logging and forest conversion will remove the habitat for many of the protected 
biodiversity forcing the wildlife moving out to other areas, and if not possible the population will be lost. 
In order to assess the biodiversity baseline of the project area, a range of survey activities were 
conducted within 2022-2023. These included transect line survey, camera trapping monitoring, 
orangutan population estimate using nest counting, herpetofauna survey, fish species and avifauna. 
The collected data was used as the baseline to determine the condition of the species diversity and 
determine strategy to conserve and monitor the biodiversity, most surveys were conducted using the 
simple line transect methods following tracks, road and rivers. For orangutan population estimation, 
nest counts were employed, and their distribution was then analysed using the Maxent application to 
assess habitat suitability. 
The camera trapping survey began in 2022 and, as of December 2024, has accumulated over 2,700 
trap days across a three-year period. A total of 40 camera units—comprising Bushnell Trophy Cam E3 
Essential Trail Cameras and Browning Dark Ops Extreme Cameras—were deployed using a grid-based 
cluster sampling approach. Each camera was placed within 40 distinct 1x1 km grid clusters, ensuring 
broad spatial coverage. 
Due to limitations in the number of available camera traps, a rotational sampling method was 
implemented. Each camera remained in a given cluster for 105 days before being re-evaluated and re-
located to a new cluster, repeating the cycle to maximize data collection across different habitats. This 
rotation will continue until a sufficient number of camera traps are acquired, allowing for permanent 
placement within all clusters. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
247 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Project staff also collect data on any possible threats found during the survey including sign of wildlife 
hunting, live-trapping, habitat disturbance, encroachment, and wildlife trade. All data collected is used 
to determine conservation action and monitoring. 
The outcome of these surveys is shown in Appendix 6-9. This included at least 39 mammals, 120 birds, 
19 amphibians, 21 reptiles, 78 freshwater fish, and 353 flora species. Based on the IUCN Red List 
(2024), there are 19 species classified as Critically Endangered (CR), 14 species as Endangered (EN), 
49 species as Vulnerable (VU), and 58 species as Near Threatened (NT). According to the Minister of 
Environment and Forestry Regulation No.106 of 2018 regarding the Second Amendment to Minister of 
Environment and Forestry Regulation No. P.20 of 2018 concerning Protected Plant and Animal Species, 
there are at least 51 species in the area protected by the Indonesian government. Meanwhile, in terms 
of trade status referring to CITES Appendix, at least 12 species are listed under App.I, 32 species under 
App.II, and 2 species under App.III. The species categorized as rare, endangered, and protected are 
presented in Appendix 6-10. Additionally, from an assessment of the Bornean orangutan population 
(Pongo pygmaeus pygmaeus), the current estimated population size is approximately 180 individuals 
(range: 98-329) with a density estimate of about 0.5 individuals per km2 (range: 0.27-0.93).

5.1.3 Without-project Scenario: Biodiversity (CCB, B1.3) 
The baseline scenario for the project, including commercial drainage, logging and land clearance for

monitoring approach. Section 5.4 provides further details. 
Prior to the project's initiation, parts of the area had been drained by previous concession owners to 
lower the water table, facilitating logging operations. This activity ceased when the concession license 
was ended. However, if the project does not proceed, drainage is expected to continue under the 
baseline scenario agents pursuing similar operations to the prior of project initiation. The dominant

land cover in the project area remains natural forest, rather than invasive species. (see Section 2.1.14

In the envisioned with-project scenario, protection and conservation to maintain and improve the 
condition of existing populations and habitats both in terms of size or extent, distribution and quality 
will have a positive impact on biodiversity in the project area/zone. Concrete measures to reduce 
habitat degradation and fragmentation by reducing several external and internal threats/pressure 
factors such as encroachment, illegal logging, and forest and land fires are expected to ensure the 
viability of species populations. In the long term, degraded areas that are successfully restored will 
form connectivity (corridors) between previously fragmented habitat patches, will also enable/enabling 
colonization and provide space for the movement of species that require a wide home range such as 
the Bornean orangutan (P. Pygmaeus pygmaeus) and other primate species (e.g. Hylobates muelleri,

catchers are expected to increase knowledge and awareness as well as positive behavioral changes. 
This positive change will encourage the practice of utilizing NTFPs and sustainable agricultural 
cultivation of local communities. Ultimately, sustainable practices by local communities are expected to 
have a significant positive impact on biodiversity conservation in the project area. 
Implementation of the proposed project scenario is poised to foster the survival and eventual recovery 
of populations belonging to various species of conservation concern. In the absence of this scenario, 
these species would face severe depletion or complete elimination from the landscape, as outlined in 
the baseline scenario.

Justification of change 
In the absence of the project, the area is likely to be commercially 
logged and drained, with parts cleared for timber plantations. The 
removal of forest cover will impact forest-dwelling wildlife, as well as 
species that rely on forest resources for food and shelter. Some 
species may face local extinction, as very few will be able to relocate 
to nearby habitats due to the limited remaining intact peat swamp 
forest. This destruction will result in habitat fragmentation, 
preventing species from using the project area as a natural corridor 
to adjacent habitats. 
As the forest is opened, encroachment for settlement and cultivation 
is expected to increase. Areas that were once difficult to access will 
become more exposed. These pressures, combined with continued 
demand for the exotic pet and traditional medicine markets, will 
further threaten biodiversity. Local extinctions could disrupt food 
chains—if a species has no substitute, its loss may have cascading 
effects on other species. 
Under the Baseline Scenario, species conservation programs are 
unlikely to be implemented regularly or according to rigorous 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
254 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
scientific standards. Most monitoring efforts may exist only on 
paper, lacking field surveys or consultation with experts. This could 
lead to biased and inaccurate data on species diversity and 
identification.

Biodiversity element 
HCV 2: Landscape-level ecosystems and mosaics 
Estimated change 
Without the implementation of this project, the forested areas within 
the project boundary would have faced extensive degradation 
through drainage, logging, and partial conversion to monoculture 
plantations. Such land-use changes would have led to the 
fragmentation of the contiguous landscape mosaic, undermining the 
very attributes that qualify the region under HCV 2. The integrity of 
this vast ecosystem complex—ranging from montane to lowland 
habitats—relies on the maintenance of ecological connectivity and 
uninterrupted natural processes across large spatial scales. 
The absence of protection would have compromised key ecosystem 
functions, including habitat continuity for wide-ranging species, 
natural hydrological cycles, and carbon storage. Fragmentation and 
simplification of the landscape through monoculture development 
would have disrupted species movement, reduced genetic exchange, 
and increased vulnerability to external stressors such as fires and 
invasive species. Over time, this would erode the landscape-level 
resilience and biodiversity that HCV 2 seeks to safeguard. 
In contrast, the project’s strategy to protect and restore the entire 
area directly contributes to preserving these critical ecosystem 
linkages—ensuring that the mosaic remains functional, biodiverse, 
and ecologically robust.

activities the project will implement to ensure habitat protection and 
restoration is provided in Section 2.1.17.

Biodiversity element 
HCV 3: Ecosystems and habitats 
Estimated change 
To uphold the values of HCV 3, the project will implement targeted 
measures to protect and restore rare and threatened ecosystems 
within the project area, particularly tropical peatlands and 
seasonally inundated forests. These ecosystems are prioritized due 
to their ecological sensitivity, limited regional extent, and importance 
for biodiversity and climate stability. 
The project will prevent further degradation through the strict 
protection of intact habitats and the enforcement of no-drainage and 
no-conversion policies across peat-dominated zones. Restoration 
efforts will focus on rewetting drained peatlands to recover their 
hydrological functions, reestablishing native vegetation cover, and 
mitigating fire risks through community-based fire prevention and 
monitoring. 
Where degradation has occurred, the project will undertake 
ecological rehabilitation using site-appropriate methods that aim to 
reintroduce native species and reestablish the structure and 
function of these ecosystems. These interventions will be integrated 
with long-term monitoring to track recovery, ensure effectiveness, 
and guide adaptive management. 
By aligning these actions with its commitments under HCV 2, the 
project ensures that the protection of threatened ecosystems is not 
isolated but forms part of a comprehensive, landscape-level 
conservation strategy. At the start of the project, the area 
encompassed approximately 28,291.33 ha of forest, 214.77 ha of 
non-forest, 16,196.34 ha of undrained peat, and 826.65 ha of 
drained peat. Under the baseline scenario for the first crediting 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
256 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
period (through 2051), these land cover types are expected to 
change as outlined in Section 3. 
Justification of change 
Without the implementation of the project, the ecosystems qualifying 
as HCV 3—particularly the tropical peatlands and seasonally 
inundated forests—would face significant and likely irreversible 
degradation. These rare habitats are already under pressure from 
regional land-use trends, and without protective measures, they 
would be highly vulnerable to drainage, logging, and conversion to 
monoculture plantations. 
Peatland ecosystems, which are central to this HCV 3 designation, 
would be at immediate risk of further drainage. This would trigger 
rapid oxidation of peat layers, releasing substantial amounts of 
greenhouse gases and greatly diminishing the area's capacity for 
carbon storage. Loss of hydrological integrity would also reduce 
water retention and increase the likelihood of peat fires—events that 
are notoriously difficult to control and devastating in both ecological 
and social terms. 
In parallel, biodiversity supported by these rare ecosystems would 
be severely impacted. Many endemic species rely on intact peat 
swamp forests and flooded forest systems; once these habitats are 
altered or fragmented, they are unlikely to support the same 
diversity or ecological interactions. Species with narrow ecological 
niches, low dispersal ability, or already-declining populations would 
be at especially high risk of local extinction. 
Moreover, the breakdown of these ecosystems would compromise 
ecosystem services that extend well beyond biodiversity: water 
regulation, climate moderation, and cultural values tied to natural 
landscapes. Without intervention, what remains of these 
endangered ecosystems could cross ecological thresholds beyond 
recovery, undermining both conservation goals and local community 
well-being. 
For further details on the baseline scenario and its contributing 
factors, refer to Sections 3.1.4 and 3.1.5. Information on the actions 
and activities the project will implement to ensure habitat protection 
and restoration is provided in Section 2.1.17. The methods and 
metrics used for monitoring forest and peat conditions are 
addressed through the project’s climate monitoring framework, 
detailed in Section 3.1. Biodiversity monitoring approaches are 
summarized below in Section 5.4.1.

5.2.2 Mitigation Measures (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.3) 
Within the project area, there are no known or anticipated negative impacts on biodiversity resulting 
from project activities. However, mitigation efforts will be focused on identifying threats, pressures, 
risks, and implementing preventive measures. Continuous monitoring will be conducted to detect any 
potential impact as early as possible.  
Mitigation efforts will include, but are not limited to protection patrols, forest and land fire control, and 
restoration and enrichment of habitat with native species. Additional measures will aim to prevent 
habitat destruction, such as the use of fire to land clearing, and habitat pollution, particularly in aquatic 
ecosystems, caused by hazardous chemicals such as pesticides and chemical fertilizers.  
Unsustainable practices, including poison fishing and overfishing by local communities, will also be 
addressed. All of these actions are intended to maintain and enhance biodiversity within the project 
area and will be guided with the precautionary principle to uphold ecosystem balance and reduce risks 
to biodiversity. 
Project measures to maintain and enhance High Conservation Values (HCVs) are outlined in Sections 
5.1.2 and 5.2.1. Specifically, regarding designated HCV areas, the project’s primary objective is to 
preserve natural ecosystems in their original state. This objective aligns fully with the precautionary 
principle, which is commonly applied to anticipate and prevent potential negative impacts from 
activities that may exploit, modify, or inadvertently alter naturally occurring ecosystems. 
Accordingly, all community-based activities carried out within the project zone—such as shifts in 
agricultural or fisheries practices—will be subject to feasibility assessments including its environmental 
impact that reflect this precautionary approach. Additional details on these assessments are provided 
in Section 4. 
5.2.3 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B2.2, GL1.4) 
Assessing the project's impact on biodiversity within the area, it is anticipated to result in a net positive 
effect. A key strategy in achieving this outcome is the preservation and expansion of forest cover, 
ensuring adequate habitat and biodiversity representation. When looking at the importance of this area 
from the perspective of its function as a wildlife corridor, especially for species such as the Bornean 
orangutan, the project zone shows significant potential. This zone has the ability to connect populations 
in meta-populations, creating a critical path between the Danau Sentarum and Betung Kerihun areas 
and the surrounding area. This connection is important for the survival and development of the species, 
forming a system that supports vital genetic movement and exchange. 
In addition, the project zone is also identified as a potential refugium area. This means that this area 
can function as a refuge for relict populations in surrounding habitat patches that may be remote or 
vulnerable. Thus, this project not only plays a role in maintaining current biodiversity, but also in 
supporting biodiversity and adaptation to possible impacts of climate change in the future. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
258 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The anticipated impacts of the project in relation to each criterion are presented in Sections 5.2.1 and 
5.5.2, respectively. 
5.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, B2.4) 
As outlined in Section 5.5.2, none of the activities undertaken during the project will have a negative 
impact on High Conservation Values (HCVs) within the project area. On the contrary, all project 
interventions are specifically designed to enhance HCV attributes and safeguard populations of key and 
trigger species during the project implementation phase. Several strategic actions were taken to ensure 
conservation and enhancement of biodiversity. Protection patrols were increased to maintain the 
integrity of the area, and rehabilitation measures such as enrichment and planting of native species as 
well as peat rewetting were carried out in degraded areas. 
Furthermore, agroforestry conservation initiatives in degraded areas provide a sustainable approach to 
managing natural resources. Canal blocking was integrated to support aquatic ecosystems and 
increase their resilience to climate change. Biodiversity survey activities were used to monitor and 
identify impacts of project initiatives on local flora and fauna. 
All these measures were designed to support the enhancement of values and positive impacts on 
biodiversity and local communities. A bioresource-based approach in livelihood development for local 
communities was prioritized to create a sustainable balance and provide mutual benefits between the 
community and the project. Thus, this project ensures sustainability and well-being for the biodiversity 
and local communities involved. 
5.2.5 Species Used (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.5, B2.6) 
The project is committed to using only native species in the revegetation program and will not introduce 
any known invasive or non-native species (Giesen 2004; Randi et al. 2014).  To safeguard the local 
ecosystem, the project will implement regular monitoring to ensure that all species incorporated align 
with the natural biodiversity of the area.

5.2.6 Invasive Species (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.5) 
No invasive species have been identified in the project area. However, the project will conduct regular 
monitoring to ensure early detection. If any invasive species are found in the future, the project will 
record this and take appropriate measures to control their spread and mitigate potential impacts on the 
ecosystem. 
5.2.7 GMO Exclusion (CCB, B2.7) 
No GMOs are used in this project. 
5.2.8 Inputs Justification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.8) 
SPCP does not use fertilizers, chemical pesticides, biological control agents, or other chemical inputs in 
its project activities. However, it recognizes that some communities have long relied on these 
substances. To address this, the project will implement awareness-raising initiatives to inform 
communities about the risks associated with chemical use, promote the reduction or elimination of 
such practices, and encourage the adoption of organic and environmentally friendly alternatives. 
5.2.9 Waste Products (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.9) 
The project will identify waste generated from its activities, which includes office waste (e.g., paper, 
plastics, electronics), transportation waste (e.g., oil cans, fuel containers), food waste from field surveys 
(e.g., packaging, leftovers), and medical waste (e.g., infusion-related items like syringes and IV bags). 
Proper management of each waste type is essential. 
The project will classify waste into categories: hazardous (e.g., oil cans, batteries, medical waste), non-
hazardous (e.g., office waste, food waste), biodegradable (e.g., food scraps, organic materials), 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
261 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
recyclable (e.g., plastics, metals), and non-recyclable waste. For hazardous waste, the project will 
establish partnerships with certified disposal companies. 
The project will develop a waste management plan and is fully committed to its implementation. This 
would include waste segregation and disposal management in an environmentally responsible manner 
as well as exploring opportunity for recycle-re-use. 
5.3 Offsite Biodiversity Impacts  
5.3.1 Negative Offsite Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B3.1) and Mitigation Measures (CCB, 
B3.2) 
The project was designed to protect and restore the biodiversity population within the project area while 
extending conservation efforts to its wider Project Zone. Potential risk of Leakage effects, where 
hunters, loggers and encroachers targeted by the project program might relocate the same activity 
elsewhere. By providing viable opportunities within the project framework, the likelihood of them 
continuing their illegal activity elsewhere is significantly reduced. 
 
Negative offsite impact  Mitigation measure(s) 
The main negative impact 
outside the project location 
(offsite) is the potential for 
leakage effects, where 
individuals involved in (illegal 
activities such as illegal logging 
and hunting within the project 
area move to other areas and 
carry out similar activities, 
especially targeting valuable tree 
and protected species in the 
alternative location.  
The off-site forest area, as crucial part of the peat swamp 
forest and secondary dryland lowland forest in Kapuas Hulu 
Regency, providing ecological significance such as carbon 
storage, water regulation and biodiversity conservation. These 
forests are formally protected as the Kapuas Hulu Highlands 
Area-Based National Park (TNBKDS) which aims to preserve 
their ecological integrity.  
While the formal protection status reduces the risk of 
leakage, certain threats, such as illegal logging, 
encroachment and habitat degradation, persist due to 
enforcement challenges. Studies on forest conversion 
patterns in the region indicate that protected areas still 
experience resource extraction pressure, particularly from 
activities driven by weak regulatory oversight or economic 
demand. Additionally, local reports have documented 
instances of unauthorized land clearing and selective logging, 
signalling that protection alone may not fully prevent leakage 
effects. 
Implementation of risk mitigation measures needs to be 
carried out although providing an accurate picture of the 
impacts at the project location (on site) compared to the 
impacts at other locations (off site) may be a challenge.

will be critical in ensuring that forest conservation efforts 
remain effective in mitigating biodiversity loss and habitat

In anticipation, the Project will develop sustainable alternative 
livelihood program including agriculture, livestock farming, 
entrepreneurship and employment opportunities, aimed at 
transitioning individuals awa from harmful practices such as 
wildlife hunting, illegal logging and forest conversion. By 
providing viable economic alternatives, the initiative seeks to 
reduce environmental degradation while promoting long-term 
community resilience. 
5.3.2 Net Offsite Biodiversity Benefits (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B3.3) 
As outlined in Section 5.3.1, offsite negative impacts on biodiversity are expected to be minimal or 
absent. In contrast, the project is anticipated to deliver substantial positive biodiversity outcomes within 
both the Project Area and the broader Project Zone—benefits that represent one of the project’s core 
overarching objectives. Nonetheless, the project recognizes the possibility of unforeseen or unmitigated 
negative impacts on biodiversity, particularly in areas adjacent to the project boundary, where indirect 
pressures such as displacement of land use or increased access may emerge. 
To evaluate these potential offsite impacts, the project will apply a combination of spatial analysis (e.g., 
land cover change detection using remote sensing), stakeholder consultations, and ecological 
monitoring to detect shifts in species distribution, habitat integrity, or resource use beyond project 
boundaries. These observations will be compared with biodiversity gains measured within the Project 
Zone, including improved habitat quality, increased connectivity, and population recovery of trigger 
species. The project will use established biodiversity indicators and baseline comparisons (see Sections 
5.4.1 and 5.5.2) to quantify changes. 
The findings from this comparative assessment will be integrated into the project’s adaptive 
management framework. Where needed, mitigation measures will be extended to buffer zones or 
addressed through local partnerships. Given the extensive conservation actions within the Project 
Area—including habitat restoration, peatland rewetting, and community-based protection efforts—the 
project expects the net effect on biodiversity to be strongly positive. This conclusion is supported by the 
scale and intensity of in-zone interventions relative to the limited and closely monitored risks beyond 
the zone. 
5.4 Biodiversity Impact Monitoring  
5.4.1 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan (CCB, B4.1, B4.2, GL1.4, GL3.4) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
263 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
SPCP project will implement monitoring plans to monitor species population and stability of key 
species. This will be measured using several indices and metrics such as population size, density, 
species richness index, diversity, abundance, and occupancy. Along with these parameters, we plan to 
monitor threats to biodiversity that usually come from outside the project area, such as illegal 
hunting/fishing/hunting (unsustainable use of wildlife species), fire, and encroachment. The area to be 
monitored includes 22 permanent plots, 22 km monitoring transects within the project area/zone grid 
(see Figure 52). 
 
Figure 52. Biodiversity monitoring map 
The sampling method is distance survey of orangutan nests, line transects, camera-trapping, visual 
encounter survey, point counts/point transects. Threat measurement will be measured using 
household surveys and interviews with local communities (Threat Reduction Assessment). In addition, 
the level of threat is also assessed from the results of forest protection patrol data analysis (Siluk Patrol 
System, hereinafter abbreviated SPS). For a detailed Biodiversity Monitoring Plan, see Appendix 11: 
Biodiversity Monitoring Plan. 
The development of alternative livelihoods within the community, particularly for fishermen in Embaloh 
hilir District is expected to positively impact biodiversity, especially fresh-water fish populations. 
Additionally, increasing community awareness will help enhance knowledge and encourage a shift away 
from unsustainable fishing towards more sustainable methods. For instance, destructive fishing 
techniques and tools such as rotenone and electric fishing will be phased out, and protected species 
such as belida will be no longer harvested. 
Enhanced protection efforts will target/focus primarily on Bornean orangutan populations, alongside 
proboscis monkeys and Bornean Banded Langurs. These populations are the primary targets of 
bushmeat hunting after the decline of ungulate/wild boar populations due to the peak of the African

Swine Fever (ASF) pandemic in 2021. By reducing hunting pressure/threats, these populations will 
recover.

5.4.2 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, B4.3) 
Biodiversity monitoring plans will be incorporated in the Project Document and its subsequent 
Monitoring Report alongside other relevant project information, as outlined in Sections 2.3.1 to 2.3.5,

5.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits  
5.5.1 High Biodiversity Conservation Priority Status (CCB, GL3.1) 
This project seeks to be validated to the Gold Level/Standard for exceptional biodiversity benefit 
because it meets the criteria of vulnerability and irreplaceability. 
The project area meets the criteria of vulnerability with the presence of at least 12 species included in 
the Critical (CR) category, 14 species with endangered status (EN), 38 Vulnerable species, 38 Near 
Threatened (NT) species. Meanwhile, based on the Regulation of the Minister of Environment and 
Forestry No. 106 of 2018 concerning the Second Amendment to the Regulation of the Minister of 
Environment and Forestry No. P.20 2018 concerning Protected Plant and Animal Species, there are at 
least 47 species protected by the Indonesian government. Meanwhile, the trade status refers to the 
CITES Appendix, at least 11 species are included in the App.I list, 31 species in App.II, and 2 species 
are included in App.III. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
270 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The Bornean orangutan subspecies Pongo pygmaeus pygmaeus—which has the smallest population 
size and the most restricted distribution among the Bornean orangutan subspecies—is considered 
resident within the project area. Due to its limited range and critically endangered status, this 
population may meet the irreplaceability criterion under HCV 1. The peat swamp forest within the 
project zone serves as a key habitat for this subspecies, further emphasizing the urgency of its 
protection. 
In addition to the orangutan, it is suspected that the 1% irreplaceability threshold may also be met by 
several other endemic and conservation-dependent species, including the Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis 
larvatus), Bornean Banded Langur (Presbytis chrysomelas), Bornean Gibbon (Hylobates muelleri), and 
various hornbill species. These species are closely associated with intact peat swamp and lowland 
forest ecosystems. However, at present, sufficient population data to confirm their irreplaceability 
status is lacking. Further field surveys and species-specific assessments will be required to 
substantiate these assumptions and inform conservation prioritization. 
5.5.2 Trigger Species Population Trends (CCB, GL3.2, GL3.3) 
Trigger species (those listed as Critically Endangered, or Endangered) are shown in Appendix 6: list of 
mid-large mammals in the project area–Appendix 10: List Flora found within project area and in the 
table below, which includes population trends (where known) and anticipated with- and without-project 
scenarios. As more trigger species are identified at the site, they will be incorporated to this list. 
Trigger species 
Orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus pygmaeus) 
The Bornean orangutan (P. pygmaeus pygmaeus) is the least studied 
sub-species in Kalimantan and also has the most restricted 
population and distribution characterized by fragmented and patchy 
population compared to other Kalimantan orangutan sub-species. 
This makes its population the most critical among Kalimantan 
Orangutan subspecies (Utami-Atmoko et al., 2017). 
Population trend at start of 
project 
According to the 2016 Population and Habitat Viability Analysis 
(PHVA), the total wild population of P. pygmaeus pygmaeus is 
estimated at 4,490 individuals, primarlyconcentrated in TNBKDS and 
the Batang Ai-Lanjak-Entimau Landscape (Sarawak). About 680 
individuals of these population, dispersed in Danau Sentarum and 
the Labian-Leboyan corridor, which  connects Danau Sentarum with 
Betung Kerihun through the Embaloh Watershed (Utami-Atmoko et 
al., 2017). 
A 2023 population study of orangutans and habitat conducted in the 
project area (PT ASK) estimated 180 individuals with a density of 0.5 
ind km
-
² (0.27-0.93). This figure remains above the Minimum Viable 
Population (MVP) threshold for Kalimantan, ensuring population 
sustainability for the next 100 years (Utami-Atmoko et al., 2017). 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
271 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
However, the development of trans-border roads since 2014 poses 
significant threat, potentially impeding the movement of orangutans 
between the lower forest blocks (Nanga Lauk, Nanga Nyabau, and 
Nanga Palin) and the upper blocks (Seluan and Nanga Awin).  
Without-project scenario 
Population monitoring in and around TNBKDS reveals a significant 
decline over time. Data from 1996 (Russon et al., 2001) estimated a 
population of 1,578 individuals, with a density of 2.86 individuals per 
km². However, population assessments conducted between 2010 and 
2014 (Yuliani et al., 2023) show a drastic reduction, with only 72 
individuals recorded, and a density dropping to 0.15 individuals per 
km².The continuation of the baseline scenario will worsen existing, 
further adcelrating the already declining population trend that begin 
before the baseline scenario was in effect.  Habitat disturbance will 
significantly impact species survival. 
Our 2023 population assessment confirms this trend, aligning with 
indicators of a shrinking population size.  
Without the project, the population faces isolation or complete loss, 
as habitat fragmentation intensifies. While some individuals from the 
southern side may attempt to cross the trans-national road to the 
northern side, the likelihood is extremely low. The already small 
population, which is below the carrying capacity, remains increasingly 
isolated on the southern side due to settlements, roads, and 
expanding community activities, such as farming and land use 
changes. 
With-project scenario 
The project will implement targeted conservation activities to 
maintain and enhance the current orangutan population. 
Conservation efforts will focus on supporting a relatively small 
population to maintain the value still on the above of the minimum 
viable population (MVP) or carrying capacity, ensuring long-term 
survival. 
Habitat fragmentation has led to population isolation, restricting gene 
flow within the metapopulation. To address this, the project will 
develop ecological corridors, facilitating individual movement across 
fragmented habitats. These corridors will restore connectivity, 
ultimately ensuring genetic exchange and promoting population 
growth. 
Additionally, the project will explore the introduction of rehabilitated 
same sub-species individuals from centers such as the Sintang 
Orangutan Center (SOC). Establishing a new founder population 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
272 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
through carefully managed reintroductions will further support 
population recovery and stability in the region. 
Initial Value 
Compared to previous study by Yanuar et al. (2019), estimated a 
population density of 1.29 individuals/km² (0.51–3.25), while Saleh 
(1991) predicted a density of 2.62 individuals/km² (1.02–6.72) 
Currently, the population is fragmented, with 133 estimated 
individuals in the lower project blocks and 47 individuals in the upper 
blocks. 
 
Trigger species 
Proboscis monkey (Nasalis larvatus) 
Proboscis monkeys are endemic to Borneo with adistribution spannig 
Sarawak, Sabah and Brunei. In Indonesia they could be found on 
South, Central and West Kalimantan Province, mostly inhabiting 
forest area near to water. As adept swimmers, they thrive in coastal 
areas, mangrove and riverine habitats, but largely avoid non-lowland 
areas that arefar from tidal influence (Sebastian A. C. (2000). 
"Proboscis monkeys in Danau Sentarum National Park". Borneo 
Research Bulletin. 31: 359–371.) 
Today, most of their habitat lies outside conservation areas due to 
habitat degradation and conversion, which has contributed to a 
decline in the monkey's population. 
Population trend at start of 
project 
Proboscis monkeys are classified as Endangered primarly due to 
habitat loss, fragmentation, and degradation, driven from agricultural 
expansion and infrastructure development. In Kapuas Hulu, these 
threats are further intensified by logging activities and the conversion 
of forested areas into plantations. The lack of comprehensive 
population data in this region highlights the urgent need for targeted 
conservation efforts, including habitat protection and species 
monitoring programs.  
The presence of Proboscis Monkey on the lowland project area 
underscroes the critical ecological role of lowland peatlands within 
the region.  
Local populations are estimated extremely small, with direct 
encounters becoming increasingly rare in locations such as Desa 
Nanga Lauk and Desa Nanga Palin over the past five years, primarily 
due to ongoing prevalence of hunting.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Without-project scenario 
The continuation of the baseline scenario will exacerbate existing 
threats, further acclerating the declining population trend as habitat 
disturbance and conversion intensify. 
Species that rely on riverine forests and water sources will be 
significantly affected, as vegetation and forested areas surrounding 
rivers and water bodies are removed, disrupting vital ecological 
dependencies. 
With-project scenario 
The project proponent's intervention, through protective patrol 
activities, aims to reduce pressure and mitigate threats from hunting 
and bush-meat activities. These efforts will halt existing threats, 
ensuring the stability of the current population. Over time, these 
conservation measures are expected to support the growth and 
recovery of a healthy, sustainable population. 
Initial Value 
The local population of the species is currently unknown. 
 
Trigger species 
Tricolour Langur (Presbytis chrysomelas cruciger) 
The Tricolour Langur, also known as the Sarawak Surili, is a critically 
endangered primate endemic to Borneo. This subspecies is primarily 
distributed across northern Borneo, including parts of Sarawak and 
Sabah in Malaysia, as well as West Kalimantan in Indonesia. 
Population trend at start of 
project 
Historically, the Tricolour Langur inhabited a broader range, but today, 
its distribution is restricted to a few fragmented areas, including 
Sarawak and Danau Sentarum National Park, West Kalimantan, 
Indonesia 
These habitats consist mainly of peat swamp forests, lowland 
rainforests, and mangroves, which are the preferred habitats of the 
Tricolour Langur 
The International Union for Conservation of Nature (IUCN) has 
classified the Tricolour Langur as Critically Endangered. Over the past 
30 years, its population has declined by approximately 80%, largely 
due to habitat loss from expanding oil palm plantations. Currently, the 
species occupies less than 5% of its historical range.   
Although, the Tricolour Langur is legally protected under the Wild Life 
Protection Ordinance. it lacks formal protection at the national level, 
partly due to limited ecological data which complicate conservation 
efforts.

Without-project scenario 
Continuing the baseline scenario will further intensify threats to the 
already declining population trend, as habitat disturbance and 
conversion accelerate. 
With-project scenario 
The project proponent's intervention, through protective patrol 
activities, aims to reduce the high threat of hunting, necessitating 
serious protective measures to ensure that the current population 
remain stable, and does not decline or disappear. Over time, these 
efforts may also contribute to population growth and long-term 
recovery 
Initial value 
The local population of the species is currently unknown. 
 
 
Trigger species 
Hairy-Nosed Otter (Lutra sumatrana) 
The Hairy-Nosed Otter (Lutra sumatrana) is one of the rarest and 
least studied otter species in the world, native to Southeast Asia. It is 
classified as Endangered due to habitat loss, poaching, and human 
disturbances. 
Population trend at start of 
project 
The Hairy-nosed Otter (Lutra sumatrana) is native to Southeast Asia. 
Once believe to be extinct in its natural range, Confirmed sightings in 
recent years,have been reported in several countries including 
Indonesia, though populations remains fragmented and scarce. In 
2015, sightings on 2015 from Sumatra confirm its existence, followed 
by camera trap records in the Project Area. 
Hairy Nosed Otters thrive in peat swamp forests, mangroves, and 
lowland flooded forests, particularly in areas with dense vegetation, 
which provide essential cover and abundant prey. 
It is classified as Endangered on the IUCN Red List, with a declining 
population trend. Additionaly, the species is listed in CITES Appendix 
II, meaning that international trade is regulated to prevent 
exploitation. It is legally protected in Indonesia, though conservation 
measures remain limite 
Major threats to survival:  
• Habitat loss due to deforestation, wetlands drinage, and 
agariculture expansion, particularly oil palm plantations. 
• Hunting and poaching for fur, meat, and the illegal pet 
trade. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
 
275 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Accidental capture in fishing gear, leading to population 
decline 
• Reduction in prey availability due to overfishing and habitat 
degradation. 
Without-project scenario 
Hairy Nosed Otters are highly dependent on the existence of thriving 
peatswamp ecosystem that provide abundant food resources. 
However, the continuation of baseline scenario activitie will lead to 
forest cover degradation and peat drainageincreasing succeptibilty   
to fire. Without adequate habitat, the otter will be forced to move into 
adjacent, unmaganged, and unprotected peat swamp, where survival 
conditions are far less favorable. Additionally, peat drainagealters 
aquatic ecosystems, reducing prey availability and changing the 
composition of potential food sources, further exacerbating their 
struggle for survival. In the long term, the continued habita 
destruction under the baseline scenario will inevitablycontribute to 
local extinction of the species unless urgent conservation 
interventions are implemented. 
With-project scenario 
The project will conduct a baseline survey to assess the distribution 
and population size of the species within the project area. This survey 
will provide critical ecological data needed for effective conservation 
planning. Key focus areas include mapping species presence, 
estimating population size, evaluating habitat suitability, identifying 
threats, and developing long-term monitoring frameworks. 
Based on the findings, targeted conservation strategies will be 
implemented, including habitat restoration, legal protections, 
sustainable land-use management, community engagement, and 
possible captive breeding and reintroduction efforts. The project 
follows a structured, data-driven approach, ensuring a scientifically 
sound and ecologically responsible recovery plan. 
Initial Value 
The local population of the species is currently unknown

and agriculture: strengthening

and disease management (IPDM), can 
minimize losses while reducing 
environmental impact.  
• For overuse of inorganic fertilizers: 
promoting organic fertilizer use and 
providing training on sustainable soil 
management practices to preserve soil 
health and biodiversity.  
• For market competition: Developing 
both B2B and B2C marketing 
strategies, supporting value addition,

APPENDIX 3: LIST OF LAWS AND REGULATION RELEVANT 
TO THE PROJECT 
 
Regulation Name Relevance and Compliancy of the Project 
Indonesian Law 
Law Number 1 of 1970 on Worker Safety Rules This law is relevant to SPCP as it ensures occupational safety for workers engaged in 
the  whole  operations.  The  law  further  mandates  risk  assessments,  safety  training, 
and protective measures to prevent accidents and health hazards in field operations. 
To comply with this law, the SPCP will implement workplace safety protocols, provide 
protective  equipment,  conduct  regular  safety  training,  and  ensure  safe  working 
conditions  for  all  personnel,  including  former  illegal  loggers,  hunters,  and  land 
encroachers involved in project activities. 
Law  Number  5  of  1990  on  the  Conservation  of 
Biological     Natural     Resources     and     Their 
Ecosystems 
This law is highly relevant to SPCP as it provides the legal foundation for biodiversity 
conservation,  habitat  protection,  and  sustainable  ecosystem  management—key 
objectives   of   the   carbon   project.   To   ensure   compliance,   SPCP   will   integrate 
biodiversity  safeguards  into  peatland  restoration,  protect  ecosystems  and  wildlife, 
and   adhere   to   legal   conservation   guidelines,   while   actively   engaging   local 
communities in its initiatives. 
Law Number 41 of 1999 on Forestry This law is relevant to SPCP as it provides the legal foundation for forest protection, 
restoration, and sustainable management within the program. To ensure compliance, 
the project will fully adhere to its provisions, reinforcing the project's legal legitimacy 
and ensuring its successful implementation throughout the project period.

Regulation Name Relevance and Compliancy of the Project 
Law Number 13 of 2003 on Manpower This is relevant to SPCP as it regulates employment relations, worker rights, and labor 
protections, ensuring fair treatment, proper wages, and safe working conditions. 
To  comply  with  this  law,  the  SPCP  will  ensure  fair  wages,  social  security  benefits, 
workplace safety, and non-discriminatory employment practices for all workers. The 
project  also  will  follow  labor  regulations  on  contracts,  working  hours,  and  dispute 
resolution,  ensuring  that  employment  within  the  project  aligns  with  national  labor 
laws and international labor standards.  
Law Number 40 of 2004 on the National Social 
Security System (SJSN) 
This  law  is  highly  relevant  to  SPCP  as  it  establishes  the  National  Social  Security 
System  (SJSN),  ensuring  social  protection  for  all  workers.  It  mandates  health  and 
employment benefits, aligning with labour welfare standards. 
To  comply  with  this  law,  the  SPCP  will  commit  to  enrolling  all  employees  in  the 
national  social  security  programs,  covering  health,  work  accidents,  and  pensions. 
This  safeguards  worker  rights,  fulfils  legal  obligations,  and  prevents  potential 
sanctions. 
Law  Number  32  Year  2009  on  Environmental 
Protection and Management 
This law supports SPCP by providing a legal framework for sustainable environmental 
management.  It  aligns  with  carbon  projects  through  its  focus  on  conservation, 
pollution  control,  and  ecosystem  protection,  while  also  regulating  environmental 
impact assessments (AMDAL) and permitting processes. 
To  comply  with  this  law,  the  SPCP  will  ensure  all  activities  follow  environmental 
protection principles, secure necessary permits, and conduct AMDAL when required.  
Law Number 24 of 2011 on the Social Security 
Organizing Agency (BPJS) 
This law mandates social security coverage for workers, making it relevant to SPCP 
which employ staff and field workers. SPCP will commit to registering all employees 
with BPJS for health and employment benefits. This ensures worker protection, meets 
legal requirements, and avoids potential penalties.

This law is relevant to SPCP as it reinforces Indonesia’s commitment to the Paris

verification (MRV) standards to ensure transparency and effectiveness.  
Law Number 18 of 2013 on The Prevention and 
Eradication of Forest Destruction 
This law is relevant to SPCP as it regulates forest destruction, ensuring the protection 
of carbon stocks and ecosystem stability. Its provisions align with SPCP’s efforts in 
sustainable forest management, conservation, and restoration. 
To comply with this law, SPCP will implement strict measures to prevent illegal logging 
and land conversion within project areas. This includes monitoring forest conditions, 
engaging  local  communities  in  conservation  efforts,  and  adhering  to  government 
regulations on land use.

sustainable land-use practices.

function.

land use planning to protect peatland carbon stocks.
